Order of Claims When a Slave Commits Both Jinnaya and Property Damage
Ruling on order of claims when slave commits both jinnaya and property damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's options when a slave has both jinnaya and property debts, and apply the rules regarding knowledge of the master and qadi.

Core Idea
Master retains choice between handing over and ransoming even when multiple claims compete.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a scenario where a slave has committed both a jinnaya (e.g., accidental killing) and property destruction (itlaf mal). The rights of the jinnaya heirs and creditors conflict. The ruling establishes that the master's option (daf' or fida') remains central, but the order of claimants and the knowledge of the master or qadi affect the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two liabilities
The slave owes two debts: one from jinnaya (to the victim's heirs) and one from property destruction (to the creditor). Both are claims on the slave's value.
The passage treats them separately and explains their interaction.
2
Understand the master's initial choice
When both claimants come together, the master can either hand over the slave to the jinnaya heirs (daf') or ransom him (fida') by paying compensation. This is the default rule.
The master is not forced; he has an option.
3
Apply consequences of ransom vs. handover
If the master ransoms, the slave's person is freed from the jinnaya claim and can be sold to pay the debt (unless the master pays the debt). If the master hands over, the jinnaya heirs then pursue the slave for the debt (the slave remains liable).
The handover transfers the slave to the jinnaya heirs, but the debt survives.
4
Analyze when creditors come first (master sells without qadi)
If the master sells the slave for the debt without a qadi's order, his knowledge of the jinnaya matters: if aware, he is deemed to have chosen ransom (must pay jinnaya compensation); if unaware, he pays only the lesser of the slave's value or the debt.
This protects the master if he was ignorant.
5
Analyze when the qadi handles the sale
If the qadi sells the slave for the debt while unaware of the jinnaya, the jinnaya claim remains valid. If the qadi knew, the jinnaya is voided (the sale is treated as the master's choice of ransom).
The qadi's knowledge is imputed to the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو جنى وأتلف مالاً قال : ولو كان العبد استهلك مالاً فوجب عليه وقتل آخر خطأ فحضر أصحاب الديون وأولياء الجناية معاً فإنه يخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء، فإن ظهرت رقبة العبد عن الجناية فبعد ذلك يباع في الدين إلا إذا قضى السيد الدين، وإن اختار الدفع دفعه إلى أولياء الجناية ثم يتبعونه في دينهم. وإن حضر أصحاب الديون أولاً فباع المولى العبد في دينهم بغير أمر القاضي فإنه ينظر؛ إن كان عالماً بالجناية صار مختاراً للفداء، وإن كان غير عالم بالجناية يلزمه الأقل من قيمته ومن الدين. وإن كان الدفع للقاضي فإن كان القاضي غير عالم بالجناية فباع العبد في الدين لم تبطل الجناية، وإن كان القاضي يعلم بالجناية فباعه في الدين بطلت الجناية.
If he [the slave] committed a jinnaya and destroyed property, he said: And if the slave consumed [someone's] property so it became incumbent upon him, and killed another by mistake, and the creditors and the guardians of the jinnaya come together, then the master is given the choice between handing over and ransoming. If the slave's person becomes free [i.e., the master chooses ransom] from the jinnaya, then afterward he is sold for the debt unless the master pays the debt. And if he chooses handing over, he hands him over to the guardians of the jinnaya, then they pursue him for their debt. And if the creditors come first, and the master sells the slave for their debt without the qadi's order, then it is considered: if he was aware of the jinnaya, he becomes a chooser of ransom; if he was unaware of the jinnaya, the lesser of his value and the debt is incumbent upon him. And if the handing over is done by the qadi, then if the qadi was unaware of the jinnaya and sold the slave for the debt, the jinnaya is not voided; if the qadi knew of the jinnaya and sold him for the debt, the jinnaya is voided.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a slave has committed both a jinnaya (e.g., accidental killing) and also destroyed property (itlaf mal), resulting in debts. When both the heirs of the jinnaya (awliya' al-jinnaya) and the creditors come together, the master still has the option between daf' and fida'. If he chooses ransom, the slave's person becomes freed from the jinnaya claim and can then be sold to pay the creditors (unless the master pays the debt). If the master chooses to hand over the slave to the jinnaya heirs, they then pursue the slave for the debt (i.e., the slave remains liable for the debt). If the creditors come first and the master sells the slave for the debt without a qadi's order, his knowledge matters: if he knew about the jinnaya, he is deemed to have chosen ransom (fida'), meaning he must pay the jinnaya compensation; if he did not know, he is liable for the lesser of the slave's value or the debt. If the qadi handles the sale, his knowledge also matters: if the qadi was unaware of the jinnaya and sells the slave for the debt, the jinnaya claim remains valid (the slave can still be claimed for jinnaya); if the qadi knew, the jinnaya claim is voided (because the sale effectively decides the master has chosen ransom).

Key Terms
إتلاف مال (itlāf māl): Destroying another's property, creating a debt obligation on the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave owes a debt from property destruction and also commits a jinnaya, and the master sells the slave for the debt without a qadi's order while aware of the jinnaya, what is the ruling?
A) The jinnaya is voided.
B) The master becomes a chooser of ransom (fida').
C) The master must pay the debt only.
D) The sale is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The master's option and the knowledge of the master or qadi determine the fate of concurrent jinnaya and property claims on a slave.

Acts Constituting Choice of Fida': Freeing, Intercourse, Marriage, Service
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on acts indicating ransom choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle (asl) to determine whether a master's act constitutes a choice of ransom, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding intercourse.

Core Idea
A master's act is a choice of ransom only if it disables handing over the slave.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: when does a master's action toward his slave who has committed a jinnaya (wrongful act) imply that he has chosen to pay ransom (fida') rather than hand over the slave? The key is a foundational principle (asl): only acts that physically prevent the master from surrendering the slave (like freeing) constitute a choice. Acts like intercourse or service, which leave the slave handoverable, do not. The passage then explores disagreements among the Hanafi imams, especially on intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the underlying principle (asl)
Identify the principle: a master becomes a chooser of ransom only if he performs an act that disables him from handing over the slave, and he was unaware of the jinnaya. If he was aware, any act makes him a chooser.
This principle is the key to all rulings in this passage.
2
Analyze the three scenarios of partial choice
The passage first gives three agreed-upon scenarios where the master chooses half the slave or ransom for half. Work through these to see how the principle applies consistently.
These are the foundation for understanding full vs. partial choice.
3
Apply the principle to freeing
Freeing the slave with knowledge of the jinnaya is unanimously a choice because it makes handing over impossible.
This is the clearest case.
4
Examine the dispute over intercourse
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say intercourse is not a choice unless it diminishes value or causes pregnancy. Zufar says it always is. Abu Yusuf has multiple narrations.
This is the central ikhtilaf in the passage.
5
Consider other acts (marriage, service, trade)
Marriage, service, and permitting trade do not constitute a choice according to the three imams, with some exceptions for pregnancy/virginity.
These further illustrate the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة وفي الأصل: إذا جنى جناية وخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء فاختار نصف العبد واختار الفداء في نصفه الآخر فهذه المسألة على وجوه أحدها أن يكون ولي الجناية واحداً بأن قتل العبد رجلاً خطأ وله ولد واحد والقتل خطأ، وفي هذا الوجه إذا اختار المولى الفداء في نصف العبد يصير مختاراً للفداء في الكل لذلك، وإذا اختار نصف العبد يصير مختاراً لدفع الكل وهذا باتفاق الروايات . والثاني أن يكون المقتول اثنين بأن قتل العبد رجلين خطأ ولكل واحد منهما ابن واختار المولى الفداء في أحدهما أو الدفع فإنه يبقى على اختياره في حق الآخر وهذا باتفاق الروايات أيضاً. الثالث إذا كان المقتول واحداً وله وليان فاختار المولى الفداء في حق الآخر ففي عامة الروايات يكون مختاراً للفداء، وفي كتاب الدرر لا يكون مختاراً للفداء، والأصل في هذه المسألة أن المولى متى أحدث في العبد تصرفاً يعجزه عن الدفع وهو غير عالم بالجناية يصير مختاراً، وإذا أحدث تصرفاً لا يعجزه عن الدفع لا يصير مختاراً. وإن كان عالماً بالجناية، فإذا ثبت هذا الأصل فنقول الإعتاق تصرف يعجزه عن الدفع لأن إعتاقه نافذ وبعد العتق لا يمكنه الدفع فإذا أعتق مع العلم بالجناية يكون مختاراً للفداء، ولو كانت أمة فوطئها فهذا ليس باختيار للفداء عند علمائنا الثلاثة. وقال زفر رحمه الله : يكون مختاراً للفداء وكذلك إذا تزوجها لا يكون مختاراً للفداء. وفي الظهيرية إلا إذا أحبلها وفي التهذيب : ولو كانت أمة فتزوجها مختاراً للفداء، وكذلك إذا وطئها لا يكون مختاراً للفداء إلا إذا كانت بكراً أو علقت . لا يصير وذكر في المنتقى عن أبي يوسف في مسألة الوطء ثلاث روايات : قال في رواية الوطء لا يكون مختاراً للفداء وإن كانت الجارية بكراً وهذه رواية هشام وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي مالك إن كان الوطء نقصها فهو اختيار للفداء، وإن لم ينقصها فليس باختيار وبه كان يقول أبو حنيفة. وعن أبي يوسف رواية أخرى أن الوطء اختياراً للفداء على كل حال . وفي الذخيرة وذكر في عتاق الأصل أنه يكون اختياراً للفداء فإن استخدمها لا يكون اختياراً للفداء. وفي السغناقي : حتى لو عطبت في الخدمة لا ضمان عليه، وكذا لو كان عليه دين فاستخدمه المولى لم يضمن الفداء وفي السراجية : المولى إذا أذن العبد الجاني في التجارة ولحقه دين لم يصيره مختاراً للفداء.
And in al-Dhakhira and al-Asl: If he [the slave] commits a jinnaya and the master is given the choice between handing over and ransoming, and he chooses half of the slave and chooses ransom for the other half, then this issue has aspects. First: that the guardian of the jinnaya is one, such as the slave killed a man by mistake and he has one son, and the killing is by mistake. In this aspect, if the master chooses ransom for half the slave, he becomes a chooser of ransom for the whole because of that; and if he chooses half the slave, he becomes a chooser of handing over the whole. This is agreed upon by the narrations. Second: that the killed are two, such as the slave kills two men by mistake, each having a son, and the master chooses ransom for one or handing over; he remains on his choice regarding the other. This is also agreed upon. Third: if the killed is one and he has two guardians, and the master chooses ransom for one, then in most narrations he is a chooser of ransom; in Kitab al-Durar he is not a chooser. And the basis (asl) in this issue is that whenever the master performs an act regarding the slave that disables him from handing over, and he is unaware of the jinnaya, he becomes a chooser [of ransom]; and if he performs an act that does not disable him from handing over, he does not become a chooser. If he is aware of the jinnaya [then any act makes him a chooser]. When this basis is established, we say: freeing is an act that disables handing over because freeing is effective and after freedom he cannot hand over; so if he frees while aware of the jinnaya, he is a chooser of ransom. And if it was a female slave and he had intercourse with her, then this is not a choice for ransom according to our three imams. And Zufar said: it is a choice for ransom. Similarly, if he marries her, it is not a choice for ransom. In al-Zuhayriyya: except if he impregnates her. And in al-Tahdhib: if it was a female slave and he married her, it is a choice for ransom; similarly, if he had intercourse with her, it is not a choice for ransom unless she was a virgin or became pregnant. It is not [؟] and it is mentioned in al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf concerning the issue of intercourse three narrations: he said in one narration: intercourse is not a choice for ransom even if the girl was a virgin – this is the narration of Hisham. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Malik: if the intercourse diminished her [value], it is a choice for ransom; if it did not diminish her, it is not a choice – and this is what Abu Hanifa used to say. And from Abu Yusuf another narration: intercourse is a choice for ransom in all cases. And in al-Dhakhira and mentioned in 'Itaq al-Asl: that freeing is a choice for ransom; if he uses her for service, it is not a choice for ransom. And in al-Sighnaqi: even if she perishes in service, no liability upon him. Similarly, if there was a debt upon him and the master employed him, he does not guarantee the ransom. And in al-Sirajiyya: if the master permits the offending slave to engage in trade and a debt befalls him, it does not make him a chooser for ransom.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling exposition. The overall principle (asl) is established: when a master performs an act that makes it impossible for him to hand over the slave (e.g., freeing), and he was unaware of the jinnaya, he becomes a chooser of ransom (meaning he must pay the fida'). If he performs an act that does not disable handing over (e.g., mere service), he does not become liable unless he was aware. If he was aware, any act makes him a chooser. The passage then applies this principle to specific acts: (1) Freeing: obviously disabling, so if done with knowledge, it's a choice of ransom. (2) Intercourse with a female slave: three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say it is not a choice; Zufar says it is. There are multiple narrations from Abu Yusuf: one says it's not a choice even if virgin; another says it is if it diminishes value; another says always. (3) Marriage: not a choice according to the three; but exceptions if pregnancy or virginity exist. (4) Service: not a choice. (5) Authorizing trade: not a choice. The passage also discusses the three scenarios of partial choice (half the slave, two victims, two heirs).

Key Terms
اختيار للفداء (ikhtiyār li al-fidā'): The master's act that indicates he has chosen to ransom the slave, making him liable for the compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following acts is explicitly stated to NOT be a choice of ransom?
A) Freeing the slave with knowledge
B) Using the slave for service
C) Intercourse causing pregnancy
D) Marrying when the slave is free

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: only acts that physically disable handing over the slave constitute a choice of ransom; mere use or intent does not.

Three Narrations on Intercourse as Election for Fida
Clarifying the Hanafi positions on whether intercourse constitutes election to pay ransom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three narrations from Abu Yusuf regarding intercourse as election for fida and identify the position of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a slave-girl after her crime has three possible effects on the master's liability for fida.

When a slave commits a crime, the master may either surrender the slave to the victim or pay ransom (fida). A key question arises: does the master's subsequent intercourse with the slave-girl constitute an irrevocable choice to pay fida? This passage from Al-Muntaqa records three differing narrations from Abu Yusuf and one from Abu Hanifa, illustrating the nuanced internal disputes within the Hanafi school. Understanding these positions is crucial for determining the master's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether a master's intercourse with his slave-girl after she commits a crime constitutes election (ikhtiyar) to pay fida, thereby preventing him from surrendering her.
Fida is a sum paid to the victim in lieu of surrendering the slave.
2
List the three narrations from Abu Yusuf
The first narration (Hisham) states intercourse is never election. The second (al-Hasan from Abu Malik, also Abu Hanifa's view) states it is election only if it diminishes her value. The third (another from Abu Yusuf) states it is always election.
These are reported by different transmitters, indicating the complexity of the school's transmission.
3
Note Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa's view, transmitted via al-Hasan from Abu Malik, is that intercourse is election only if it causes a defect (i.e., reduces her value). Otherwise, it is not election.
This is the most nuanced position.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if intercourse is election, the master must pay fida; if not, he can choose to surrender the slave. The defect condition determines which rule applies.
The ruling affects the master's financial liability and the victim's compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في المنتقى عن أبي يوسف في مسألة الوطء ثلاث روايات : قال في رواية الوطء لا يكون مختاراً للفداء وإن كانت الجارية بكراً وهذه رواية هشام وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي مالك إن كان الوطء نقصها فهو اختيار للفداء، وإن لم ينقصها فليس باختيار وبه كان يقول أبو حنيفة. وعن أبي يوسف رواية أخرى أن الوطء اختياراً للفداء على كل حال.
And it is mentioned in al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf regarding the issue of intercourse three narrations: He said in one narration that intercourse does not constitute election for fida even if the slave-girl was a virgin, and this is the narration of Hisham. And in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Malik, if intercourse caused her defect, it is election for fida; if it did not defect her, it is not election, and this was the view of Abu Hanifa. And from Abu Yusuf there is another narration that intercourse is election for fida in all cases.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents three differing narrations from Abu Yusuf and one from Abu Hanifa on whether a master's act of intercourse with his slave-girl after she commits a crime constitutes his election to pay the fida (ransom) instead of surrendering her. The first narration (from Hisham) says it is never election. The second (from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad via Abu Malik) distinguishes based on whether the intercourse diminished her value; if it did, it is election; if not, it is not. Abu Hanifa held this view. The third narration from Abu Yusuf says it is always election, regardless. This ikhtilaf is important because it determines whether the master becomes liable to pay the fida or can still surrender the slave.

Key Terms
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse with a female slave.
اختيار للفداء (ikhtiyār li al-fidā'): The master's election to pay ransom (fida) instead of surrendering the slave to the victim's guardian.
رواية (riwāyah): A transmission of a legal ruling from an early authority, often through a chain of narrators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi imam's view is that intercourse is election for fida only if it causes a defect in the slave-girl?
A) Abu Yusuf (first narration)
B) Abu Hanifa
C) Hisham
D) Abu Malik

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has multiple opinions on whether intercourse after a slave's crime constitutes election to pay fida; the view of Abu Hanifa, which conditions election on a defect, is often preferred.

When Master's Actions Do Not Constitute Election for Fida
Explains actions that do not constitute election for fida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions by the master after a slave's crime do not constitute election for fida and explain the legal principle behind it.

Core Idea
Use in service, trade authorization, and debt do not make the master liable for fida.

In Hanafi diyat law, when a slave commits a crime, the master has a choice: surrender the slave or pay the ransom (fida). However, not every interaction with the slave implies the master has made that election. This lesson clarifies that routine actions such as using the slave for service, authorizing trade, or incurring debts on his account do not automatically bind the master to pay the fida. Understanding this distinction protects the master from implied liability and preserves his right to choose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Determine what counts as an election to pay fida. The master must have clearly indicated his choice, not merely interacted with the slave routinely.
2
Review the three scenarios
The passage lists: (1) using the slave in service, (2) authorizing trade, (3) incurring debts. In all three, the master does not become mukhtar.
Even if the slave perishes during service, no liability arises.
3
Note the key sources
The passage draws from al-Dhakhira, al-Sighnaqi, and al-Sirajiyya, showing broad agreement in the Hanafi school.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
Only explicit acts of retention or sale indicate election. Routine management does not imply acceptance of liability.
This principle prevents masters from being unfairly burdened.
5
Apply to a new scenario
If the master rents out the criminal slave, does that constitute election? Apply the same logic: renting is a routine commercial act, so no election.
Contrast with selling the slave, which does indicate election.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة وذكر في عتاق الأصل أنه يكون اختياراً للفداء فإن استخدمها لا يكون اختياراً للفداء. وفي السغناقي : حتى لو عطبت في الخدمة لا ضمان عليه، وكذا لو كان عليه دين فاستخدمه المولى لم يضمن الفداء وفي السراجية : المولى إذا أذن العبد الجاني في التجارة ولحقه دين لم يصيره مختاراً للفداء.
And in al-Dhakhira: It is mentioned in 'Utaq al-Asl that it becomes election for fida; if he uses her in service, it is not election. In al-Sighnaqi: Even if she perishes in service, there is no liability. Similarly, if there was a debt on him and the master used him, he does not guarantee the fida. And in al-Sirajiyya: If the master authorizes the criminal slave to trade and a debt accrues to him, this does not make him mukhtar for fida.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage discusses scenarios where the master's actions after the crime may or may not indicate his election to pay fida. The principle is that mere use of the slave in service, authorizing trade, or incurring debts on his account does not automatically make the master liable for the fida. The master retains the option to either surrender the slave or pay the ransom. This is important because it protects the master from implied liability due to routine actions.

Key Terms
استخدام (istikhdām): Using the slave for service or labor.
إذن في التجارة (idhn fī al-tijārah): Master's permission for the slave to engage in trade.
اختيار للفداء (ikhtiyār li al-fidā'): Master's election to pay ransom instead of surrendering the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The master authorizes his criminal slave to trade, and the slave incurs a debt. What is the ruling?
A) The master becomes mukhtar for fida.
B) The master is not made mukhtar by this.
C) The slave must be sold to pay the debt.
D) The fida is cancelled.

Key Takeaway
A master does not become liable for fida merely by using a criminal slave in service, authorizing trade, or incurring debts on his account.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Liability When a Slave Who Kills Is Killed: The Master's Non-Election (Mukhtar)
A complex case of a slave who kills a freeman and then is mistakenly killed himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's liability when his slave kills a free man and is then killed, and explain the concept of mukhtar (election) in this context.

Core Idea
Accepting the slave's value does not make the master mukhtar; he must still pay diya for the free man.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a layered case of two mistaken killings involving a slave. It clarifies a common point of confusion: the master's receipt of compensation for his slave does not automatically imply that he has elected to pay fida (mukhtar). Understanding this distinction is key to applying Hanafi diyat law correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two killings
Recognize that the slave killed a free man by mistake (first killing), and then another man killed the slave by mistake (second killing). Both are classified as qatl khaṭa'.
Both killings are accidental, so qisas does not apply; only diya is due.
2
Determine the master's receipt of value
The master took the value (qīmah) of his slave from the second killer. This value is compensation for the slave's death.
The value is paid by the second killer's ʿāqila.
3
Clarify the master's status (mukhtar)
The text explicitly states that the master took the value 'without being mukhtar'. Mukhtar means he has elected to pay fida (ransom) for the free man's death caused by his slave. Accepting the slave's value does not constitute election.
Election would trigger an immediate obligation to pay diya for the free man; non-election leaves the obligation intact.
4
Conclude the master's liability
Since the master is not mukhtar, he must guarantee a similar amount (i.e., the diya of a free man) to the heirs of the free man. He cannot offset this liability with the value he received.
The master ends up paying diya to the free man's family, ensuring the victim's blood is not wasted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً : عبد قتل حراً خطأ ثم قتله رجل آخر خطأ فأخذ المولى قيمته قاتله من لم يكن مختاراً ويضمن مثلها لمولى الحر.
And in it also: A slave killed a free man by mistake, then another man killed him by mistake, so the master took his value from his killer without being mukhtar, and he guarantees the like of it to the master of the free man.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a situation where a slave commits manslaughter, then is himself killed by another person. The master receives the slave's value from the second killer. However, the master is not considered to have elected to pay fida (i.e., he is not mukhtar) simply by accepting the blood money for his slave. He must also pay compensation to the heirs of the free man killed by his slave. The ruling clarifies that receiving compensation for the slave does not imply election; the master still has the option to surrender the slave's corpse? Actually, the text indicates the master's non-election and liability to pay a similar amount to the free man's master (i.e., the heirs).

Key Terms
قتل خطأ (qatl khaṭa'): Mistaken killing, where there is no intent to kill.
قيمة (qīmah): The monetary value of the slave, paid as blood money by the killer.
مختار (mukhtār): One who elects to pay fida (the master).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a slave kills a free man by mistake and is then killed by another, which statement is correct regarding the master's liability?
A) The master becomes mukhtar by accepting the slave's value and is free from further liability.
B) The master is not mukhtar and must pay diya to the free man's heirs.
C) The master is exempt if the slave's killer pays the diya.
D) The master must choose between surrendering the slave's corpse or paying nothing.

Key Takeaway
Accepting the value for a killed slave does not make the master mukhtar; he remains liable to pay diya for the free man his slave killed.

Manumission of the Criminal Slave with Guardian's Permission: Master's Liability
Ruling on master's liability after freeing criminal slave with permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the master's liability when he frees a criminal slave with the victim's guardian's permission, and distinguish it from the case of sale ratification.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave with guardian's permission makes the master liable for diya.

When a slave commits a crime, the master has options. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq (explaining Kanz al-Daqa'iq) clarifies a specific scenario: if the master frees the slave with the explicit permission of the victim's guardian, he becomes 'mukhtar' (choosing to pay fida) and must pay the diya. However, merely ratifying a sale of the criminal slave does not have this effect, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining liability in diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The key act is manumission ('itq) of a slave who has committed a crime. The critical condition is that this manumission must be done with the permission (idhn) of the victim's guardian (wali al-jinayah).
Without permission, the ruling may differ.
2
Determine the legal effect
According to Ibn Sama'a (in al-Nawadir), this act makes the master 'mukhtar' for fida (i.e., he has elected to keep the slave and compensate the victim). Consequently, the master owes the diya (blood money).
The master cannot later claim that the slave is responsible.
3
Compare with sale ratification
The passage contrasts this with ratifying the sale of the slave after the crime. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (in al-Imla'), ratification of sale does NOT constitute an election for fida. The buyer is given a choice: either surrender the slave or pay the ransom.
This distinction shows that different actions with the same slave lead to different legal consequences.
4
Understand the logic
Manumission with permission is seen as a positive act of choice (ikhtiyar) for keeping the slave and thereby assuming liability. Sale ratification, however, is merely confirming an existing transaction and does not imply a similar choice.
The principle is that the master's expressed choice (by freeing) with consent triggers liability.
5
Apply the ruling
If a master frees a criminal slave with the guardian's permission, he must pay diya. The guardian cannot demand the slave (since he is free), but the master is liable. If the master refuses, what then? The passage does not specify, but logically, the guardian could sue for diya.
In practice, the master would negotiate payment or face legal action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوادر ابن سماعة : إذا أعتقه المولى بإذن ولي الجناية فهو اختيار للفداء وعليه الدية . وفي الإملاء عن محمد رحمه الله : إن إجازة بيع العبد بعد جنايته في يده ليس باختيار للفداء في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ويقال للمشتري ادفع أو رد وفي التجريد: وأطلق في العتق فشمل ما إذا أعتق أو أمر به قال ولو أمر المولى المجني عليه بإعتاقه فأعتقه صار المولى مختاراً.
And in al-Nawadir from Ibn Sama'a: If the master frees the slave with the permission of the guardian of the victim, it is an election for fida, and he owes the diya. And in al-Imla from Muhammad: The ratification of the sale of the slave after his crime while in his hand is not an election for fida according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The purchaser is told: surrender or pay ransom. And in al-Tajrid: The expression 'manumission' is absolute and includes when he frees or orders; he said: if the master orders the victim's guardian to free him and he frees him, the master becomes mukhtar.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of manumitting the criminal slave. According to Ibn Sama'a in al-Nawadir, if the master frees the slave with the consent of the victim's guardian, this constitutes an election to pay the fida, meaning the master is liable for the diya. In contrast, ratifying a sale of the slave after the crime is not election according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; the buyer is given the option to surrender or pay. The commentary in al-Tajrid clarifies that if the master orders the guardian to free the slave and he does so, the master becomes mukhtar. This distinction is crucial, as it determines whether the master assumes liability for the crime.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Manumission; freeing a slave.
إذن ولي الجناية (idhn walī al-jināyah): Permission from the guardian of the victim (the one entitled to claim compensation).
دية (diyah): Blood money; compensation paid for homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Not liable for anything
B) Mukhtar for fida and owes diya
C) Free from all obligations
D) Must surrender the slave

Key Takeaway
Manumission of a criminal slave with the guardian's permission constitutes an election to pay fida, making the master liable for diya; this differs from mere sale ratification.

Slave in Mortgage: Effect of Acknowledgment on Fida
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for fida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a master's acknowledgment after a slave's crime makes him liable for fida, and distinguish the views of Zufar and the majority.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment alone does not make the master liable; only an active choice like emancipation or payment does.

When a slave in mortgage commits a crime, the master may either surrender the slave or pay ransom (fida). But what if the master merely acknowledges that the slave belongs to another? Does that act as a choice for fida? The Hanafi majority, including Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, says no—mere words do not create liability. Zufar, however, holds that such acknowledgment constitutes a binding choice. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
First, understand the scenario: a slave in mortgage commits a crime. The mortgagee cannot surrender the slave; only the mortgagor (master) can. If the master frees the slave, he becomes liable for fida. This is the baseline ruling.
2
Locate the dispute
The passage from al-Kafi adds a twist: what if the master, after the crime, acknowledges that the slave belongs to another? Zufar considers this a choice (ikhtiyar) for fida; the Hanafi majority ('indana) does not.
Zufar is a known Hanafi imam, but his view is not mufta bihi here.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the master acknowledges, Zufar would hold him liable for fida; the Hanafi majority would not. This affects whether the master must pay or can still surrender the slave.
4
Apply the tarjih
The text sides with 'indana (our view), indicating the majority position is mufta bihi. Thus, in fatwa, acknowledgment alone does not make the master mukhtar.
This is based on the principle that liability requires action, not mere speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وليس له أن يدفع فإن قال لا أفدي كان للراهن أن يدفع بالجناية، فإن أعتقه كان مختاراً للفداء. وفي الكافي : ولو أقر مولى الجناية بعد العلم بالجناية أن العبد لهذا فهو اختيار للفداء عند زفر، وعندنا لا يكون مختاراً.
If he does not have the right to surrender, if he says I will not ransom, then the mortgagor may surrender the slave for the crime. If he frees him, he becomes one who chooses the ransom. And in al-Kafi: If the master of the crime, after knowing of the crime, acknowledges that the slave belongs to this, then according to Zufar this is a choice for ransom, but according to us he does not become one who chooses.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It first establishes the basic ruling: when a slave held as collateral (marhun) commits a crime, the mortgagee (murtahin) does not have the right to surrender the slave; only the owner (rahin) may surrender or pay ransom (fida). If the owner instead emancipates the slave, he becomes mukhtar for fida (liable to pay ransom). Then it cites a dispute from al-Kafi: if the master (the person holding the slave) acknowledges after the crime that the slave belongs to someone else, Zufar considers this a choice (ikhtiyar) for fida, making the master liable. However, the Hanafi school ('indana) holds that mere acknowledgment does not constitute a choice, and the master is not liable. This ikhtilaf hinges on whether words alone can create liability, or only actual payment or emancipation.

Key Terms
فداء (fida'): Ransom paid by the master to free the slave from liability for the crime.
مختار (mukhtar): One who chooses the option of fida, thus becoming liable to pay it.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession, here the master's statement that the slave belongs to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, if a master acknowledges after his slave's crime that the slave belongs to another, what is the legal effect?
A) He becomes liable for fida.
B) He does not become liable for fida.
C) He must surrender the slave immediately.
D) The slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mere acknowledgment of another's ownership after a slave's crime does not make the master liable for fida; only an active choice like emancipation or payment does.

Liability of Possessor Claiming Slave as Deposit or Loan
Ruling on a slave in hand claimed as deposit or loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for a possessor claiming a slave belongs to another and distinguish Zufar's alternative view, identifying the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Mere claim does not shift liability; evidence is required unless you are Zufar.

What happens when a slave in your possession commits a crime, and the victim says 'he is your slave,' but you claim it's a deposit for someone else? The Hanafi school says you must prove it; otherwise, you are treated as the owner and must surrender or ransom. But a prominent Hanafi dissenter, Zufar, holds that your mere statement is enough to avoid liability. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
State the facts: a slave in hand commits a crime; the victim's guardian says the possessor is the owner; the possessor claims it is a deposit, loan, lease, or mortgage for an absent third party. The dispute is whether the possessor's mere claim exempts him from being treated as owner.
The passage from al-Sighnaqi is authoritative.
2
State the Hanafi position (main view)
The main Hanafi position requires the possessor to bring evidence (bayyinah) of his claim. If he does, the matter is permitted (i.e., he is not liable). If he does not, he is ordered to either surrender the slave or pay ransom (fida).
This view is attributed to Abu Hanifa and the majority of the Hanafi school.
3
State Zufar's dissenting view
Zufar says: by the possessor's mere statement that the slave belongs to so-and-so, he becomes liable for diya (full blood money) immediately, without need for evidence. Then, if he ransoms and the absentee appears, the owner takes the slave without paying anything; if he surrenders, the absentee has the option to confirm the surrender or take the slave and pay the compensation (arsh).
Zufar's view shifts liability solely based on the possessor's verbal claim.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference is evident in the possessor's liability before the absentee appears. Under the main Hanafi view, the possessor must prove his claim or face surrender/ransom; under Zufar's view, his mere statement makes him liable for diya, and the absentee's rights are affected (if ransom, absentee takes slave free; if surrender, absentee can reclaim slave and pay arsh).
This ikhtilaf has implications for the burden of proof in tort liability.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi, but the structure (presenting the main ruling first as the general rule) suggests the requirement of evidence is the relied-upon position. Later Hanafi fatwa collections consistently require proof for such claims.
Zufar's view is considered a minority opinion within the school, but it is preserved as a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السغناقي: ولو أن عبداً في يد رجل جنى جناية فقال ولي الجناية هو عبدك وقال الرجل هو وديعة عندي لفلان أو عارية أو إجارة أو رهن، أقام على ذلك بينة أجزت الأمر فيه وإن لم يقم خوطب بالدفع أو الفداء. وقال زفر : مختار الدية بمجرد قوله إنه لفلان فإن فداه ثم قدم الغائب أخذه عبده بغير شيء، وإن كان دفعه فالغائب بالخيار إن شاء أمضى ذلك، وإن شاء أخذ العبد ودفع الأرش.
And in al-Sighnaqi: If a slave in the hand of a man commits a crime, and the guardian of the crime says 'he is your slave', and the man says 'he is a deposit with me for so-and-so, or a loan, or a lease, or a mortgage', and he brings evidence of that, I permit the matter; if he does not bring evidence, he is addressed to surrender or ransom. Zufar said: He is liable for diya by his mere statement that it belongs to so-and-so; if he then ransoms and the absent one comes, he takes his slave without anything; if he surrendered, the absent one has the choice: if he wishes he confirms that, and if he wishes he takes the slave and pays the compensation.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Sighnaqi (likely a Hanafi jurist) followed by a dissenting view of Zufar. The scenario: a slave in someone's possession commits a crime. The victim's guardian claims the possessor is the owner. The possessor claims it is a deposit (wadi'ah), loan ('ariyah), lease (ijarah), or mortgage (rahn) for an absent third party. If the possessor proves his claim with evidence, the ruling is that he is not required to surrender or ransom (since he is not the owner). If he lacks evidence, he is ordered to either surrender the slave or pay ransom (fida). Zufar opposes: he holds that the possessor becomes liable for diya (full blood money) by his mere statement that it belongs to another, even without evidence. Subsequently, if the possessor ransoms and the true owner appears, the owner takes the slave without compensation (fida was paid). If the possessor surrendered the slave, the absent owner has an option: either ratify the surrender or take the slave and pay the compensation (arsh) to the victim. This ikhtilaf is on whether a mere claim can shift liability.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): Deposit; goods left with someone for safekeeping without compensation.
عارية ('ariyah): Loan (for use), gratuitous loan of an object.
إجارة (ijarah): Lease; hire of services or usufruct for payment.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury; damages paid to the victim for wounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man has a slave in his possession and the slave commits a crime, what does Hanafi law require of the possessor if he claims the slave is a deposit for another?
A) He must bring evidence; otherwise he is treated as owner.
B) His mere statement exempts him from liability.
C) He must surrender the slave immediately.
D) He is always liable for diya.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a person claiming an item in his possession belongs to another must prove that claim; mere assertion is insufficient, unless following Zufar's minority view.

Slave Kills Two Victims with Confession
Ruling when master confesses slave killed another victim after evidence for first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi opinions on the liability of a master when his slave kills two victims (one proven by evidence, the other by confession) and identify the practical consequence of each opinion.

Core Idea
Evidence and confession differ in weight: the victim with evidence takes priority over the confessional victim in slave surrender.

This passage from al-Muntaqa presents a scenario where a slave kills two people, but only one killing is established by evidence; the second is confessed by the master. The Hanafi jurists differ on how the master should surrender or ransom the slave, reflecting deeper principles about the relative strength of proof types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
The scenario involves a slave who killed two victims. One killing is proven by evidence (bayyinah) and the other is confessed by the master. The question is how the master must satisfy the heirs.
Evidence is stronger than confession in establishing liability.
2
State the first ruling from al-Muntaqa
The first ruling orders the master to surrender the slave to both heirs in half shares, then pay half the slave's value to the heir with evidence as compensation.
This ensures both heirs receive something while compensating the evidence-holder.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's view via al-Hasan ibn Ziyad
Abu Yusuf holds that the master should surrender only to the first victim (the one with evidence) or ransom him. If surrendered, the second victim gets nothing. If ransomed from the first, the master must then surrender half to the second or ransom with half diya.
This view prioritizes the evidence-holder entirely over the confessional victim.
4
Examine Zufar's view via Ibn Malik and al-Abbas
Zufar's view gives the master options: if he ransoms from one victim, he surrenders entirely to the other, except for half. This allows the master to choose which victim to compensate with the slave's value.
Zufar focuses on the master's flexibility in satisfying both claimants.
5
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the first ruling, both heirs share the slave (half each) and the evidence-holder gets additional compensation. Under Abu Yusuf, the evidence-holder may take the whole slave, leaving the confessional victim with nothing unless ransom is paid. Under Zufar, the master may choose which victim to surrender to.
The ikhtilaf affects how much each heir actually receives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : عبد قتل قتيلاً وقامت عليه البينة بذلك ثم أقر المولى أنه قتل قتيلاً آخر فإنه يؤمر بدفعه إليهما نصفين ثم يضمن نصف قيمته لصاحب البينة الحسن بن زياد عن أبي يوسف : رجل أقر أن عبده قتل رجلاً خطاً ثم أقر عليه أيضاً برجل آخر أنه قتله خطأ يقال للمولى ادفع عبدك للأول خاصة أو افده، فإن دفعه فلا شيء للآخر، وإن فداه من الأول قيل له ادفع إلى الآخر نصيبه أو افده بنصف الدية وروى ابن مالك أنه يقال للمولى ادفعه إليهما نصفين، فإن دفعه غرم الأول نصف قيمته وإن قال أنا أفديه من الآخر دفعه كله إلى الأول، فإن قال أفديه من الأول فدع نصفه إلى الآخر وهو قول زفر . وذكر العباس بن الوليد عنه أنه إذا دفع نصفه إلى الثاني فهو مختار الدية من الأول.
And in al-Muntaqa: A slave killed a person and evidence was established against him, then the master confessed that he killed another person. Then he is ordered to surrender him to both half-and-half, then he guarantees half his value to the one with evidence. al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Yusuf: A man confessed that his slave killed a man by mistake, then also confessed against him regarding another man that he killed him by mistake. It is said to the master: Surrender your slave to the first only, or ransom him. If he surrenders him, then nothing for the other. If he ransoms him from the first, it is said to him: Surrender to the other his share, or ransom him with half the diya. And Ibn Malik narrated that it is said to the master: Surrender him to both half-and-half. If he surrenders him, the first is reimbursed half the value. If he says: I will ransom him from the other, he surrenders him entirely to the first. If he says: I will ransom him from the first, then he leaves half to the other. That is Zufar's opinion. And al-'Abbas ibn al-Walid mentioned from him that if he surrenders half to the second, he becomes one who chooses diya from the first.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage from al-Muntaqa deals with a slave who kills two victims, one proven by evidence and the other by the master's confession. The initial ruling says the master is ordered to surrender the slave to both victims' heirs in half shares, and then he must pay half the slave's value to the first victim's heir (who had evidence) as compensation for the lost opportunity. Then it quotes an opinion of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Yusuf with a different approach: the master is told to surrender the slave to the first victim only or ransom him. If he surrenders, the second gets nothing. If he ransoms from the first, then he must deal with the second by surrendering half or ransoming with half diya. Another narration from Ibn Malik says the master must surrender the slave to both equally, and if he does so, the first gets reimbursed half the value. Then it gives Zufar's view: if the master ransoms from one, he surrenders entirely to the other except half. This ikhtilaf revolves around the order of victims and the effect of confession vs. evidence on liability.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony establishing the crime.
أقر (aqarra): Confessed; the master's admission that his slave killed someone.
غرم (gharima): He paid/compensated; the master pays half the value to the first victim's heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first ruling in al-Muntaqa, if a slave kills two victims (one proven by evidence, the other by the master's confession), what is the initial order?
A) Surrender the slave to the first victim only.
B) Surrender the slave to both victims half-and-half.
C) The master must ransom both.
D) The slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when evidence and confession conflict regarding slave killings, the evidence-holder often has a priority claim, requiring the master to share the slave and compensate the evidence-holder.

Liability for a Slave's Crime When Ownership is Unknown
Ruling on a slave in unknown ownership who commits a crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a slave in unknown ownership commits a crime, distinguishing between proof by evidence and proof by confession.

Core Idea
The true owner's liability depends on how the crime was proven: evidence binds, confession does not.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario: a slave is held by someone who neither claims ownership nor is acknowledged by the slave. The slave commits a crime. How is liability determined? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the crime was proven by independent evidence (bayyinah) or by the possessor's own confession (iqrar). Understanding this distinction clarifies the principles of vicarious liability and the effect of confessions by non-owners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
Note the possessor (one holding the slave), the slave, and the later-claimed true owner. The possessor does not claim ownership, the slave does not acknowledge the possessor, but the slave admits being a slave. Then the slave commits a crime.
2
Distinguish the two modes of proof
The crime is proven either by independent evidence (bayyinah) such as witnesses, or by the possessor's own confession (iqrar) that the slave committed the crime.
The mode of proof is critical to the ruling.
3
Apply the ruling for each mode
If proven by evidence, the true owner (who later acknowledges ownership and denies the crime) must either ransom the slave or surrender it for retaliation. If proven by the possessor's confession, the true owner takes the slave and the crime claim is invalidated; the confessor incurs no liability.
In the confession case, the crime is 'invalidated' (batalat).
4
Understand the rationale
The possessor's confession cannot bind the true owner because the confessor is not the owner. Independent evidence, however, proves the crime regardless of ownership. Thus liability follows the evidence, not the confession of a non-owner.
This aligns with the principle that a confession by a non-owner does not affect the true owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل في يديه عبد لا يدري أنه له أن لغيره لم يدع صاحب اليد أنه له ولم يسمع من العبد إقراره أنه عبد صاحب اليد إلا أنه يقر بأنه عبد فجنى هذا العبد جناية وثبت ذلك بالبينة أو بإقرار صاحب اليد ثم إن صاحب اليد أقر أنه عبد رجل وصدقه المقر له بذلك وكذبه في الجناية، فإن كانت الجناية ببينة قيل للمقر له ادفع أو افده، وإن كانت الجناية بإقرار الذي كان العبد في يده أخذ المقر له العبد وبطلت الجناية ولم يكن على المقر من الجناية شيء.
A man has a slave in his hands, he does not know whether it is his or belongs to another. The possessor does not claim it as his, nor is it heard from the slave an acknowledgment that he is the possessor's slave, but he acknowledges that he is a slave. Then this slave commits a crime, and that is established by evidence or by the possessor's confession. Then the possessor acknowledges that he is a slave of a man, and the acknowledged one affirms that and denies the crime. If the crime is established by evidence, it is said to the acknowledged one: Surrender or ransom. If the crime is established by the confession of the one who had the slave in his hand, the acknowledged one takes the slave and the crime is invalidated, and nothing is upon the confessor regarding the crime.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage deals with a unique scenario: a slave is in someone's hands, but the possessor does not claim ownership, nor does the slave acknowledge him as master. The slave merely admits he is a slave (without specifying master). Then the slave commits a crime, proven either by evidence or by the possessor's own confession. After that, the possessor acknowledges that the slave belongs to a specific man, and that man confirms the acknowledgment but denies the crime. The ruling distinguishes based on how the crime was proven: if by evidence (bayyinah), the acknowledged owner must either surrender the slave or ransom it. If by the possessor's confession (iqrar), then once the possessor acknowledges the true owner, that owner takes the slave, and the crime is nullified (since the confession was by a non-owner). The confessor (possessor) incurs no liability. This shows the principle that a confession by a non-owner regarding a crime does not bind the true owner.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence proving the crime, typically witnesses.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession; here the possessor's admission that the slave committed the crime.
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made; the claimed owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave's crime is proven only by the possessor's confession, and the possessor later acknowledges a true owner who denies the crime, what happens?
A) The true owner must ransom or surrender.
B) The true owner takes the slave and the crime is invalidated.
C) The possessor remains liable.
D) The slave is executed.

Key Takeaway
The mode of proof determines the true owner's liability: evidence binds, confession does not.

Slave-Caused Hand Injuries: Three Liability Rulings
Three rulings on slave causing hand injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for three scenarios of slave-caused hand injuries and identify the legal consequences.

Core Idea
A slave's hand injury triggers liability that can revert or become void depending on settlement and recovery.

This lesson presents three distinct Hanafi rulings regarding a slave who causes hand injuries. The cases cover a healed wound that becomes fatal, a finger cut where the victim's family ransoms the slave without court order, and an intentional cut settled with the slave. Each scenario carries specific legal effects on liability and the slave's status.

Guided Path
1
First scenario: healed wound reopens
The slave cuts a hand by mistake, the wound heals, the master surrenders the slave. Then the wound reopens and kills the victim. Ruling: The master must pay the value of the slave.
Liability reverts because the injury directly caused death.
2
Second scenario: finger cut and ransom without qadi
The slave cuts a finger. The victim's family pays a ransom to free the slave without a judge's order. Ruling: The ransom is void, and the master owes full diya, as if he emancipated while knowing the crime.
Only the master can ransom; family's action without qadi is invalid.
3
Third scenario: intentional hand cut settled with slave
A man cuts another's hand intentionally. They settle with the slave as compensation. The victim then emancipates the slave and later dies. Ruling: The settlement is valid; if not freed, the slave returns to the original master, and the heirs choose between execution or pardon.
For intentional injury, heirs have option of qisas or 'afw.
4
Synthesize the common principle
Liability is determined by the validity of transactions affecting the slave. Without court approval, certain actions are void. The reopening of a wound reverts liability to the master.
These rulings emphasize the importance of judicial oversight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً : عبد قطع يد رجل خطأ فبرأت فدفعه مولاه بجنايته ثم انتقض الجرح فمات منه قال يدفع قيمة عبده . وفي العيون الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة في عبد قطع أصبع رجل خطأ ففداه المولى المقطوع أصبعه كان ذلك الفداء باطلاً وكان عليه تمام الدية إن كان الفداء بغير قضاء القاضي، وصار بمنزلة من أعتق وهو يعلم. وفي الكافي: رجل قطع يد رجل عمداً فصالح المقطوعة يده على عبد ودفع إليه فأعتقه المقطوع يده ثم مات من ذلك فالعبد صلح بالجناية، وإن لم يعتقه رد على مولاه وقيل للأولياء إما أن تقتلوه وأما أن تعفوا.
And in it also: A slave cut the hand of a man by mistake, then it healed, so his master surrendered him for his crime. Then the wound reopened and he died from it. He said: He pays the value of his slave. And in al-'Uyun: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa regarding a slave who cut a man's finger by mistake, and the master of the one whose finger was cut ransomed him. That ransom is void, and he is liable for the full diya if the ransom was without the judge's ruling, and he becomes like one who emancipates knowingly. And in al-Kafi: A man cut another's hand intentionally, so the one whose hand was cut settled for a slave and he was given to him. Then the one whose hand was cut emancipated him, then he died from that. So the slave is (considered) settled by the crime. If he did not emancipate him, he is returned to his master, and it is said to the heirs: Either you kill him or pardon.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This lesson combines three independent rulings from different sources. First (from the same source as previous), a slave cuts a hand, it heals, the master surrenders him, then the wound reopens and kills the victim. The ruling: the master must pay the value of the slave (to the victim's heirs? Actually the text is ambiguous: 'he pays the value of his slave' likely means the master pays the value of the slave as compensation, since the slave was already surrendered). Second (from al-'Uyun, quoting Abu Hanifa): a slave cuts a finger, and the victim's family ransomed the slave (i.e., paid fida to free him). This ransom is void unless done by court order, and the master owes full diya. This is because the victim's family cannot unilaterally ransom the slave; only the master can. If done without qadi, it is like the master emancipating while knowing of the crime, thus becoming liable. Third (from al-Kafi): a deliberate hand-cutting is settled with the slave as compensation, then the victim emancipates the slave, then dies from injury. The slave is considered compensation (the settlement is valid). If the victim did not emancipate, the slave is returned to the original master, and the victim's heirs have the choice to execute or pardon (since it is intentional).

Key Terms
برأت (bara'at): Healed; the wound closed and recovered.
انتقض الجرح (intaqada al-jarh): The wound reopened; an injury that seemed healed becomes fatal.
صلح (sulh): Settlement; the victim agreed to accept the slave as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if the victim's family ransoms a slave who cut a finger without court order, what is the effect?
A) The ransom is valid and the master is free.
B) The ransom is void and the master owes full diya.
C) The slave goes free.
D) The victim's family must pay diya.

Key Takeaway
Liability in slave-caused injuries can revert if the wound reopens, and out-of-court ransoms are void without qadi.

Son's Acknowledgment of Slave's Freedom and Liability for Diya
Ruling from al-Nawadir on son's acknowledgment and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling from al-Nawadir when a master's son acknowledges a slave is free and then inherits, and explain the basis for the son's liability.

Core Idea
A son's acknowledgment of a slave's freedom before inheriting makes the slave free but imposes diya on the son.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Nawadir collection of rare transmissions. It deals with a scenario where a slave commits a crime, the master's son declares the slave free before the master's death, and then the son inherits the slave. The ruling determines that the slave is free, but the son becomes liable for the diya. Understanding this ruling clarifies the interplay between acknowledgment, inheritance, and liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and sequence of events
A slave commits a crime (jina) incurring diya. The master's son acknowledges (aqarra) before the master dies that the slave is free. Then the master dies, and the son inherits the slave (as part of the estate).
The acknowledgment occurs while the slave is still owned by the father; the son is not yet the owner.
2
Determine the legal effect of the acknowledgment
The son's acknowledgment is treated as a cause for emancipation. Even though the son is not the owner at the time, his statement makes the slave free upon his inheritance? Actually, the text says 'he is free' after the father's death and inheritance. The son's acknowledgment seems to operate retroactively or as a condition.
This is a special ruling from Nawadir; in Zahir al-Riwayah, a non-owner's acknowledgment of freedom is generally ineffective.
3
Identify who is liable for the diya
Because the slave becomes free, he cannot be sold to pay the diya. The son, by his acknowledgment, effectively prevented the victim from claiming against the slave's value. Therefore, the son must pay the diya personally.
Liability is transferred from the slave's value to the son personally.
4
Assess the authority of the ruling
This ruling is from al-Nawadir, a collection of rare transmissions. It may not be the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school. The mufti should be cautious and prefer Zahir al-Riwayah rulings where available.
The authority_weight is 'nawadir', meaning it is less authoritative than the six core books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوادر : عبد جنى فأقر ابن السيد أنه حر فمات السيد فورثه هذا الابن فهو حر وعلى الابن الدية.
And in al-Nawadir: A slave committed a crime, then the son of the master acknowledged that he is free, then the master died, and this son inherited him. So he is free, and upon the son is the diya.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This brief ruling from al-Nawadir (a collection of rare transmissions) deals with a specific scenario: a slave commits a crime. The master's son acknowledges (before his father's death) that the slave is free. Then the master dies, and the son inherits the estate (which presumably includes the slave, but the son's acknowledgment would make the slave free, so he cannot inherit him). The ruling: the slave is indeed free (due to the son's acknowledgment), but the son is liable for the diya (blood money) of the crime. This is because his acknowledgment prevented the victim from claiming against the slave's value, so he must compensate. The source is al-Nawadir, which contains opinions that may not be Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
نوادر (nawadir): Rare or less authoritative transmissions, in contrast to Zahir al-Riwayah.
أقر (aqarra): Acknowledged; the son stated the slave is free.
دية (diyah): Blood money; compensation for homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ruling from al-Nawadir, who is liable for the diya after the son acknowledges the slave is free and inherits?
A) The father's estate.
B) The son.
C) The slave.
D) No one.

Key Takeaway
The son's acknowledgment of a slave's freedom before inheriting makes the slave free but imposes personal liability for the diya on the son, according to this Nawadir ruling.

Liability for Crimes Involving Slaves and Their Offspring
Rulings on pregnant slave committing crime, sold slave giving birth, joint slave's child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different rulings for liability when a pregnant slave commits a crime, and distinguish between Abu Yusuf's and Zufar's positions on diya vs. value.

Core Idea
Claiming a slave's child after a crime makes the claimant liable for the crime.

This lesson examines several scenarios from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq involving slaves and their children when crimes occur. The rulings depend on timing, knowledge, and whether the child is claimed. The text also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Zufar on the measure of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers four distinct cases: (1) pregnant slave commits crime, master emancipates fetus; (2) master frees fetus, then slave commits crime; (3) sold slave gives birth at buyer's within less than six months, seller claims child after injury; (4) jointly-owned slave gives birth, one co-owner claims child after child's crime.
2
Determine the governing principle
The central principle is that claiming paternity/ownership of a child after a crime makes the claimant liable for that crime. The type of liability (diya vs. qimah) is disputed.
3
Trace the differences between Abu Yusuf and Zufar
In the sale case, Abu Yusuf imposes diya, Zufar imposes only the child's value. In the joint slave case, Abu Yusuf always imposes half diya; Zufar distinguishes based on knowledge of the crime.
The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf in the sale case.
4
Understand the role of knowledge
According to Zufar, if the claimant was unaware of the crime at the time of claiming, liability is limited to the child's value. Abu Yusuf holds that knowledge is irrelevant; liability is always diya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جارية جنت وهي حامل فأعتق السيد ما في بطنها وهو يعلم بالجناية صار مختاراً قبل تضع ولو لم يكن عالماً بالجناية، فإن حضر الطالب قبل الوضع خير إن شاء ضمن المولى قيمتها حاملاً، وإن شاء أخذها حاملاً بجنايتها وكان ولدها حراً، وإن حضر بعد ما ولدت أن خير المولى إن شاء دفع، وإن شاء فدى ولا سبيل على الولد وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف : إذا أعتق الرجل ما في بطن جاريته ثم جنت جناية فدفعها بالجناية جاز. وفي العيون أيضاً باع جارية فولدت عند المشتري لأقل من ستة أشهر فجنى على الولد ثم ادعاه البائع وهو يعلم بالجناية فعليه الدية لأصحاب الجناية في قول أبي يوسف. وقال زفر رحمه الله تعالى : عليه القيمة دون الدية والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف. وفيه أيضاً: جارية بين رجلين فولدت ولدها، فإن ادعاه أحدهما وهو عالم بالجناية قال أبو يوسف : الدية عليه، وإن لم يعلم قال زفر : إذا علم فعليه نصف القيمة. وفي العيون جارية بين رجلين جاءت بولد فحتى الولد جناية فادعاه أحدهما، فإن علم بالجناية فعليه نصف الدية، وإن لم يعلم فعليه نصف القيمة، وهذا قول زفر وقال أبو يوسف عليه نصف الدية علم أو لم يعلم.
A slave girl committed a crime while pregnant, so the master emancipated what is in her womb while knowing of the crime; he becomes one who chooses (for ransom) before she gives birth. If he was not aware of the crime, then if the claimant comes before birth, he has the choice: if he wishes he holds the master liable for her value while pregnant, and if he wishes he takes her pregnant with her crime and her child is free. If he comes after she gave birth, the master has the choice: if he wishes he surrenders, and if he wishes he ransoms, and there is no recourse against the child. And in Nawadir Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Yusuf: If a man emancipates what is in his slave girl's womb, then she commits a crime, then he surrenders her for the crime, it is permissible. And in al-'Uyun also: He sold a slave girl, so she gave birth at the buyer's for less than six months, then an injury was inflicted on the child, then the seller claimed him while knowing of the crime; he is liable for the diya to the owners of the crime according to Abu Yusuf. Zufar said: He is liable for the value without diya, and the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. And in it also: A slave girl between two men, she gave birth to her child. If one of them claims him while he is aware of the crime, Abu Yusuf said: Diya is upon him. If he was not aware, Zufar said: If he knows, he owes half the value. And in al-'Uyun: A slave girl between two men, she came with a child, then the child caused a crime, so one of them claimed him. If he knows of the crime, he owes half the diya. If he did not know, he owes half the value. This is Zufar's statement. Abu Yusuf said: He owes half the diya, whether he knows or not.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This lesson collects several rulings about slaves and their offspring when crimes occur. The first ruling: a pregnant slave commits a crime. If the master knowingly emancipates the fetus, he becomes mukhtar (liability for fida). If unaware, and the victim comes before birth, the victim can choose: either hold the master liable for the value of the pregnant slave, or take the pregnant slave (with the crime) and the child will be free. If the victim comes after birth, the master may choose surrender or ransom, and the child is immune. Then from Nawadir: if the master emancipates the fetus and later the slave commits a crime, surrendering her is valid. Then from al-'Uyun: a sold slave gives birth at the buyer's for less than six months (indicating the child may belong to the seller), then the child is injured. The seller claims the child knowing of the injury. Abu Yusuf says seller owes diya; Zufar says value; fatwa on Abu Yusuf. Then from the same source: a joint slave between two men gives birth. One claims the child. If he knows of the crime (done by the child), Abu Yusuf says full diya; if not, Zufar says half value. Another version: child commits crime, one claims him. Abu Yusuf says half diya regardless of knowledge; Zufar distinguishes. The fatwa likely on Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
جارية (jariyah): Slave girl; female slave.
حامل (hamil): Pregnant.
ادعى (idda'a): Claimed paternity or ownership of the child.
مختار (mukhtar): One who chooses; here, one who becomes liable for ransom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a sold slave girl giving birth at the buyer's for less than six months, and the seller claims the child after it is injured, what is the fatwa according to the text?
A) The seller owes diya (Abu Yusuf's view).
B) The seller owes value (Zufar's view).
C) The buyer owes diya.
D) No one owes anything.

Key Takeaway
Liability for a slave's child's crime passes to the one who claims the child; the relied-upon Hanafi position is that diya is owed regardless of knowledge.

Intra-Hanafi Disputes on Slave Crimes and Usurpation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave emancipation and usurpation liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and Muhammad regarding liability and distribution in cases of emancipation designation and usurped slave crimes, and apply them to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Intra-Hanafi disputes expose the tension between liability based on knowledge and value on emancipation, and between distribution to heirs and retention.

This lesson examines three related rulings from the Hanafi school, each containing disagreement among the early masters. The first involves a master who declares emancipation for two slaves, one commits a crime, then the master designates that slave. The second involves both slaves committing crimes after the declaration. The third involves a usurped slave who kills two victims—one while usurped, one after return. Understanding these disputes deepens your ability to apply Hanafi principles in multi-variable cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario and dispute
Read the first part: the master says to two slaves (an ijab), then one commits a crime, then the master directs the emancipation to that slave. Note the disagreement: Abu Yusuf says diya is due if the master knew of the crime; Zufar says the slave's value is due.
Abu Yusuf conditions liability on knowledge; Zufar sees the emancipation as triggering value liability regardless of knowledge.
2
Analyze the second scenario (both slaves commit crimes after ijab)
Read the al-Zahiriya passage: both slaves commit crimes after the ijab, then only one is freed. The freed slave is liable for the lesser of his value and the diya of his crime; the other slave remains property with standard surrender/ransom option.
The master does not become 'chooser for fida' automatically; the option remains separate.
3
Examine the usurpation scenario (al-Tajrid)
Read the third case: a usurped slave kills a person by mistake while with the usurper, then is returned to the master and kills another. The master surrenders for both crimes. Abu Yusuf rules that the guardian of the first victim takes half the value from the usurper and pays it to the first victim's heir, then reclaims from the usurper.
Pay attention to the flow: usurper pays half value to first guardian, who passes it to first victim's heir, then re-collects from usurper.
4
Compare Abu Yusuf with Muhammad and Zufar on the usurpation case
Muhammad and Zufar disagree: the guardian takes half the value and keeps it, not paying it to the first victim's heir. The difference hinges on whether the first victim's right is prioritized or whether the usurper's liability is settled first.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf within the school; both views are recorded.
5
Synthesize the practical implications
The disputes show how the Hanafi school handles multiple liabilities and the role of knowledge, timing, and possession. In practice, a mufti would need to know which view is preferred (mufta bihi) and why.
The text does not specify mufta bihi, so the student must recognize that further tarjih is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لعبديه ثم جنى أحدهما ثم صرف المولى العتق إليه قال أبو يوسف : إن علم بالجناية فعليه الدية. وقال زفر عليه القيمة وفي الظهيرية : ولو جنى كل واحد منهما بعد الايجاب ثم بين العتق في أحدهما عتق ولزمه الأقل من قيمته ومن الدية وبقي الآخر ملكاً له يقال ادفعه أو افده بالدية ولا يصير مختاراً للفداء، ولكن لو كانت جناية أحدهما قطع يد رجل وجناية الآخر قتل نفس لا يختلف الجواب. وفي التجريد قال أبو يوسف : إذا غصب رجل عبداً فقتل عنده قتيلاً خطأ ورده على مولاه فقتل عنده قتيلاً ودفعه المولى بالجنايتين رجع الولي على الغاصب بنصف القيمة ودفع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثم يرجع به على الغاصب فيسلم له . وقال محمد وزفر : يأخذ نصف القيمة فيسلم له ولا يدفعها إلى ولي الجناية .
He said to his two slaves, then one of them committed a crime, then the master directed the emancipation to him. Abu Yusuf said: If he knows of the crime, then diya is upon him. Zufar said: Value is upon him. And in al-Zahiriya: If each of them committed a crime after the (emancipation) declaration, then the emancipation is executed on one of them, he is freed and he is liable for the lesser of his value and the diya, and the other remains his property; it is said: surrender him or ransom him with diya, and he does not become one who chooses for fida. But if one's crime was cutting a hand and the other's was killing a person, the answer does not differ. And in al-Tajrid, Abu Yusuf said: If a man usurps a slave, and the slave kills a person by mistake while in his possession, then he returns him to his master, and he kills another person by mistake while with the master, and the master surrenders him for both crimes, then the guardian (of the first victim) takes half the value from the usurper and pays it to the guardian of the first crime, then he (the guardian) returns with it to the usurper, so it remains for him. Muhammad and Zufar said: He takes half the value and it remains for him, and he does not pay it to the guardian of the crime.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This lesson combines two distinct but related issues. First, a master says to his two slaves (an emancipation declaration), then one commits a crime, then the master designates emancipation for that particular one. Abu Yusuf says if he knew of the crime, diya is due; Zufar says value. The second part from al-Zahiriya deals with both slaves committing crimes after the declaration, then only one is freed: he is liable for the lesser of value and diya, and the other remains slave with standard surrender/ransom option. The third part from al-Tajrid involves a usurped slave: he kills one victim while usurped, then is returned, then kills another. The master surrenders for both. The guardian of the first victim claims half value from the usurper and pays to the first victim's heir, then reclaims from the usurper. Muhammad and Zufar say the value remains with the guardian without paying to the first heir.

Key Terms
أوجب (awjaba): Made the emancipation declaration binding (e.g., said 'you are free after my death').
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property unlawfully.
ولي (wali): Guardian, here the heir of the victim who has the right to blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf in al-Tajrid, what does the guardian of the first victim do with the half value taken from the usurper?
A) Keeps it.
B) Pays it to the guardian of the first crime.
C) Returns it to the usurper.
D) Gives it to the master.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi disputes on slave liability revolve around knowledge and timing, and distribution of compensation depends on whether the priority is the victim's right or the usurper's accountability.
