Liability of Executor and Agent When Freeing a Slave Who Committed a Crime
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for freeing a slave who committed a crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the divergent positions of Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the executor's liability for diya when freeing a slave with knowledge of a crime, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An executor who frees a slave knowing of a crime may or may not be liable for diya — the later Hanafi position exonerates the agent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a nuanced scenario: a slave commits a crime, then the master bequeaths his emancipation. Who bears the financial liability if the executor or agent frees the slave knowing of the crime? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, with Zufar holding that the executor is not liable, Abu Yusuf initially disagreeing, and then adopting the view of Zufar and Muhammad in his later opinion. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and practical application of these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic scenario
A slave commits a crime (jinaayah), and the master makes a bequest (wasiyyah) to free him during his terminal illness. After death, the heir or executor frees the slave. Determine the legal consequences based on knowledge and timing.
Two variables: timing of bequest (before/after crime) and knowledge of the one who frees.
2
State Zufar's position
Zufar holds that if the executor was unaware of the crime, the slave's value is due from the deceased's estate (not the executor personally). If aware, the text does not specify his ruling, but the implication is that he may not be personally liable either, as his view aligns with the later Abu Yusuf.
Zufar's view is cited as a baseline.
3
Outline Abu Yusuf's two opinions
Abu Yusuf initially held that if the executor knew of the crime, diya is upon the executor (personal liability). Later, he retracted, holding that the executor is not liable, aligning with Zufar and Muhammad. This later opinion is supported by analogy with agency in sales from Kitab al-Buyu'.
The later opinion is considered mufta bihi.
4
Distinguish the timing of the bequest
If the bequest was made before the crime, the executor is liable for the crime if he frees knowing; if unaware, he is liable for the value without recourse to heirs. This is different from the case where the bequest was after the crime, where the later opinion exonerates the executor.
Timing changes the ruling significantly.
5
Apply to agency (wakalah)
If the master appointed two agents to free the slave, and one agent frees knowing of the crime, the master is liable for the slave's value if the master was unaware. This further illustrates that the agent himself is not personally liable.
Agency rulings reinforce the later opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد جنى فأوصى المولى بعتقه في مرضه فأعتقه الوارث أو الوصي، فإن الوصي عالماً بالجناية فعليه الدية قدر المال والزيادة من الثلث، وإن لم يكن عالماً بها تجب القيمة في مال الميت في قول زفر، ولم يذكر أن الذي أعتق هل يضمن وماذا يضمن. وقال أبو يوسف : إن علم الذي أعتقه بالجناية فعليه الدية . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : أن يكون هذا قول أبي يوسف الأول أما على قياس قوله الآخر ينبغي أن يكون قوله مثل قول زفر كما قال في آخر كتاب البيوع : لو اشترى عبداً ولم ينقد الثمن حتى وكل وكيلاً بعتقه فأعتقه الوكيل لا ضمان على الوكيل في قول أبي يوسف الآخر وهو قول محمد. وهكذا روي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. هذا إذا كانت الوصية بالعتق بعد ما جنى، أما إذا أوصى بعتقه قبل الجناية ثم جنى فمات الموصي فأعتقه الوصي وهو يعلم بالجناية فهو ضامن للجناية، وإن لم يعلم فهو ضامن من القيمة ولا يرجع على الورثة. إذا وكل رجلين بعتق عبده ثم إن العبد جنى جناية ثم أعتقه الوكيل وهو يعلم بالجناية فالمولى ضامن لقيمة العبد إن لم يكن عالماً بالجناية. وفي المنتقى وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : إذا أوصى بعتق عبده ثم مات وقد كان أوصى إلى رجل فجنى العبد جناية بعد موت الموصي ثم أعتقه الوصي وهو يعلم بالجناية فهو مختار الدية في ماله، وإن لم يعلم فعليه القيمة. وفي الظهيرية : قيمته من جميع
A slave committed a crime, so the master bequeathed his emancipation in his illness, then the heir or executor freed him. If the executor was aware of the crime, then diya is upon him to the extent of the property and the excess from the third. If he was not aware, then the value is obligated from the deceased's estate according to Zufar. He did not mention whether the one who freed guarantees and what he guarantees. Abu Yusuf said: If the one who freed him knew of the crime, then diya is upon him. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This is Abu Yusuf's first opinion; according to the analogy of his latter opinion, his statement should be like Zufar's, as he said at the end of Kitab al-Buyu': If he bought a slave and did not pay the price until he appointed an agent to free him and the agent freed him, there is no liability on the agent according to Abu Yusuf's latter opinion, and it is Muhammad's statement. Thus it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. This is if the bequest of emancipation was after the crime. As for if he bequeathed his emancipation before the crime, then he commits a crime, then the testator dies, and the executor frees him knowing of the crime, then he is liable for the crime. If he does not know, he is liable for the value and does not recourse to the heirs. If he appointed two men to free his slave, then the slave commits a crime, then the agent frees him while knowing of the crime, the master is liable for the value of the slave if he was not aware of the crime. And in al-Muntaqa and in Nawadir Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad: If he bequeaths emancipation of his slave, then dies, and he had appointed an executor, then the slave commits a crime after the testator's death, then the executor frees him while knowing of the crime, he is one who chooses diya from his own property. If he does not know, then value is upon him. And in al-Zahiriya: his value from all...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This lesson consolidates several rulings regarding emancipation of a slave who has committed a crime, particularly when the emancipation is by will (wasiyyah) or by agency. The first case: a slave commits a crime, then the master makes a will to free him during illness. The heir or executor frees him. If the executor knew of the crime, he is liable for diya (up to the value of the estate and beyond from one-third). If unaware, Zufar says the value is due from the deceased's estate. Abu Yusuf initially said diya upon the freed if he knew. There is a later opinion of Abu Yusuf, siding with Zufar and Muhammad, that the freed (executor) is not liable. Then the rule distinguishes whether the will was made before or after the crime. If after, the executor's knowledge triggers liability; if before, and the testator dies, the executor frees knowing of the crime, he is liable for the crime; if unaware, liability for value without recourse. Then agency: if two agents are appointed to free, and the slave commits a crime, then an agent frees knowing, the master is liable for the slave's value if the master did not know. Finally, from al-Muntaqa and Nawadir: a bequest after death, slave commits crime after testator's death, executor frees knowing, he is mukhtar for diya from his own wealth; if unaware, value. The incomplete final line from al-Zahiriya suggests value from all (estate?).

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): Bequest; a will made by the master to free the slave after death.
وصي (wasi): Executor; person appointed to execute the will.
وكيل (wakil): Agent; someone authorized to perform an act on behalf of the principal.
مختار الدية (mukhtar al-diyah): One who chooses the diya (i.e., becomes liable to pay it).
ضمان (daman): Liability; financial responsibility for a loss or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the latter opinion of Abu Yusuf (as cited by Abu al-Layth), if an agent frees a slave knowing of the crime, is the agent liable?
A) Yes, liable for diya.
B) Yes, liable for value.
C) No, no liability on the agent.
D) Liability depends on the master's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position exonerates the executor/agent from personal liability when freeing a slave who committed a crime, as the liability lies with the estate.

The Master's Option and Liability When Claiming a Slave Child
Presents the ruling on master's option with intra-madhhab disputes on claiming a slave child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's option to ransom or hand over a criminal slave and apply Abu Yusuf's ruling on full diya when a father claims a slave child knowing of the crime.

Core Idea
The master may choose to ransom or hand over his slave; claiming a slave child after a crime obligates full diya.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq presents the core Hanafi rule: when a slave commits a crime, the master has the option to hand over the slave or ransom him. The sharh then expands into several cases involving fetuses, sales, and joint ownership, revealing an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yusuf and Zufar over the amount payable when a father claims a slave child. Understanding these details sharpens your ability to apply the option and the liability rules.

Guided Path
1
State the basic rule
Identify the master's option when a slave commits a crime: he may hand over or ransom. The child of such a slave is not liable.
2
Locate the expansions
Note the three additional cases from Nawadir and Al-Uyun that test the boundaries of the rule.
3
Analyze the first expansion
Freeing a fetus: the mother may still be handed over after a crime, showing the option applies even if the slave was partly freed.
4
Examine the second expansion
A sold slave woman gives birth; the seller claims the child knowing it committed a crime. Abu Yusuf says full diya, Zufar says value. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.
5
Examine the third expansion
A jointly-owned slave woman's child commits a crime; the claiming co-owner pays half diya per Abu Yusuf or half value per Zufar.
6
Apply the tarjih
Conclude that the mufta bihi position is full diya (or half diya for joint) when the father claims the child knowing of the crime.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدكما حر : خير المولى إن شاء دفع، وإن شاء فدى ولا سبيل على الولد وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف : إذا أعتق الرجل ما في بطن جاريته ثم جنت جناية فدفعها بالجناية جاز. وفي العيون أيضاً باع جارية فولدت عند المشتري لأقل من ستة أشهر فجنى على الولد ثم ادعاه البائع وهو يعلم بالجناية فعليه الدية لأصحاب الجناية في قول أبي يوسف. وقال زفر رحمه الله تعالى : عليه القيمة دون الدية والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف. وفيه أيضاً: جارية بين رجلين فولدت ولدها، فإن ادعاه أحدهما وهو عالم بالجناية قال أبو يوسف : الدية عليه، وإن لم يعلم قال زفر : إذا علم فعليه نصف القيمة. وفي العيون جارية بين رجلين جاءت بولد فحتى الولد جناية فادعاه أحدهما، فإن علم بالجناية فعليه نصف الدية، وإن لم يعلم فعليه نصف القيمة، وهذا قول زفر وقال أبو يوسف عليه نصف الدية علم أو لم يعلم.
One of you is free: the master has the option—if he wishes he may hand over, and if he wishes he may ransom, and there is no recourse against the child. And in the Nawadir of Ibn Sama'a from Abu Yusuf: If a man frees what is in the womb of his slave woman and then she commits a crime, and he hands her over for the crime, it is permissible. And in Al-Uyun also: He sold a slave woman, and she gave birth at the buyer's before six months, and a crime was committed against the child, then the seller claimed him while knowing of the crime—then upon him is the diya for the people of the crime according to the saying of Abu Yusuf. And Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Upon him is the value without the diya, and the fatwa is upon the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in it also: A slave woman between two men, and she gave birth to her child; if one of them claims him while he is aware of the crime, Abu Yusuf said: The diya is upon him. And if he did not know, Zufar said: If he knows, then upon him is half the value. And in Al-Uyun: A slave woman between two men, she came with a child, and then the child committed a crime; one of them claimed him. If he knew of the crime, then upon him is half the diya; if he did not know, then upon him is half the value. This is the saying of Zufar, and Abu Yusuf said: Upon him is half the diya whether he knew or not.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq, beginning with the matn stating that if a master says to his two slaves 'one of you is free', and one then commits a crime, the master has the option to hand over the slave or ransom him. The child of such a slave is not liable. The sharh then expands with several auxiliary cases from Nawadir and Al-Uyun. The first expansion: if a man frees his slave's fetus and the mother later commits a crime, handing her over is valid. The second: a sold slave woman gives birth shortly after sale; the seller claims the child but knows the child had previously committed a crime. Abu Yusuf holds the seller must pay full diya; Zufar says only the slave's value. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf. The third case involves a jointly-owned slave woman's child: if one co-owner claims the child knowing the child committed a crime, Abu Yusuf says pay full diya; Zufar says half value if he knew, but if he did not know, then half value? Actually there are two versions: in one, if he did not know, Zufar says nothing? The text is slightly ambiguous but the dispute centers on whether the claiming father pays diya or value. The mufta bihi position is Abu Yusuf's.

Key Terms
خيار المولى (khiyar al-mawla): The master's right to choose between paying ransom (fida) or handing over the slave (daf') when the slave commits a crime.
دفع (daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's family to satisfy the crime liability.
فداء (fida'): Ransoming the slave by paying compensation (diya or value) to keep him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Half the value
B) The full diya
C) The value only
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The master has an option to ransom or hand over his criminal slave; if he claims a slave child after a crime, he pays full diya per Abu Yusuf.

Two Slaves: One Freed After Crime
Ruling on the obligation of the master when freeing a slave after his crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a master who frees one of two slaves after they have committed crimes, and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

Core Idea
When freeing a slave after his crime, the master owes the lesser of the slave's value and the diya — fairness prevents double burden.

Here we examine a scenario where a master declares 'one of you is free' to his two slaves, then a crime occurs, and then he specifies which slave is free. The Hanafi scholars differ on what the master must pay: is it the full diya (blood money) or the slave's market value? The rule from Al-Zahiriyyah provides a nuanced resolution that balances the rights of the victim and the master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial declaration
The master says to two slaves 'one of you is free' — this is a declaration (ijab) without specification. At this point, neither slave is yet free.
2
Note the crime and its timing
After the declaration, a crime occurs (e.g., one slave cuts a man's hand). Then the master specifies which slave is free. The timing of the crime relative to the declaration and specification is crucial.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that if the master knew of the crime at the time of specification, he must pay the diya (fixed blood money).
Knowledge of the crime is a condition.
4
State Zufar's position
Zufar holds that regardless of knowledge, the master must pay the value (qima) of the freed slave.
No condition of knowledge.
5
Apply the Zahiriyyah ruling
If both slaves commit crimes after the declaration, then the master specifies one for freedom. The freed slave owes the lesser of his own market value and the diya. The other remains a slave; the master may hand him over or ransom him with diya. The master does not become automatically liable for ransom.
This ruling is likely the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لعبديه ثم جنى أحدهما ثم صرف المولى العتق إليه قال أبو يوسف : إن علم بالجناية فعليه الدية. وقال زفر عليه القيمة وفي الظهيرية : ولو جنى كل واحد منهما بعد الايجاب ثم بين العتق في أحدهما عتق ولزمه الأقل من قيمته ومن الدية وبقي الآخر ملكاً له يقال ادفعه أو افده بالدية ولا يصير مختاراً للفداء، ولكن لو كانت جناية أحدهما قطع يد رجل وجناية الآخر قتل نفس لا يختلف الجواب.
He said to his two slaves, then one of them committed a crime, then the master directed the emancipation to him. Abu Yusuf said: If he knew of the crime, then upon him is the diya. And Zufar said: Upon him is the value. And in Al-Zahiriyyah: If each of them committed a crime after the declaration (of freedom for one), then he specifies the emancipation for one of them, he is free, and he is liable for the lesser of his value and the diya. And the other remains his property; it is said: hand him over or ransom him with diya, and he does not become a chooser for ransom. But if one's crime was cutting a man's hand and the other's was killing a person, the answer does not differ.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a master tells his two slaves 'one of you is free', then one commits a crime, and then the master specifies the emancipation for that slave. Abu Yusuf holds that if the master knew of the crime at the time of specification, he must pay the diya. Zufar says he must pay the value (qima). The ruling in Al-Zahiriyyah adds nuance: if both slaves commit crimes after the declaration, and then the master specifies one for freedom, the freed slave is liable for the lesser of his value and the diya. The other slave remains a slave, and the master has the option to hand him over or ransom him with diya. The master does not become a 'chooser for ransom' (i.e., automatically liable for fida) by this specification. The ruling remains the same even if one crime was amputation and the other murder.

Key Terms
أقل من قيمته ومن الدية (aqall min qimatihi wa min al-diya): The obligation to pay whichever is less: the slave's market value or the diya.
ديَة (diya): Fixed blood money paid for bodily harm or homicide.
قيمَة (qima): Market value of the slave at the time of the crime or freeing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two slaves where both commit crimes after the master's declaration 'one of you is free', and then the master specifies one for freedom, what does the freed slave owe?
A) The full diya
B) The full value
C) The lesser of value and diya
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
When both slaves commit crimes and one is freed, the freed slave owes the lesser of his value and the diya, and the other slave remains the master's property to be handed over or ransomed.

Usurped Slave's Crimes After Return: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad & Zufar
Dispute on distribution of compensation when a usurped slave is returned and commits more crimes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar on the distribution of compensation when a usurped slave commits crimes both during and after usurpation, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two views.

Core Idea
When a usurped slave kills after return, Abu Yusuf requires the usurper to compensate the first victim twice; Muhammad and Zufar say once is enough.

This passage from Al-Tajrid presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and the pair of Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar. The case involves a slave who was wrongfully seized (ghasb) and later returned to the owner after committing an accidental killing while with the usurper. After return, the slave commits a second killing. The owner surrenders him to both victims. The question is: how should the usurper compensate the guardian of the first victim? The two positions reflect different views on the extent of the usurper's liability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the case facts
Identify the parties: a usurper (ghasib) takes a slave, the slave kills one person accidentally in the usurper's possession, then the slave is returned to the owner and kills a second person. The owner hands over the slave for both crimes. Determine who is liable for compensation.
The slave is surrendered, meaning his value is used to compensate the victims.
2
State Abu Yusuf's view
According to Abu Yusuf, the guardian of the first victim (the one killed under the usurper) recovers half the slave's value from the usurper. Then that guardian pays that amount to the guardian of the first crime (which is himself; the text describes a circular payment). Then he recovers that amount again from the usurper, so he ends up with the amount and the usurper pays twice.
Abu Yusuf's view effectively makes the usurper pay twice: once to cover the first victim's share, and then again to cover the second victim's share from the slave's value.
3
State Muhammad and Zufar's view
Muhammad and Zufar hold that the guardian simply takes half the value from the usurper and keeps it, without any further payment to the guardian of the first crime. Thus the usurper pays only once.
In their view, the first victim's guardian does not receive anything beyond the half value, and the usurper is not liable for a second payment.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference is whether the first victim's guardian receives the half value directly (Muhammad/Zufar) or whether there is a second payment from the usurper that ultimately goes to the first guardian (Abu Yusuf). In Abu Yusuf's view, the usurper ends up paying twice; in Muhammad/Zufar's view, the usurper pays only once and the first guardian gets nothing extra.
The difference affects the total compensation paid by the usurper and the allocation between the two victims' guardians.
5
Consider possible tarjih
The passage does not indicate which view is preferred or relied upon for fatwa. Later Hanafi works may have a preferred position. Students should note that this is an unresolved difference in the context of this text.
In practice, a mufti would need to consult later authoritative works to determine the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجريد قال أبو يوسف : إذا غصب رجل عبداً فقتل عنده قتيلاً خطأ ورده على مولاه فقتل عنده قتيلاً ودفعه المولى بالجنايتين رجع الولي على الغاصب بنصف القيمة ودفع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثم يرجع به على الغاصب فيسلم له . وقال محمد وزفر : يأخذ نصف القيمة فيسلم له ولا يدفعها إلى ولي الجناية .
And in Al-Tajrid, Abu Yusuf said: If a man usurps a slave, and then the slave accidentally kills a person in his possession, and he returns him to his master, and then the slave kills another person in the master's possession, and the master hands him over for both crimes, then the guardian (of the first victim) takes recourse against the usurper for half the value, and pays it to the guardian of the first crime, then he takes recourse again against the usurper, and it comes to him safely. And Muhammad and Zufar said: He takes half the value and it comes to him safely, and he does not pay it to the guardian of the first crime.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute from Al-Tajrid. The case: a slave is usurped (ghasb) and while with the usurper, he accidentally kills someone. The usurper returns the slave to the owner. Then, while with the owner, the slave kills another person. The owner hands over the slave for both crimes (i.e., surrenders him). Now, the guardian of the first victim wants compensation. The slave's value is presumably taken. Abu Yusuf's view: the guardian of the first victim can claim half the value from the usurper (since the first crime occurred under the usurper's possession), and then that guardian pays that half to the guardian of the first crime? Actually the text is somewhat confusing: 'رجع الولي على الغاصب بنصف القيمة ودفع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثم يرجع به على الغاصب فيسلم له' This means the (first) guardian recovers half the value from the usurper, pays it to the guardian of the first crime (himself?), then again recovers from the usurper, ending up with the amount. Perhaps it means the guardian takes half from the usurper and gives it to the first victim's guardian (who is the same? Unclear). Muhammad and Zufar hold that the guardian simply takes half the value and keeps it, without paying to the first guardian (meaning the first guardian gets nothing extra?). The dispute is about the proper distribution of compensation from the usurper.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful seizure of property; in this case, a slave taken without right.
ولي الجناية (wali al-jinaya): The guardian of the victim in a crime; the one entitled to claim compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad and Zufar, what happens to the half value taken from the usurper?
A) It is given to the first guardian
B) It is given to the guardian of the second crime
C) The guardian keeps it and does not pay it to the first guardian
D) It is split between both guardians

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar on usurped slave's crimes turns on whether the usurper must pay compensation twice or once, with the practical difference being the total amount he must pay.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Executor's Liability for a Slave's Crime: The Main Dispute
Ruling on executor's liability for diya when freeing a slave with known crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Zufar and Abu Yusuf regarding an executor's liability when freeing a slave who committed a crime, and explain the later Hanafi position.

Core Idea
An executor who frees a slave knowing the crime pays diya; if unaware, no personal liability.

When a master wills emancipation of a slave who has committed a crime, and the executor (or heir) frees him, a dispute arises over the executor's liability. The Hanafi school has two positions, with Abu Yusuf eventually aligning with Zufar: knowledge is key. This lesson unpacks that dispute through the lens of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the main case: the master wills emancipation after the slave committed a crime, and the executor frees him. Differentiate from variations.
The will is made in terminal illness (marad al-mawt), so it is limited to one-third of the estate.
2
State Zufar's Ruling
Zufar holds: if the executor knows of the crime when freeing, he must pay diya; if unaware, the slave's value is due from the testator's estate.
Diya here means the full blood money for the crime.
3
State Abu Yusuf's First Position
Abu Yusuf initially said only the executor who knows is liable for diya, without distinction for the unaware case.
This position is recorded as his earlier saying.
4
Analyze the Reconciliation by Abu al-Layth
Abu al-Layth notes that Abu Yusuf later changed his view to align with Zufar, based on a similar ruling in Kitab al-Buyu' regarding an agent who frees a slave without knowledge.
The analogy: if an agent buys a slave and then frees him without knowing the price was unpaid, the agent is not liable.
5
Determine the Operative Hanafi Position
The later saying of Abu Yusuf (no liability for the executor if unaware) is the mufta bihi (relied upon) position within the school.
This is also supported by narrations from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد جنى فأوصى المولى بعتقه في مرضه فأعتقه الوارث أو الوصي، فإن الوصي عالماً بالجناية فعليه الدية قدر المال والزيادة من الثلث، وإن لم يكن عالماً بها تجب القيمة في مال الميت في قول زفر، ولم يذكر أن الذي أعتق هل يضمن وماذا يضمن. وقال أبو يوسف : إن علم الذي أعتقه بالجناية فعليه الدية . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : أن يكون هذا قول أبي يوسف الأول أما على قياس قوله الآخر ينبغي أن يكون قوله مثل قول زفر كما قال في آخر كتاب البيوع : لو اشترى عبداً ولم ينقد الثمن حتى وكل وكيلاً بعتقه فأعتقه الوكيل لا ضمان على الوكيل في قول أبي يوسف الآخر وهو قول محمد. وهكذا روي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. هذا إذا كانت الوصية بالعتق بعد ما جنى، أما إذا أوصى بعتقه قبل الجناية ثم جنى فمات الموصي فأعتقه الوصي وهو يعلم بالجناية فهو ضامن للجناية، وإن لم يعلم فهو ضامن من القيمة ولا يرجع على الورثة. إذا وكل رجلين بعتق عبده ثم إن العبد جنى جناية ثم أعتقه الوكيل وهو يعلم بالجناية فالمولى ضامن لقيمة العبد إن لم يكن عالماً بالجناية. وفي المنتقى وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : إذا أوصى بعتق عبده ثم مات وقد كان أوصى إلى رجل فجنى العبد جناية بعد موت الموصي ثم أعتقه الوصي وهو يعلم بالجناية فهو مختار الدية في ماله، وإن لم يعلم فعليه القيمة. وفي الظهيرية : قيمته من جميع
A slave committed a crime, then the master willed his emancipation in his illness, and the heir or executor freed him. If the executor is aware of the crime, then upon him is the diya in the amount of the property and the increase from the third (of the estate). And if he was not aware, then the value is obligatory in the property of the deceased according to Zufar's saying, and he did not mention whether the one who freed is liable and for what. And Abu Yusuf said: If the one who freed him knew of the crime, then upon him is the diya. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This could be the first saying of Abu Yusuf; but as for the analogy of his other saying, his saying should be like Zufar's saying, as he said at the end of Kitab al-Buyu': If he bought a slave and did not pay the price until he appointed an agent to free him, and the agent freed him, there is no liability on the agent according to the other saying of Abu Yusuf, and this is the saying of Muhammad. And similarly it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. This is if the will of emancipation was after the crime. But if he willed his emancipation before the crime, then he committed the crime, then the testator died, and the executor freed him while knowing of the crime, he is liable for the crime. And if he did not know, he is liable for the value and does not recourse against the heirs. If he appointed two men to free his slave, and then the slave committed a crime, then the agent freed him while knowing of the crime, the master is liable for the value of the slave if he was not aware of the crime. And in Al-Muntaqa and in Nawadir Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: If he willed the emancipation of his slave, then died, and he had appointed a man (as executor), and the slave committed a crime after the testator's death, then the executor freed him while knowing of the crime, he is a chooser for diya in his property; and if he did not know, then upon him is the value. And in Al-Zahiriyyah: his value from all (unfinished).
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage discusses multiple sub-cases involving a slave who commits a crime and then the master makes a will of emancipation during his terminal illness. The heir or executor frees the slave. The key dispute: Zufar holds that if the executor knows of the crime, he pays diya (from the estate's third?), but if he does not know, the slave's value is due from the estate. Abu Yusuf initially held that only if the freer knows does he pay diya. However, Abu al-Layth notes that later Abu Yusuf changed his opinion to align with Zufar, as seen in his later ruling in Kitab al-Buyu' where an agent who frees a slave without knowledge is not liable. The text then distinguishes two scenarios: (1) the will was made after the crime (as above), and (2) the will was made before the crime. In the latter, if the executor knows of the crime when freeing, he is liable for the crime; if he does not know, he is liable for the value and cannot recourse to the heirs. The passage also includes the case of two agents freed by the master: if an agent frees a slave knowing of the crime, the master is liable for the value if the master did not know. Finally, a narration from Muhammad in Al-Muntaqa: the executor is a chooser for diya (meaning he has the option to pay diya from his own property) if he knew, otherwise he pays the value.

Key Terms
مختار الفداء (mukhtar al-fida'): One who chooses to ransom; here the executor who knows of the crime becomes a chooser for diya, liable to pay from his own property.
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation for homicide or bodily injury.
قيمة (qimah): Value; the market price of the slave, which is taken from the estate if the executor is unaware.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dispute in this lesson, what is the ruling when an executor frees a slave who committed a crime, and the executor did not know of the crime?
A) The executor must pay the diya from his own property.
B) The value of the slave is due from the testator's estate.
C) The executor is not liable for anything.
D) The heirs must pay the diya.

Key Takeaway
An executor's knowledge of a slave's crime at the time of freeing determines personal liability for diya or value from the estate.

Variations: Will Before Crime, Two Agents, and Muhammad's Narration
Further scenarios on liability for a slave's crime after emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the liability rules based on the timing of the will, the number of agents, and the narration from Muhammad, and apply them to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Timing of will and number of agents affect whether executor personally pays diya or value from estate.

The previous lesson covered the core dispute. Now we explore variations: what if the will was made before the slave committed the crime? What if the master appointed two agents? And a special narration from Imam Muhammad further defines the executor's role as a 'chooser' (mukhtar al-fida'). These rules demonstrate the depth of Hanafi case law on liability.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate Timing of the Will
First scenario: will after the crime (already covered). Second scenario: will before the crime. The text states: if the executor knows of the crime when freeing, he is liable for the crime (i.e., diya from his property). If unaware, he pays the value from the estate and cannot recourse to the heirs.
The reasoning: the will was valid before the crime, so the slave's freedom is effective, but the crime attaches to the slave's value.
2
Analyze the Case of Two Agents
If the master appointed two agents to free the slave, and the slave then commits a crime, and one agent frees him knowing of the crime, then the master is liable for the value of the slave if the master was unaware. This implies the agent is not personally liable.
The master's liability flows from his appointment; the agent's knowledge does not transfer to the master unless the master also knew.
3
Examine Muhammad's Narration from Al-Muntaqa
Muhammad narrates: if the will was made, then the slave commits a crime after the testator's death, and the executor frees knowing, he is a 'chooser for diya' (mukhtar al-fida'), meaning he has the option to pay diya from his property or hand over the slave. If unaware, he pays the value.
This adds the concept of choice (ikhtiyar) in liability.
4
Synthesize the Variations
Overall, knowledge remains the key factor. The timing of the will (before or after crime) and the number of agents affect who is liable (executor, master, or estate).
The rulings are consistent with the principle that liability follows the ability to choose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Various Rulings on Slave Crimes (Diyat)
Compilation of rulings on slave crimes in diyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for seven specific scenarios involving slave crimes and compensation.

Core Idea
A master's actions after a slave's crime can shift liability; slaves' value and crime sequence determine compensation shares.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings on slave crimes. It covers cases from a master knowingly freeing a criminal slave to complex calculations when a slave commits multiple crimes. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi approach to justice and liability in slave ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle: Master's knowledge triggers liability.
When a master frees a slave knowing of the crime, he becomes liable for the arsh, similar to selling the slave.
2
Analyze the two-slave ambiguity case.
If a master says 'one of you is free' and one commits a crime, then the master dies without specifying, each slave becomes half-free and must earn half his value, and the master's estate pays the full value of the criminal slave.
3
Understand the usurped slave scenario.
If a usurped slave dies in the usurper's hand and had prior crimes, his value goes to the crime victims; the master has no option.
4
Examine the rules on slave confession.
A slave's confession of a crime during slavery is invalid, and he does not have to work for it after emancipation. Confession after emancipation is also not binding.
5
Calculate apportionment for multiple crimes and the will case.
When a slave commits multiple crimes (e.g., hand cut then killing), the blood money is shared proportionally. If master and victim dispute the sequence, the victim's word is taken. Also, if a master wills emancipation and the slave commits a crime, the will becomes void if heirs refuse ransom, unless the slave pays from other funds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو عالماً بها لزمه الارش كبيعه وتعليق عتقه بقتل فلان ورميه وشجه إن فعل ذلك عبد ولو قال لعبديه وقيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر : ثم قتل أحدهما إنساناً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان وهو عالم بالجناية عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه ويسعى في نصف قيمته ويجب على المولى قيمة العبد الجاني فيستوفي من جميع تركته ولا يصير مختاراً للفداء بالموت من غير بيان واحد من العبدين. وفي التجريد ولو قتل العبد المغصوب في يد الغاصب ومات وقد كان جنى قبل الغصب جنايات فالقيمة لأصحاب الجنايات ولا خيار للمولى في ذلك ولا يجوز إقرار العبد المأذون والمحجور عليه بالجناية ولا يسعى بعد العتق، ولو أقر بعد العتق أنه كان جنى في حالة الرق لم يلزمه شيء، ولو قتل العبد قتيلاً خطأ ثم قطعت يد العبد ثم آخر خطأ فأرش يده يسلم لأولياء الجناية الأولى ثم يدفعه العبد فيكون بين ولي الجنايتين، ولو اختلف المولى. وولي الجناية فادعى المولى أن قتل كان قبل الجناية وادعى ولي الجناية أنه كان بعدها فالقول قول الولي. ولو شج انساناً موضحة وقيمته ألف ثم قال قتل آخر وقيمته ألفان فإن المولى يدفع بينهما على أحد وعشرين سهماً لصاحب الموضحة سهم، وعشرون لولي القتيل. وكذلك لو كان القتل قبل الشجة وما يحدث من الزيادة والنقصان فهو على الشركة. وفي العيون: إذا أوصى بعتق عبد له فجنى العبد جناية أرشادهم فقالت الورثة بعد موت الموصى لا نفدي فلهم . فإذا تركوا الفداء يدفع بالجناية وتبطل بالوصية إلا أن يؤدي العبد من غير ما اكتسبه بأن يقول ذلك الدرهم ديناً على العبد يطالب به إذا اعتق .
And if he (the master) frees him while knowing of it, he is liable for the compensatory payment (arsh) like selling him, and like suspending his emancipation on killing so-and-so or shooting him or wounding him – if a slave does that. And if he said to his two slaves, each worth one thousand, 'one of you is free', then one of them killed a person accidentally, then the master died before specifying, while he was aware of the crime – then each of them becomes half-free, and must work to earn half his value. And upon the master is the value of the criminal slave, which is taken from all his estate. He does not become a chooser for ransom by death without specifying one of the two slaves. And in Al-Tajrid: If the usurped slave kills someone in the hand of the usurper and dies, and he had earlier committed crimes before the usurpation, then the value goes to the people of the crimes, and there is no option for the master in that. And the confession of an authorized or interdicted slave to a crime is not valid, nor does he have to work after emancipation. And if he confesses after emancipation that he had committed a crime during slavery, nothing is binding upon him. And if a slave kills a person accidentally, then the hand of the slave is cut off, then he kills another accidentally, then the blood money for his hand goes to the first victims' guardians, then the slave pays it, and it is shared between the guardians of the two crimes. And if the master and the victim's guardian dispute – the master claiming that the killing happened before the crime, and the victim's guardian claiming it was after – then the word is that of the guardian. And if he inflicted a head wound (mudihah) on someone, and the slave's value is one thousand, then he killed another, and his value is two thousand, then the master pays between them on twenty-one shares: for the head wound victim one share, and for the victim's family twenty shares. Similarly, if the killing was before the wound, and any increase or decrease occurs, it is according to the partnership. And in Al-Uyun: If he willed the emancipation of his slave, and then the slave committed a crime, and his master directed them (to something?), and the heirs after the testator's death said 'we will not ransom', then they (the heirs) have (the option?). If they abandon the ransom, the slave is handed over for the crime, and the will becomes void, unless the slave pays from something other than what he earned – by saying 'that dirham is a debt upon the slave, to be claimed when he is freed'.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage begins with the matn phrase about freeing a slave knowingly after a crime, which will be explained in the next lesson. It then presents several distinct cases: (1) If a master says to two equally-valued slaves 'one of you is free', and one kills accidentally, then the master dies before specifying, knowing of the crime; each slave becomes half-free and must seek half his value; the master's estate pays the value. (2) A usurped slave who dies in the usurper's hand: his value goes to his earlier crime victims; no option for the master. (3) A slave's confession of crime during slavery is invalid whether he is authorized or interdicted; no work after emancipation. (4) A slave kills accidentally, then loses a hand, then kills again; the hand's blood money goes to the first victims, then the slave pays it, and it is shared among both sets of victims. (5) If master and victim differ on sequence, the victim's word is taken. (6) A slave inflicts a head wound (mudihah) and then kills another; the master pays in shares: 1/21 for the wound, 20/21 for the killing. (7) If a master wills emancipation, and the slave later commits a crime, and the heirs refuse to ransom, the slave is handed over and the will voids, unless the slave can pay from other means (e.g., a debt he owes).

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injuries to body parts or wounds; here the liability for freeing a slave with knowledge.
موضحة (mudihah): A head wound that exposes the bone; a specific category in diyat with a fixed arsh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave inflicts a mudihah (head wound) and then kills another person, the master pays in how many shares?
A) 10 shares: 1 for wound, 9 for killing
B) 21 shares: 1 for wound, 20 for killing
C) 20 shares: 10 each
D) None; master has option

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rules on slave crimes emphasize justice for victims while considering the master's knowledge and actions, and they provide specific calculations for multiple crimes.

intermediate
10 min
Freeing a Slave Knowingly After a Crime: The Ruling and Its Basis
Commentary explaining liability for ransom upon freeing a slave after a crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a master who frees a slave knowingly after a crime becomes liable for ransom, and distinguish this from mere acknowledgment (iqrar).

Core Idea
Freeing a slave after a crime constitutes a choice for ransom because it prevents handover; transfers impose liability, iqrar does not.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling in Hanafi diyat: if a master frees his slave after learning that the slave committed a crime, he becomes liable for the ransom (arsh). This is because the master's act of freeing prevents him from handing over the slave to the victim's guardian. The commentator explains that the same applies to sale, gift, tadbir, and istilad, but not to mere acknowledgment (iqrar), which does not transfer ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a master freeing his slave after learning the slave committed a crime. The ruling is that the master becomes liable for the ransom (arsh). This is because the master is now considered to have chosen ransom over handing over the slave.
The key phrase in the matn is 'ولو عالماً بها لزمه الارش' (and if he is aware of it, he is liable for arsh).
2
Understand the underlying reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentator states: 'لأن الإعتاق يمنع من الدفع فالإقدام عليه اختيار' (because emancipation prevents handover, so proceeding upon it is a choice). By freeing the slave, the master makes it impossible to hand him over, thus effectively choosing to pay ransom instead.
This reasoning is the core proof; it is a logical inference (talil) based on the legal effect of emancipation.
3
Compare with similar acts that transfer ownership
The commentator lists sale, gift, tadbir (declaring freedom after death), and istilad (master's child by slave woman) as analogous acts. Each of these removes ownership from the master or transfers it to another, thereby preventing handover and imposing ransom liability.
The phrase 'كبيعه' (like selling him) indicates qiyas (analogy) is used to extend the ruling to other acts.
4
Distinguish acknowledgment (iqrar)
Iqrar (acknowledging the slave belongs to another) does not transfer ownership; it merely declares a possible pre-existing right. Therefore, it does not prevent the master from handing over the slave, and the master does not become liable for ransom unless he proves the slave is not his.
The commentator notes that in iqrar, the acknowledged person is addressed to hand over or ransom, and the master is not automatically liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال عمي بعد ذلك وهو للإنسان أدّ عني در هماً ففعل يصح ويصير رحمه الله : (ولو) عالماً بها لزمه الارش كبيعه وتعليق عتقه بقتل فلان ورميه وشجه إن فعل ذلك) يعني أعتق عبده عالماً بالجناية صار مختاراً للفداء بهذا العتق لأن الإعتاق يمنع من الدفع فالإقدام عليه اختيار فإذا أعتقه وهو يعلم بالجناية صار مختاراً للفداء لما قلنا المراد بقوله «كبيعه» يعني لو باعه عالماً بالجناية وعلى هذين الوجهين الهبة والتدبير والاستيلاد لأن كل واحد منهما يمنع من الدفع لزوال الملك والتمليك به بخلاف الإقرار لغيره بالعبد الجاني على رواية الأصل لأنه لا يسقط به حق ولي الجناية فإن المقر له يخاطب بالدفع إليه وليس فيه نقل الملك لأن الإقرار ليس بتمليك من جهة المقر وإنما إظهار الحق فيحتمل أن يكون صادقاً بذلك، فإذا لم يصر مختاراً لا يلزمه الفداء وتندفع الخصومة عنه إن أقام بينة أنه للمقر له، وإن دفعه كان المقر له إما أن تفديه أو تدفعه فإن فداه صار متطوعاً بالفداء حتى لا يرجع به على المقر له إذا حضر وصدقه أنه ،له وإن دفعه كان المقر له بالخيار إذا حضر إن شاء أجاز دفعه، وإن شاء فداه ولا فرق في هذا المعنى بين أن تكون الجناية في النفس أو في الأطراف لأن الكل موجب للفداء فلا يختلف وكذا لا فرق في البيع بين أن يكون بتاً أن يكون فيه خيار المشتري لأن الكل يزيل الملك بخلاف ما إذا كان الخيار للبائع ثم نقضه أو العرض على البيع لأن الملك لم يزل به ولا يقال المشتري بالخيار إذا باع بشرط الخيار مختاراً للإجارة به فوجب هنا أن يكون مختاراً للفداء لأنا نقول : لو لم يكن المشتري وبين له يصير
My uncle said after that: And it is for a person; 'pay for me a dirham' and he did, it is valid and becomes. May Allah have mercy on him: (And if he is aware) of it, he is liable for the arsh, like selling him, and like suspending his emancipation on killing so-and-so or shooting him or wounding him if a slave does that. Meaning: if he freed his slave while aware of the crime, he becomes a chooser for ransom by this emancipation, because emancipation prevents handing over (daf'), so proceeding upon it is a choice. So if he freed him while knowing of the crime, he becomes a chooser for ransom, as we said. The intended meaning of his statement 'like selling him' is: if he sold him while aware of the crime. And in these two ways: gift, tadbir, and istilad, because each of them prevents handing over due to the transfer of ownership and making it owned. Unlike the acknowledgement (iqrar) of the criminal slave to someone else according to the original narration, because it does not nullify the right of the guardian of the crime. For the person to whom he is acknowledged is addressed with handing over to him, and there is no transfer of ownership in it, because acknowledgement is not a transfer from the acknowledging person, but rather a manifestation of a right, so it is possible that he is truthful in that. So if he does not become a chooser, the ransom is not required of him, and the dispute is repelled from him if he establishes evidence that it is for the acknowledged person. And if he hands him over, then the acknowledged person either ransoms him or hands him over. If he ransoms him, he becomes a volunteer in ransom, so he does not recourse against the acknowledger when he appears and affirms that it is his. And if he hands him over, the acknowledged person has the option when he appears: if he wishes he may ratify the handover, and if he wishes he may ransom him. And there is no difference in this meaning between whether the crime is against the person or against limbs, because all necessitate ransom, so it does not differ. Similarly, there is no difference in sale between whether it is final or includes an option for the buyer, because all remove ownership. Unlike if the option is for the seller and then he rescinds, or an offer for sale, because ownership is not removed by it. Nor is it said that the buyer with an option, if he sells with a condition of option, is a chooser for leasing? So here he must be a chooser for ransom; because we say: if the buyer were not and it becomes clear for him.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This is the sharh (commentary) by the author's uncle (presumably al-Kashifi or similar) explaining the matn phrase 'ولو عالماً بها لزمه الارش كبيعه وتعليق عتقه' (And if he frees him while knowing of it, he is liable for arsh, like selling him...). The commentator explains that freeing a slave after a crime constitutes a choice of ransom (mukhtar al-fida') because emancipation prevents the master from handing over the slave (daf'). Thus, proceeding to free him is an exercise of choice, making the master liable for ransom. The same applies to selling, gifting, tadbir (declaring a slave free after death), and istilad (the master's child by the slave woman), because all these transfers of ownership prevent handing over. However, this is not the case with iqrar (acknowledging that the slave belongs to someone else), because iqrar does not transfer ownership; it merely declares a possible pre-existing right. If the master merely acknowledges the slave to another, he does not become a chooser for ransom, and the dispute is deflected if he can prove ownership. Then the acknowledged person has the option to ransom or hand over. The commentator also notes that the rule applies regardless of whether the crime is against life or limbs, and in sale whether the sale is final or with an option for the buyer (since ownership transfers), but not if the option is for the seller or if it is merely an offer, since ownership does not transfer.

Key Terms
مختار الفداء (mukhtar al-fida'): One who chooses to pay ransom; the master who performs an act that prevents handover becomes liable for ransom.
تدبير (tadbir): Master's declaration that a slave will be free after his death; it prevents handover of the slave for a crime.
استيلاد (istilad): Making a slave woman the mother of a free child; she becomes umm walad and cannot be sold or handed over.
ارش (arsh): Ransom or compensation paid for injuries or crimes committed by a slave.
دفع (daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does freeing a slave after a crime make the master a 'chooser for ransom'?
A) Because the master gains reward for freeing
B) Because freeing prevents handover, so the master has effectively chosen ransom
C) Because the slave becomes free and thus cannot be handed over
D) Because the crime is forgiven

Key Takeaway
A master who frees, sells, gives, or declares tadbir/istilad for a slave after a crime becomes liable for ransom because these acts prevent handover; iqrar does not impose liability.

Ambiguous Manumission, Manslaughter, and the Master's Death
Ruling on ambiguous manumission, manslaughter, and master's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for two slaves of equal value when the master says 'one of you is free' and then one commits manslaughter, and the master dies without specifying, and explain the obligations of the slaves and the estate.

Core Idea
Ambiguous manumission plus master's death makes both slaves half-free; estate pays the criminal's value.

This lesson presents a classic Hanafi case that combines issues of ambiguous manumission (ta‘yīn al-mubham), manslaughter (qatl khata'), and the timing of the master's death. The ruling is a straightforward application of the principle that when a master dies without specifying which slave is free, the ambiguity is resolved by granting half-freedom to each. Additionally, the master's knowledge of the crime at death means the estate, not the victim's family, bears the diyat of the criminal slave.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Scenario
The master has two slaves of equal value. He says 'one of you is free' but does not specify which. Before he clarifies, one slave commits manslaughter. The master dies while knowing of the crime but still without specifying.
The key factors: equal value, ambiguous statement, manslaughter, master dies aware.
2
Identify the Status of the Slaves
Since the master died without bayān, each slave becomes half-free (nisf al-‘itq). This is because the statement applied to both, and death prevented clarification.
They are now muba'dh (partially free).
3
Determine the Obligation of the Slaves
Each half-free slave must perform sa‘y (labor to earn) the remaining half of his value to complete his freedom.
Sa‘y is mandatory unless the master (or his heirs) waive it, but here the master is dead.
4
Determine Liability for the Manslaughter
The value of the criminal slave (the jānī) must be paid from the master's estate to the victim's guardians. The estate pays this diyat because the master was aware and did not choose to surrender the slave before death.
The master does not become a 'chooser for ransom' by dying without specification; the option is not automatically exercised.
5
Assemble the Complete Ruling
Combine the parts: both slaves are half-free, must earn half value, and the estate is liable for the criminal's value.
This is the muftā bihī position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لعبديه وقيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر : ثم قتل أحدهما إنساناً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان وهو عالم بالجناية عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه ويسعى في نصف قيمته ويجب على المولى قيمة العبد الجاني فيستوفي من جميع تركته ولا يصير مختاراً للفداء بالموت من غير بيان واحد من العبدين.
If he says to his two slaves, each worth one thousand: 'One of you is free,' then one of them kills a person by mistake, then the master dies before clarification while knowing of the crime: from each of them half is freed, and he must labor to earn half his value, and the value of the criminal slave is obligatory upon the master, so it is collected from all of his estate, and he does not become a chooser for ransom by death without specifying one of the two slaves.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It addresses a scenario where a master has two slaves of equal value, and he declares that one of them is free without specification. Before he clarifies which one, one slave commits manslaughter (qatl khata'). The master dies while knowing of the crime but still without designating the freed slave. The ruling: each slave becomes half free (because the master's statement 'one of you is free' applies to both equally, and his death prevents clarification, so each gets half). The half-free slaves must work (sa'y) to earn half of their value to pay for their freedom. Additionally, the master's estate is liable for the value of the slave who committed the crime (the jani), because the master was aware and died, and the obligation to ransom (fida) is not triggered by death without specification. The estate pays this value to the victim's guardians. The master does not become a 'chooser for ransom' merely by dying without specifying; the option is not considered exercised.

Key Terms
سعى (sa‘y): Labor to earn money for freedom, required of a partially free slave (muba'dh) to pay for the remaining portion of his value.
بيان (bayān): Clarification or specification of which slave is intended to be free.
جناية (jināyah): Crime or offense, here specifically manslaughter (qatl khata').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a master says 'one of you is free' to two slaves of equal value, one commits manslaughter, and the master dies aware without specifying, how is each slave's freedom determined?
A) Both become fully free because the master's death prevents contradiction.
B) Each becomes half-free and must work to earn the remaining half of his value.
C) Only the slave who did not commit the crime becomes free.
D) Neither becomes free because the master died without declaring.

Key Takeaway
When a master ambiguously frees one of two slaves and dies without specifying, both become half-free and must work for the remainder, and the estate is liable for any diyat owed by the criminal slave if the master was aware.

Ruling on a Stolen Slave Killed While with Thief and Had Prior Crimes
Ruling on the distribution of value of a stolen slave who had prior crimes and is killed while with the thief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the distribution of a stolen slave's value when the slave had prior crimes and is killed while with the usurper, and identify the master's lack of option.

Core Idea
When a stolen slave with prior crimes is killed, the victims of those crimes take precedence over the master.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh concerning the liability of a stolen slave's value. The case involves a slave who was wrongfully taken (ghasb) and then killed while in the usurper's possession, but who had committed crimes before the usurpation. The text from al-Tajrīd clarifies that the slave's value belongs to the prior crime victims, and the master has no choice in the matter. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the priorities of claims in tort law (jināyāt) when property and criminal liability intersect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal situation
Recognize the key elements: a slave is wrongfully taken (ghasb), is killed while with the usurper, and had committed crimes before the usurpation.
The slave's death occurs while in the usurper's hand.
2
State the ruling
The value (qīmah) of the slave belongs to the owners of the prior crimes (aṣḥāb al-jināyāt).
The value is assessed at the time of death, as per standard Hanafi rules.
3
Note the master's lack of option
The master has no choice (lā khiyār) in this distribution; he cannot retain the value or choose to pay instead.
This contrasts with other situations where a master may have an option to ransom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجريد ولو قتل العبد المغصوب في يد الغاصب ومات وقد كان جنى قبل الغصب جنايات فالقيمة لأصحاب الجنايات ولا خيار للمولى في ذلك
And in al-Tajrid: If a stolen slave is killed while in the hand of the usurper and dies, and he had committed crimes before the usurpation, the value belongs to the owners of the crimes, and the master has no option in that.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from al-Tajrid. It deals with a slave who was wrongfully taken (ghasb) and then killed while in the hands of the ghāṣib (usurper). Prior to the usurpation, the slave had committed multiple crimes (jinaayat). The ruling: the value of the slave (qīmah) is given to the victims of those prior crimes (aṣḥāb al-jināyāt). The master has no choice (lā khiyār) in this distribution; he cannot ransom the slave or choose to pay instead, because the slave is already dead and the prior crimes' rights take precedence.

Key Terms
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation, wrongful taking of property that is owned by another.
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary value of the slave, which is given to the crime victims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The usurper.
B) The master.
C) The prior crime victims.
D) The master and victims equally.

Key Takeaway
The value of a stolen slave killed while with the usurper belongs to the prior crime victims, and the master has no say in the distribution.

Ruling on a Slave's Confession to a Crime
Ruling that a slave's confession to a crime is not valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave's confession to a crime is invalid and explain that emancipation does not retroactively impose liability.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to a crime never binds him, even after freedom.

In Hanafi fiqh, a slave lacks full legal capacity. This lesson examines a specific ruling on confessions (iqrār) in criminal matters. You will learn that neither a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) nor a mahjūr (interdicted) slave's confession is accepted, and even after emancipation, a confession regarding a past crime has no effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave's confession (iqrār) to a crime (jināyah). The ruling is that it is not valid (lā yajūz).
This applies to both types of slaves mentioned.
2
Distinguish the two categories of slaves
The text explicitly mentions the ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) and the mahjūr 'alayhi (interdicted). Both are equal in this ruling.
The distinction between them in trade matters does not affect criminal confession.
3
Consequence during slavery
Because the confession is invalid, the slave does not 'labor' (lā yas'ā) to pay compensation. The master is not liable.
"Sa'y" here refers to working to pay off the value of the crime.
4
Consequence after emancipation
Even if the slave is freed and later confesses that he committed a crime while enslaved, nothing is binding upon him.
Emancipation does not retroactively validate the confession or create liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز إقرار العبد المأذون والمحجور عليه بالجناية ولا يسعى بعد العتق، ولو أقر بعد العتق أنه كان جنى في حالة الرق لم يلزمه شيء
The confession of the permitted slave (ma'dhūn) and the interdicted slave (mahjūr 'alayhi) regarding a crime is not valid, and he does not labor after emancipation. If he confesses after emancipation that he had committed a crime during slavery, nothing is binding upon him.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This ruling statement addresses the invalidity of a slave's confession to a crime. Whether the slave is ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) or mahjūr 'alayhi (under interdiction), his confession to a crime (jināyah) is not accepted. Consequently, after the slave is emancipated, he does not have to work (lā yas'ā) to pay the value because the confession is void. Similarly, if after emancipation he confesses that he committed a crime while still a slave, he is not obligated to pay anything.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade with third parties.
محجور عليه (maḥjūr 'alayhi): A slave under interdiction, not permitted to trade or make binding statements regarding financial obligations.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must pay the value or be surrendered.
B) The confession is valid if the slave is permitted to trade.
C) The confession is invalid, and after emancipation he is not liable.
D) Only the interdicted slave's confession is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to a crime is never valid, and emancipation does not make him liable for a past crime.

Prioritization of Claims When a Slave Commits Multiple Injuries
Ruling on a slave who commits two separate crimes (killing then wounding) and how the proceeds of a later injury are distributed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequence of compensation distribution when a slave commits multiple offenses and identify which guardians receive priority.

Core Idea
The first crime's guardians get paid first from the slave's available arsh.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi diyat law: a slave commits two manslaughters, and between them his hand is severed. The arsh (blood money) for the hand becomes a resource. How should it be distributed among the victims' guardians? The ruling establishes a clear priority rule based on the order of crimes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of events
The slave first commits qatl khata' (accidental killing) against victim A. Then the slave's hand is cut off (for reasons not specified). Then he commits qatl khata' against victim B.
The hand severing could be due to retaliation, accident, or external cause; the ruling treats it as a separate incident.
2
Determine the available asset
The asset in question is the arsh (monetary compensation) for the severed hand. This arsh is a sum owed to the slave (or his master) but is to be paid out for the crimes.
In Hanafi law, arsh is the prescribed amount for injuries.
3
Apply the priority rule
The arsh of the hand is first given to the guardians of the first crime (victim A). The second crime's guardians have no claim on this initial payment.
The text explicitly says: 'fa-arsh yadihi yusallim li-awliya' al-jinayah al-ula' – so the arsh is handed over to the first guardians.
4
Distribute the remainder
After the first payment, the slave pays this same amount (yadfa'uhu al-'abd) so that it becomes divided between the guardians of both crimes.
The slave pays the arsh amount out of his own resources (or via the master's liability), and that payment is shared between both sets of guardians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قتل العبد قتيلاً خطأ ثم قطعت يد العبد ثم آخر خطأ فأرش يده يسلم لأولياء الجناية الأولى ثم يدفعه العبد فيكون بين ولي الجنايتين
If the slave kills a person by mistake, then the slave's hand is cut off, then [he kills] another by mistake, then the blood money (arsh) of his hand is handed over to the guardians of the first crime, then the slave pays it, so it becomes [divided] between the guardians of the two crimes.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This ruling statement deals with a sequence of events: a slave commits manslaughter (qatl khata'). Later, the slave's hand is cut off (maybe as retaliation or accident). Then the slave commits another manslaughter. The question is how to distribute the blood money (arsh) for the hand. The ruling: the arsh of the hand is first given to the guardians of the first crime (awliyā' al-jināyah al-'ūlā). Then the slave pays this amount (yadfa'uhu al-'abd) so it is shared between the two sets of guardians (fayakūnu bayna walī al-jināyatayn). So the first crime gets paid first from the slave's asset (the hand's arsh), and then the remaining is divided between both.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Monetary compensation for wounds or injuries, especially for loss of a limb.
ولي الجناية (walī al-jināyah): The legal guardian of the victim, who has the right to claim compensation or retaliate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits manslaughter, then his hand is cut off, then he commits another manslaughter, how is the blood money for the hand distributed?
A) It is split equally between both sets of guardians.
B) It goes entirely to the second crime's guardians.
C) It is first given to the first crime's guardians, then shared between both.
D) It goes to the slave's master.

Key Takeaway
In cases of multiple claims on a slave's assets, the first crime's guardians are paid first from the slave's available compensation.

Dispute on Timing of Killing: Guardian's Word Accepted
Ruling on who is believed in a sequence dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the guardian's word is taken in a dispute over the timing of a killing relative to a crime, and apply it to determine liability.

Core Idea
In disputes over sequence, the guardian's word is taken—the claimant's representative is believed.

This lesson presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school on a procedural issue in diyat and qada. When the master of a slave and the victim's guardian disagree about whether a killing occurred before or after the slave's crime, the guardian's claim is given precedence. Understanding this rule clarifies the burden of proof in liability cases involving slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Determine who the master (mawlā) and the guardian of the crime (walī al-jināyah) are in the scenario.
2
Recognize each party's claim
Note that the master claims the killing occurred before the crime, while the guardian claims it occurred after.
3
State the ruling
The ruling is that the guardian's statement is taken (al-qawl qawl al-walī).
This is a default rule; the master may disprove only with evidence.
4
Understand the reasoning
The guardian is the claimant seeking compensation; the master is the defendant. In Hanafi procedure, the claimant's word is accepted until the defendant provides evidence.
5
Apply to a similar scenario
Given a new scenario with the same structure, repeat the rule: the guardian's word is taken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلف المولى وولي الجناية فادعى المولى أن قتل كان قبل الجناية وادعى ولي الجناية أنه كان بعدها فالقول قول الولي
If the master and the guardian of the crime disagree, and the master claims that the killing was before the crime, and the guardian of the crime claims that it was after, then the word is that of the guardian.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This ruling statement addresses a dispute between the master (mawlā) and the victim's guardian (walī al-jināyah) about the sequence of events. The master claims that a killing occurred before the slave's crime, which might affect liability. The guardian claims it occurred after. The ruling: the guardian's statement is taken (al-qawl qawl al-walī). This is because the guardian is the one seeking compensation, and the master is the defendant; in disputes about the cause of death, the claim of the plaintiff's representative is given precedence unless the defendant proves otherwise.

Key Terms
قول الولي (qawl al-walī): The statement of the guardian is accepted as the default proof in this dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master's
B) The guardian's
C) Both equally
D) It depends on other evidence

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over the sequence of a killing and a crime, the Hanafi school takes the guardian's word as default.

Distribution of Slave's Value for Multiple Jinaaya
Ruling on distribution of slave's value for two jinaaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the proportional distribution of a slave's value when the slave commits a mudihah and a killing, applying the ratio derived from the arsh of each crime.

Core Idea
When a slave commits multiple crimes, compensation is proportional to the arsh of each, not equal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific scenario: a slave commits a mudihah (head wound exposing bone) when his value is 1000, and later commits a killing when his value is 2000. The ruling shows how the master must compensate both victims proportionally, based on the fixed compensation (arsh) for each crime. Understanding this ratio is essential for correctly handling similar cases in diyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two crimes and their arsh
Recognize that the crimes are a mudihah (wound) and a killing. The arsh for mudihah is 1/20 of full diyah, and for killing it is full diyah. In the given values, the ratio is 1:20.
The slave's value at the time of each crime is used as the base for compensation.
2
Determine the proportional shares
Since the arsh ratio is 1:20, total shares = 21. The wound victim gets 1/21, the killing victim gets 20/21 of the slave's value as paid by the master.
The values given (1000 and 2000) are consistent with this ratio: the wound arsh is 500 (if diyah = 10000), but here the slave's value is the ceiling.
3
Apply the ruling regardless of order
The same proportion applies even if the killing occurred before the wound. The order does not affect the ratio.
This is explicitly stated in the text.
4
Handle changes in slave value after crimes
Any increase or decrease in the slave's value after the crimes is shared between the two victims in the same proportion (1:20).
This ensures fairness if the slave's value fluctuates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شج انساناً موضحة وقيمته ألف ثم قال قتل آخر وقيمته ألفان فإن المولى يدفع بينهما على أحد وعشرين سهماً لصاحب الموضحة سهم، وعشرون لولي القتيل. وكذلك لو كان القتل قبل الشجة وما يحدث من الزيادة والنقصان فهو على الشركة
If the slave wounds a person with a mudihah (a skull fracture exposing bone) and his value is one thousand, then he kills another and his value is two thousand, then the master pays between them in twenty-one shares: for the owner of the mudihah one share, and twenty for the guardian of the killed. Similarly if the killing was before the wound, and any increase or decrease that occurs is on the partnership.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This ruling statement deals with a slave who commits two different crimes: a wound (shajjah mudihah) and a killing. The slave's value is stated for each crime: 1000 for the wound, 2000 for the killing. The master must pay compensation to both victims proportionally. The ratio is 1/21 for the wound and 20/21 for the killing. This ratio is derived from the arsh (compensation) for mudihah (which is 1/20 of diyah, i.e., 50 dinars or 500 dirhams, but here the values are proportional) and the full diyah for the killing (1000 dinars). The master divides the slave's value in that ratio. The same applies even if the killing occurred before the wound. Any increase or decrease in the slave's value after the crimes is shared proportionally.

Key Terms
موضحة (mūḍiḥah): A head wound that exposes the white bone (skull fracture).
شجة (shajjah): A head wound, specifically a wound on the head or face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 1/20 to the wound victim, 19/20 to the killing victim.
C) 1/21 to the wound victim, 20/21 to the killing victim.
D) All to the killing victim because killing is more severe.

Key Takeaway
When a slave commits a mudihah and a killing, the master pays compensation in a fixed 1:20 ratio (1/21 for wound, 20/21 for killing), regardless of order or subsequent value changes.
