Ruling on a Slave Bequeathed Freedom Who Commits a Crime
Ruling when a slave bequeathed freedom commits a crime and heirs refuse ransom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when heirs refuse to ransom a bequeathed slave who committed a crime, and identify the exception where the bequest remains valid.

Core Idea
If heirs refuse ransom, the bequest is void and the slave is surrendered, unless the slave pays from his own earnings.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from al-‘Uyūn: a master bequeaths freedom to his slave, but the slave commits a crime before the master’s death. After the master dies, the heirs must decide whether to ransom the slave. If they refuse, the slave is surrendered and the bequest is void—unless the slave himself can pay the compensation from his own earnings. This ruling highlights the interplay between wills, debts, and criminal liability in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial situation
A master makes a will (wasiyyah) to free his slave after death. Before the master dies, the slave commits a crime (jinaaya) that requires compensation.
2
Determine the heirs' option
After the master's death, the heirs have the choice to ransom the slave (pay the compensation) or surrender him to the victim's guardians.
3
State the ruling upon refusal
If the heirs refuse to ransom, the slave is surrendered, and the bequest of freedom becomes void (tabṭal al-waṣiyyah).
The bequest is void because the victim's right takes precedence.
4
Recognize the exception
The bequest remains valid if the slave pays the compensation from his own earnings (kash). The amount is treated as a debt against the slave, to be collected after he is freed.
The slave's earnings must be from lawful means acquired during slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي العيون: إذا أوصى بعتق عبد له فجنى العبد جناية أرشادهم فقالت الورثة بعد موت الموصى لا نفدي فلهم . فإذا تركوا الفداء يدفع بالجناية وتبطل بالوصية إلا أن يؤدي العبد من غير ما اكتسبه بأن يقول ذلك الدرهم ديناً على العبد يطالب به إذا اعتق
And in al-‘Uyūn: If a master bequeaths freedom to his slave, then the slave commits a crime for which they are aggrieved, and the heirs after the testator's death say 'we will not ransom', then for them (the right to surrender). So if they abandon the ransom, the slave is surrendered for the crime, and the bequest becomes void, unless the slave pays from what he has earned himself, by saying that dirham as a debt upon the slave, to be claimed when he is freed.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This ruling statement is from the book al-‘Uyūn. It deals with a master who makes a will (wasiyyah) to free a slave after his death. Before the master dies, the slave commits a crime (jinaaya) that requires compensation. After the testator's death, the heirs refuse to ransom the slave (i.e., they do not pay the value to keep the slave). The ruling: the heirs have the right to surrender the slave to the victim's guardians. If they surrender the slave (i.e., abandon the ransom), the slave is handed over for the crime, and the bequest of freedom becomes void (tabṭal al-waṣiyyah). However, there is an exception: if the slave himself pays the compensation from his own earnings (kash) that he acquired during slavery, then the bequest can be fulfilled. The phrase 'that dirham as a debt upon the slave' refers to the possibility that the slave may earn money and the heirs can claim it as a debt after his freedom.

Key Terms
وصية (waṣiyyah): A bequest, a declaration that something (e.g., freedom of a slave) should take effect after death.
فداء (fidā'): Ransom, payment to keep the slave from being surrendered for his crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The bequest stands and the heirs must pay.
B) The bequest is void and the slave is surrendered.
C) The slave goes free after paying from his earnings.
D) The bequest is reduced by the value of the crime.

Key Takeaway
The heirs' refusal to ransom a bequeathed slave who committed a crime voids the bequest, unless the slave pays from his own earnings.

Master's Liability upon Freeing a Slave: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains khiyar and iqrar in slave's crime with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a master becomes liable for the ransom when freeing a slave after a crime, distinguish between crimes requiring financial compensation and retaliation, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the slave's report.

Core Idea
A master becomes liable for the ransom only when the crime requires financial compensation; the dispute over the slave's report turns on the reliability of the informant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal consequences when a master frees his slave after the slave commits a crime. The ruling depends critically on whether the crime is punishable by financial compensation or by retaliation. Additionally, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises when the master frees the slave based on the slave's own report: does he become liable?

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The master suspends the slave's freedom on the occurrence of a crime. The ruling on his liability depends on the type of crime.
The act is taw'een (freeing) after a crime.
2
Distinguish between financial and retaliatory crimes
If the crime requires qisas (retaliation), the master is not liable because the slave and free person are equal in qisas. If the crime requires financial compensation (diyah), the master may become liable.
Examples: khat' (mistake) and shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional) require financial liability.
3
Apply the condition of expiation
For killing that requires kaffarah (expiation), like direct killing, the master becomes a chooser (mukhtar). For killing by causation (tasabbub), no liability as it is not direct killing.
Direct killing: e.g., hitting with a sword. Causation: e.g., digging a well.
4
Analyze the dispute on the slave's report
If the slave informs the master of the crime and the master frees him, then according to Abu Hanifa, the master is not liable unless a free just man informs him. According to the Sahibayn, he is liable even if the informant is a sinner or disbeliever.
This dispute was previously mentioned in Agency and Preemption.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later fatwa practice follows the Sahibayn's position, so the master is liable upon freeing, regardless of the informant's reliability.
This ensures the victim's guardian does not lose compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه. يصير دلالة الاختيار هذا إذا علقه بجناية توجب المال كالخطأ وشبه العمد، وإن علقه بجناية توجب القصاص بأن قال له إن ضربته بالسيف فأنت حر فلا يجب على المولى شيء بالاتفاق لأنه لا فرق بين العبد والحر في القصاص فلم يكن المولى مفوتاً حق ولي الجناية بالعتق . وبكل قتل تجب الكفارة فيه يصير المولى مختاراً كالقتل بالمباشرة، وإن لم تجب الكفارة فيه لا مختاراً وهو القتل تسبباً كما لو وقع في بئر حفرها المولى لأن القتل تسبباً ليس بقتل حقيقة لأن القتل فعل في الحر ويؤثر في إزهاق الروح والتسبب ليس بفعل في الحر لأنه لم يوصل إلا إلى الدية ولهذا لم يجب القصاص ولا يحرم الإرث فلم يصر مستملكاً للعبد وبالقتل مباشرة صار مستدعياً للعمد في كل موضع صار متلفاً للعبد يضمن الفداء لما بينا. ولو أخبره عبده بالجناية فاعتقه المولى وقال لم أصدقه فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا يضمن ما لم يخبره رجل حر عدل، وعندهما يضمن الدية وإن كان المخبر فاسقاً أو كافراً وقد مرت في الوكالة والشفعة ولو به لغيره فهو على قسمين : إما أن أقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو على عكسه، وكل قسم لا يخلوا إما أن كان الملك في العبد معروفاً للمقر أو كان مجهولاً. أما القسم الأول لو أقر بالجناية ثم بالملك لغيره والملك في العبد معروف للمقر ، فإن صدقة المقر له في الملك والجناية جميعاً يقال للمقر له ادفع العبد أو فده لأنه صح الإقرار لأن حق المجني عليه لا يمنع نفوذ تصرف المولى لأن حقه في الدافع أو الفداء وهو باق بعد الاقرار والثابت بالإقرار كالثابت بالبينة العادلة، ومتى ظهر الملك للمقر له بالإقرار ظهر أن الجناية صدرت من ملكه وإن كان كذبه فيها لا يكون المقر مختاراً للفداء خلافاً لزفر ; له أن صحة الإقرار لا تتوقف على تصديق المقر له، ولهذا لو مات المقر قبل التصديق يصير المقر به ميراثاً لورثته فقد زال العبد عن ملكه بنفس الإقرار وهو عالم بالجناية فيصير مختاراً. ولنا أن صحة الإقرار لا توجب على التصديق والبطلان يتوقف على التكذيب وإذا اتصل به التكذيب بطل من الأصل، فلو صدقه في الملك وكذبه في الجناية صار المقر مختاراً للفداء لأن الإقرار بالجناية على العبد صادف ملكه في العبد فصح، ثم إذا أقر بالملك لغيره وصدقه المقر له صار مزيلاً للعبد عن ملكه فصار كما لو باعه أو وهبه . وأما القسم الثاني لو أقر بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية إن صدقه فيهما فالخصم هو المقر له، وإن كذبه فيهما فالخصم. المقر، وإن صدقه في الملك وكذبه في الجناية هدرت الجناية لأنه لما صدقه المقر في الملك ظهر أن اقراره بجناية العبد صادق فلا يصح إقراره بالجناية متى كذبه المقر له فلم تثبت الجناية، وكذلك إن كان العبد مجهولاً لا يدري أنه للمقر أم لغيره فأقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية لأن الملك ثابت للمقر بظاهر اليد لا يستند إلى دليل والملك الثابت بظاهر اليد لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق واختيار الفداء فلم يصر مختاراً للفداء بخلاف ما لو كان الملك له معروفاً لأن ملكه ثابت مستند إلى دليل سوى ظاهر اليد فصلح حجة لاثبات ما لم يكن، ولو قال كنت بعته من فلان قبل الجناية وصدقه فلان يخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء لأنه ثبت الملك بتصادقهما .
Book of Diyat, Chapter on the Crime of a Slave and Crime against a Slave. The indication of choice becomes this: if he [the master] suspends [freedom] on a crime that requires financial liability like mistake and quasi-intentional, and if he suspends it on a crime that requires retaliation by saying to him: 'If you strike him with the sword, you are free', then nothing is due from the master by agreement because there is no difference between a slave and a free person in retaliation, so the master is not forfeiting the right of the victim's guardian by freeing. And with every killing that requires expiation, the master becomes a chooser, like direct killing; if expiation is not required, he is not a chooser, and it is killing by causation, like if he fell into a well dug by the master, because killing by causation is not actual killing in reality, because killing is an act upon a free person affecting the taking of life, and causation is not an act upon the free person because it only leads to blood money; hence retaliation is not required, and inheritance is not prevented, so he did not become an appropriator of the slave, and by direct killing he becomes a demander of the slave's value in every case where he destroys the slave, he guarantees the ransom as we stated. And if his slave informs him of the crime, and the master frees him and says: 'I did not believe him', then according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) he does not guarantee unless a free, just man informs him; according to the two [companions], he guarantees the blood money even if the informant is a sinner or disbeliever, and this has been mentioned in Agency and Preemption. And if he acknowledges for another, it is of two types: either he acknowledges the crime first and then ownership, or the opposite; and each type is not free from either the ownership in the slave being known to the acknowledger or being unknown. As for the first type: if he acknowledges the crime and then ownership for another, and the ownership in the slave is known to the acknowledger, and if the acknowledgee confirms him in ownership and crime, it is said to the acknowledgee: 'Hand over the slave or ransom him', because the acknowledgment is valid, because the right of the victim does not prevent the validity of the master's disposition, because his right is in the handing over or the ransom, and it remains after the acknowledgment, and what is established by acknowledgment is like what is established by just testimonial evidence. And whenever the ownership appears to the acknowledgee by the acknowledgment, it appears that the crime occurred from his property; and if he denies the crime, the acknowledger does not become a chooser for the ransom, contrary to Zufar; his proof is that the validity of the acknowledgment does not depend on the acknowledgee's confirmation, and for this reason if the acknowledger dies before confirmation, the acknowledged [slave] becomes an inheritance for his heirs, so the slave has left his ownership by the acknowledgment itself while he knows the crime, so he becomes a chooser. Our proof is that the validity of the acknowledgment does not necessitate confirmation, and nullification depends on denial; and when denial attaches to it, it becomes void from the beginning. So if he confirms him in ownership but denies the crime, the acknowledger becomes a chooser for the ransom because the acknowledgment of the crime against the slave meets his ownership in the slave, so it is valid; then when he acknowledges ownership for another and the acknowledgee confirms him, he becomes a remover of the slave from his ownership, so it is as if he sold or gifted him. As for the second type: if he acknowledges ownership first and then the crime; if he confirms him in both, the opponent is the acknowledgee; if he denies both, the opponent is the acknowledger; if he confirms him in ownership and denies the crime, the crime is rendered null because when the acknowledgee confirms him in ownership, it appears that his acknowledgment of the slave's crime is correct, so his acknowledgment of the crime becomes invalid when the acknowledgee denies it, so the crime is not established. Likewise if the slave is unknown, not known whether it belongs to the acknowledger or another, and he acknowledges the crime first and then ownership, or ownership first and then the crime, because ownership is established for the acknowledger by apparent possession not based on evidence, and ownership established by apparent possession is not suitable as a proof for entitlement and choice of ransom, so he does not become a chooser for the ransom, unlike if his ownership is known because his ownership is established based on evidence other than apparent possession, so it is suitable as a proof to establish what was not. And if he said: 'I sold him to so-and-so before the crime' and so-and-so confirms him, the buyer is given the choice between handing over and ransom because ownership is established by their mutual confirmation.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings regarding a master's liability when he frees his slave after the slave commits a crime. The author first distinguishes between crimes requiring financial compensation (such as khat' and shibh 'amd) and those requiring qisas (retaliation). If the master suspends the slave's freedom on a crime requiring qisas, he is not liable because the slave and free person are equal in qisas, and the master did not forfeit the victim's right by freeing. Then the passage discusses cases where the master frees the slave after being informed of the crime. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the master is not liable unless two just free men inform him; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), he is liable even if the informant is a sinner or disbeliever, as previously mentioned in the chapters on agency (wakalah) and pre-emption (shuf'ah). The passage then details the rules of acknowledgment (iqrar) when a person acknowledges a crime and then later acknowledges that the slave belongs to another, or vice versa. The key principle is that the validity of the acknowledgment depends on the ownership being known and the confirmation of the acknowledgee. If the slave's ownership is known to the acknowledger and the acknowledgee confirms both the ownership and the crime, the acknowledgee must either hand over the slave or ransom him. If the acknowledgee denies the crime, the master does not become liable for the ransom (khilafan li Zufar). However, if the acknowledgee confirms ownership but denies the crime, the master becomes liable for the ransom. If ownership is unknown, the master's acknowledgment does not make him liable for the ransom because apparent possession is not sufficient proof. Finally, if the master claims he sold the slave before the crime and the buyer confirms, the buyer has the option to hand over the slave or pay the ransom.

Key Terms
دلالة الاختيار (dalālāt al-ikhtiyār): The indication that the master has exercised his choice (to free the slave) and thus becomes liable for the ransom.
مختاراً (mukhtāran): The master who is deemed to have chosen to be liable for the ransom by freeing the slave after the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does the master become liable for the ransom?
A) When the crime requires qisas.
B) When the crime requires financial compensation like khat' or shibh 'amd.
C) Only when a free just man informs him of the crime.
D) In all cases where the master frees the slave.

Key Takeaway
The master becomes liable for the ransom only when the crime requires financial compensation, and the relied-upon view holds him liable regardless of the informant's reliability.

Acknowledgment (Iqrar) and Its Effect on Master's Liability in Slave Crimes
Explains khiyar and iqrar in slave's crime with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of acknowledgment (iqrar) to determine the master's liability when he acknowledges the crime and ownership in different orders, and identify the conditions under which the master becomes a chooser (mukhtar).

Core Idea
The master's acknowledgment of crime and ownership creates liability only if ownership is known and the acknowledgment is not contradicted by the acknowledgee's denial.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details complex scenarios where the master acknowledges a slave's crime and later acknowledges that the slave belongs to another, or vice versa. The rulings hinge on whether the slave's ownership is known (ma'ruf) or unknown (majhul), and whether the acknowledgee confirms or denies the acknowledgment. The text also addresses a dispute with Zufar regarding the effect of denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two orders of acknowledgment
The text divides cases into two types: the master acknowledges the crime first then ownership, or ownership first then crime.
Each type has subcases based on known or unknown ownership.
2
Determine the nature of ownership
If ownership of the slave is known (ma'ruf) to the acknowledger, the acknowledgment can establish liability. If unknown (majhul), the master does not become a chooser.
Known ownership means established by evidence beyond mere possession.
3
Apply the effect of confirmation or denial by the acknowledgee
If the acknowledgee confirms both ownership and crime, he must hand over the slave or pay ransom. If he denies the crime, the acknowledgment becomes void and the master does not become a chooser.
This differs from Zufar's view that denial does not affect validity.
4
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Zufar argued that the master becomes a chooser because the acknowledgment is valid without the acknowledgee's confirmation. The Hanafi majority respond that denial voids the acknowledgment from the beginning.
The dispute is about the nature of iqrar: is it binding unilaterally or requires acceptance?
5
Apply the rule for unknown ownership
If the slave's ownership is unknown (only apparent possession), the master's acknowledgment does not make him a chooser because apparent possession is not sufficient evidence for liability.
This contrasts with known ownership, which is based on solid evidence.

كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه. يصير دلالة الاختيار هذا إذا علقه بجناية توجب المال كالخطأ وشبه العمد، وإن علقه بجناية توجب القصاص بأن قال له إن ضربته بالسيف فأنت حر فلا يجب على المولى شيء بالاتفاق لأنه لا فرق بين العبد والحر في القصاص فلم يكن المولى مفوتاً حق ولي الجناية بالعتق . وبكل قتل تجب الكفارة فيه يصير المولى مختاراً كالقتل بالمباشرة، وإن لم تجب الكفارة فيه لا مختاراً وهو القتل تسبباً كما لو وقع في بئر حفرها المولى لأن القتل تسبباً ليس بقتل حقيقة لأن القتل فعل في الحر ويؤثر في إزهاق الروح والتسبب ليس بفعل في الحر لأنه لم يوصل إلا إلى الدية ولهذا لم يجب القصاص ولا يحرم الإرث فلم يصر مستملكاً للعبد وبالقتل مباشرة صار مستدعياً للعمد في كل موضع صار متلفاً للعبد يضمن الفداء لما بينا. ولو أخبره عبده بالجناية فاعتقه المولى وقال لم أصدقه فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا يضمن ما لم يخبره رجل حر عدل، وعندهما يضمن الدية وإن كان المخبر فاسقاً أو كافراً وقد مرت في الوكالة والشفعة ولو به لغيره فهو على قسمين : إما أن أقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو على عكسه، وكل قسم لا يخلوا إما أن كان الملك في العبد معروفاً للمقر أو كان مجهولاً. أما القسم الأول لو أقر بالجناية ثم بالملك لغيره والملك في العبد معروف للمقر ، فإن صدقة المقر له في الملك والجناية جميعاً يقال للمقر له ادفع العبد أو فده لأنه صح الإقرار لأن حق المجني عليه لا يمنع نفوذ تصرف المولى لأن حقه في الدافع أو الفداء وهو باق بعد الاقرار والثابت بالإقرار كالثابت بالبينة العادلة، ومتى ظهر الملك للمقر له بالإقرار ظهر أن الجناية صدرت من ملكه وإن كان كذبه فيها لا يكون المقر مختاراً للفداء خلافاً لزفر ; له أن صحة الإقرار لا تتوقف على تصديق المقر له، ولهذا لو مات المقر قبل التصديق يصير المقر به ميراثاً لورثته فقد زال العبد عن ملكه بنفس الإقرار وهو عالم بالجناية فيصير مختاراً. ولنا أن صحة الإقرار لا توجب على التصديق والبطلان يتوقف على التكذيب وإذا اتصل به التكذيب بطل من الأصل، فلو صدقه في الملك وكذبه في الجناية صار المقر مختاراً للفداء لأن الإقرار بالجناية على العبد صادف ملكه في العبد فصح، ثم إذا أقر بالملك لغيره وصدقه المقر له صار مزيلاً للعبد عن ملكه فصار كما لو باعه أو وهبه . وأما القسم الثاني لو أقر بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية إن صدقه فيهما فالخصم هو المقر له، وإن كذبه فيهما فالخصم. المقر، وإن صدقه في الملك وكذبه في الجناية هدرت الجناية لأنه لما صدقه المقر في الملك ظهر أن اقراره بجناية العبد صادق فلا يصح إقراره بالجناية متى كذبه المقر له فلم تثبت الجناية، وكذلك إن كان العبد مجهولاً لا يدري أنه للمقر أم لغيره فأقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية لأن الملك ثابت للمقر بظاهر اليد لا يستند إلى دليل والملك الثابت بظاهر اليد لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق واختيار الفداء فلم يصر مختاراً للفداء بخلاف ما لو كان الملك له معروفاً لأن ملكه ثابت مستند إلى دليل سوى ظاهر اليد فصلح حجة لاثبات ما لم يكن، ولو قال كنت بعته من فلان قبل الجناية وصدقه فلان يخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء لأنه ثبت الملك بتصادقهما .
Book of Diyat, Chapter on the Crime of a Slave and Crime against a Slave. The indication of choice becomes this: if he [the master] suspends [freedom] on a crime that requires financial liability like mistake and quasi-intentional, and if he suspends it on a crime that requires retaliation by saying to him: 'If you strike him with the sword, you are free', then nothing is due from the master by agreement because there is no difference between a slave and a free person in retaliation, so the master is not forfeiting the right of the victim's guardian by freeing. And with every killing that requires expiation, the master becomes a chooser, like direct killing; if expiation is not required, he is not a chooser, and it is killing by causation, like if he fell into a well dug by the master, because killing by causation is not actual killing in reality, because killing is an act upon a free person affecting the taking of life, and causation is not an act upon the free person because it only leads to blood money; hence retaliation is not required, and inheritance is not prevented, so he did not become an appropriator of the slave, and by direct killing he becomes a demander of the slave's value in every case where he destroys the slave, he guarantees the ransom as we stated. And if his slave informs him of the crime, and the master frees him and says: 'I did not believe him', then according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) he does not guarantee unless a free, just man informs him; according to the two [companions], he guarantees the blood money even if the informant is a sinner or disbeliever, and this has been mentioned in Agency and Preemption. And if he acknowledges for another, it is of two types: either he acknowledges the crime first and then ownership, or the opposite; and each type is not free from either the ownership in the slave being known to the acknowledger or being unknown. As for the first type: if he acknowledges the crime and then ownership for another, and the ownership in the slave is known to the acknowledger, and if the acknowledgee confirms him in ownership and crime, it is said to the acknowledgee: 'Hand over the slave or ransom him', because the acknowledgment is valid, because the right of the victim does not prevent the validity of the master's disposition, because his right is in the handing over or the ransom, and it remains after the acknowledgment, and what is established by acknowledgment is like what is established by just testimonial evidence. And whenever the ownership appears to the acknowledgee by the acknowledgment, it appears that the crime occurred from his property; and if he denies the crime, the acknowledger does not become a chooser for the ransom, contrary to Zufar; his proof is that the validity of the acknowledgment does not depend on the acknowledgee's confirmation, and for this reason if the acknowledger dies before confirmation, the acknowledged [slave] becomes an inheritance for his heirs, so the slave has left his ownership by the acknowledgment itself while he knows the crime, so he becomes a chooser. Our proof is that the validity of the acknowledgment does not necessitate confirmation, and nullification depends on denial; and when denial attaches to it, it becomes void from the beginning. So if he confirms him in ownership but denies the crime, the acknowledger becomes a chooser for the ransom because the acknowledgment of the crime against the slave meets his ownership in the slave, so it is valid; then when he acknowledges ownership for another and the acknowledgee confirms him, he becomes a remover of the slave from his ownership, so it is as if he sold or gifted him. As for the second type: if he acknowledges ownership first and then the crime; if he confirms him in both, the opponent is the acknowledgee; if he denies both, the opponent is the acknowledger; if he confirms him in ownership and denies the crime, the crime is rendered null because when the acknowledgee confirms him in ownership, it appears that his acknowledgment of the slave's crime is correct, so his acknowledgment of the crime becomes invalid when the acknowledgee denies it, so the crime is not established. Likewise if the slave is unknown, not known whether it belongs to the acknowledger or another, and he acknowledges the crime first and then ownership, or ownership first and then the crime, because ownership is established for the acknowledger by apparent possession not based on evidence, and ownership established by apparent possession is not suitable as a proof for entitlement and choice of ransom, so he does not become a chooser for the ransom, unlike if his ownership is known because his ownership is established based on evidence other than apparent possession, so it is suitable as a proof to establish what was not. And if he said: 'I sold him to so-and-so before the crime' and so-and-so confirms him, the buyer is given the choice between handing over and ransom because ownership is established by their mutual confirmation.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings regarding a master's liability when he frees his slave after the slave commits a crime. The author first distinguishes between crimes requiring financial compensation (such as khat' and shibh 'amd) and those requiring qisas (retaliation). If the master suspends the slave's freedom on a crime requiring qisas, he is not liable because the slave and free person are equal in qisas, and the master did not forfeit the victim's right by freeing. Then the passage discusses cases where the master frees the slave after being informed of the crime. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the master is not liable unless two just free men inform him; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), he is liable even if the informant is a sinner or disbeliever, as previously mentioned in the chapters on agency (wakalah) and pre-emption (shuf'ah). The passage then details the rules of acknowledgment (iqrar) when a person acknowledges a crime and then later acknowledges that the slave belongs to another, or vice versa. The key principle is that the validity of the acknowledgment depends on the ownership being known and the confirmation of the acknowledgee. If the slave's ownership is known to the acknowledger and the acknowledgee confirms both the ownership and the crime, the acknowledgee must either hand over the slave or ransom him. If the acknowledgee denies the crime, the master does not become liable for the ransom (khilafan li Zufar). However, if the acknowledgee confirms ownership but denies the crime, the master becomes liable for the ransom. If ownership is unknown, the master's acknowledgment does not make him liable for the ransom because apparent possession is not sufficient proof. Finally, if the master claims he sold the slave before the crime and the buyer confirms, the buyer has the option to hand over the slave or pay the ransom.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or liability, here of the crime or ownership.
مختاراً (mukhtāran): The master who is deemed to have chosen to be liable for the ransom by acknowledging the crime and ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the master become a chooser (mukhtar) for the ransom?
A) When he acknowledges the crime first and then ownership, and the acknowledgee denies both.
B) When the slave's ownership is known and the acknowledgee confirms ownership but denies the crime.
C) When the slave's ownership is unknown and the master acknowledges the crime.
D) When the master frees the slave before knowing the crime.

Key Takeaway
The master's liability through acknowledgment depends on the slave's ownership being known and the acknowledgee's response; denial can void the acknowledgment.

Settlement with a Slave for Intentional Cutting Leading to Death
Ruling on sulh and the effect of sirāyah on qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a sulh settlement when the injury spreads to death, and apply the distinction between qiyas and istihsan in intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A settlement becomes void if the injury spreads to death and the slave was not freed; but a pardon of the hand does not lead to qisas due to shubhah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a slave cuts a free man's hand, the victim accepts the slave as a settlement and frees him, but then dies from the wound. The ruling turns on whether the slave was freed. The author then engages a deeper question: why does this settlement lead to qisas when a pardon of the hand in similar circumstances does not? The answer reveals the interplay of qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
The act is a settlement (sulh) on a slave as compensation for a deliberately cut hand. The initial ruling: if the victim freed the slave, the settlement is valid; if not, the settlement is void and the slave is returned to his master, and retaliation (qisas) is taken against the slave.
Note that retaliation between free and slave in limbs is not applicable initially; diyah was the basis.
2
Trace the effect of sirāyah
When the wound spreads to death, the obligation shifts from diyah of the hand to qisas of the soul. Since the settlement was for diyah, it becomes void because the subject matter no longer exists. A void settlement creates no shubhah to avert qisas.
The victim's knowledge that the amputation was intentional is key; ignorance of spread does not create a doubt here.
3
Understand the debate about shubhah
The author questions whether voidness creates shubhah if the victim did not know the wound would spread. Analogies from the Book of Hudud show that ignorance can create shubhah, but in this case, the victim knew the act was intentional, so no shubhah arises.
Contrast with the case of sexual intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife: if he knew it was prohibited, no shubhah; if he did not know, shubhah exists.
4
Compare with the 'afw (pardon) case
Abu Hanifa rules that if a victim pardons the hand and then dies, the pardon becomes void but no qisas is required. This seems inconsistent with the sulh case where qisas is required when the slave is not freed.
The challenge is to reconcile the two rulings.
5
Distinguish via qiyas vs. istihsan
The sulh case is based on qiyas: the settlement confirms the crime, so the underlying liability remains. The 'afw case is based on istihsan: pardon nullifies the right, and the victim's ignorance of the spread creates a shubhah that averts qisas.
Alternatively, some distinguish that sulh is a substitute that confirms the crime, while 'afw cancels the right entirely.

عبد قطع يد حر عمداً ودفع إليه فحرره فمات من اليد فالعبد صلح بالجناية وإن لم يحرره رد على سيده ويقاد) لأنه إذا لم يعتقه وسرى ظهر أن الصلح كان باطلاً لأن الصلح وقع على المال وهو العبد عن دية اليد لأن القصاص لا يجري بين الحر والعبد في الأطراف وبالسراية ظهر أن دية اليد غير واجبة وأن الواجب هو القود فصار الصلح باطلاً لأن الصلح لا بد له من مصالح عنه والمصالح عنه المال ولم يوجد فبطل الصلح والباطل لا يورث شبهة كما لو وطئ مطلقته ثلاثاً في عدتها مع العلم بحرمتها عليه فإنه لا يصير شبهة في درء الحد فكذا هذا فوجب القصاص. أقول : فيه بحث وهو أنه إذا أراد أن البطلان لا يورث الشبهة فيما إذا علم بطلانه كما هو الظاهر مما ذكره في نظيره حيث قال فيه مع العلم بحرمتها عليه فهو مسلم لكن لا يجدي نفعاً ههنا لأن الدافع لم يعلم أن القطع يسري فيكون موجبه القود بل ظن أن لا يسري وكان موجبه المال، وإن أراد أن الباطل لا يورث الشبهة وإن لم يعلم بطلانه فهو ممنوع ألا ترى أنه إذا وطئ المطلقة ثلاثاً في عدتها ولم يعلم بحرمتها عليه بل ظن أنها تحل له فإنه يورث الشبهة فيدرء الحد كما صرحوا به في كتاب الحدود، ويفهم أيضاً ههنا من قوله مع العلم بحرمتها عليه. وأما إذا أعتقة فقد قصد صحة الإعتاق ضرورة لأن العاقل يقصد تصحيح تصرفه ولا صحة له إلا بالصلح عن الجناية وما يحدث منها ابتداء ولهذا لو نص عليه ورضي به جاز فكان مصالحاً عن الجناية، وما يحدث منها على العبد مقتضى الاقدام على الإعتاق والمولى أيضاً مصالحاً معه على هذا الوجه راضياً به لأنه لما رضي بكون العبد عوضاً عن القليل كان راضياً بكونه عوضاً عن الكثير، فإذا أعتقه الصلح في ضمن الإعتاق ابتداء، وإذا لم يعتقه لم يوجد الصلح ابتداء والصلح الأول وقع باطلاً فيرد العبد إلى المولى والأولياء بالخيار إن شاؤوا عفوا عنه، وإن شاؤوا قتلوه. وذكر في بعض نسخ الجامع الصغير : رجل قطع يد رجل عمداً فصالح المقطوع يده على عبده ودفعه إليه فأعتقه المقطوع يده ثم مات من ذلك فالعبد صلح بالجناية وإن لم يعتقد رد على مولاه، وقيل للأولياء إما أن تقتلوه أو تعفوا عنه والوجه ما بيناه فاتحد الحكم والعلة واختلفا صورة: ثم هذه المسألة مسألة وهي الصلح ترد إشكالاً على قول أبي حنيفة فيما إذا عفا عن اليد ثم سرى إلى النفس ومات حيث يبطل العفو ولا يجب القصاص هناك، وفي هذه المسألة قال : يبطل الصلح ويجب القصاص فيما إذا لم يعتق العبد، وإن أعتقه فالصلح باق على حاله. فالجواب أما إذا لم يعتقه فقد قيل ما ذكر في مسألة الصلح جواب القياس، وما ذكر في مسألة الصلح جواب الاستحسان فيكونان على القياس والاستحسان وقيل بالفرق بينهما ووجه أن الصلح عن الجناية على مال يقرر الجناية ولا يبطلها لأن الصلح عن الجناية استيفاء للجناية معنى باستيفاء بدلها ولهذا تعينت الجناية وتوفر عليه عقوبتها وهو القصاص، أقول : يرد عليه صح
A slave intentionally cut the hand of a free man, and [the victim] was given possession [of the slave] and freed him, and [the victim] died from the hand [wound]. Then the slave is a valid settlement for the crime; but if he did not free him, he [the slave] is returned to his master, and retaliation is taken [against the slave]. Because if he did not free him and the wound spread, it becomes clear that the settlement was void, because the settlement was made on property (the slave) as compensation for the diyah of the hand, since retaliation does not apply between a free person and a slave in limbs; and by the spread, it becomes clear that the diyah of the hand is not obligatory, and what is obligatory is retaliation; so the settlement becomes void because a settlement must have something being settled for, and the thing being settled for was property, which did not exist. Consequently, the settlement is void, and a void thing does not create a doubt (shubhah) – just as if one had sexual intercourse with his thrice-divorced wife during her 'iddah with knowledge of its prohibition, that does not create a doubt to avert the hadd punishment; likewise here, so retaliation becomes obligatory. I say: there is a discussion about this. If he meant that a void thing does not create a doubt when one knows it is void, as is apparent from what he mentioned in the analogy, where he said 'with knowledge of its prohibition', that is accepted, but it does not benefit here because the hand [victim] did not know that the cut would spread, so its required consequence would be retaliation; rather, he thought it would not spread, and its required consequence was property. And if he meant that a void thing does not create a doubt even if one does not know it is void, then it is objectionable. Do you not see that if one has intercourse with a thrice-divorced woman during her 'iddah not knowing its prohibition but thinking it is permissible for him, it creates a doubt and thus averts the hadd punishment, as they explicitly stated in the Book of Hudud. This is also understood here from his statement 'with knowledge of its prohibition.' As for when he freed him, he intended the validity of the freeing necessarily, because a rational person intends to validate his action, and there is no validity except by a settlement for the crime and what arises from it initially. For this reason, if he explicitly stated it and was content with it, it is permissible; so he is settling for the crime and what arises from it on the basis of the slave, in accordance with proceeding with the freeing; and the master (owner) is also settling with him in this manner, being content with it, because when he was content with the slave as compensation for the minor injury, he is content with it as compensation for the major injury. Thus, when he freed him, the settlement was included within the freeing initially; and if he did not free him, the initial settlement did not exist, and the first settlement became void; so the slave is returned to his master, and the guardians have the choice: if they wish, they may pardon him; if they wish, they may kill him. And it is mentioned in some copies of al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'A man intentionally cut the hand of a man, and the cut-hand man settled on his slave, and gave him to him, and the cut-hand man freed him, then he died from that; then the slave is a valid settlement for the crime. And if he did not free him, he is returned to his master, and it is said to the guardians: either you kill him or you pardon him.' And the correct view is what we have explained; thus the ruling and the reason are the same, but they differ in form. Then this issue – the settlement – presents an objection to the statement of Abu Hanifa regarding when one pardons the hand, then it spreads to the soul and he dies: there, the pardon becomes void and retaliation is not obligatory; while in this issue, he said: the settlement becomes void and retaliation is obligatory when he does not free the slave; if he frees him, the settlement remains as it was. The answer: as for when he did not free him, it is said that what was mentioned in the settlement issue is the answer according to qiyas; and what was mentioned in the pardon issue is the answer according to istihsan. Thus they are based on qiyas and istihsan. And it is said that there is a difference between them: the explanation is that a settlement for a crime on property confirms the crime and does not nullify it, because a settlement for a crime is a taking of the crime's substitute in meaning, so the crime is confirmed and its punishment – retaliation – remains applicable. I say: this is objected to. Correct.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a specific case: a slave intentionally cuts off the hand of a free man. The victim agrees to a sulh (settlement) on the slave, takes possession, and frees the slave. Later, the victim dies from the wound. The ruling is: if the victim freed the slave, the settlement is valid and the slave is considered the compensation for the entire crime (including the death). If he did not free the slave, the settlement becomes null and void because the spread of the wound reveals that the true right was retaliation (qisas), not blood money (diyah) for the hand. Since retaliation does not apply between a free person and a slave in limbs, the settlement on the hand's diyah is based on a mistake; when death occurs, the obligation shifts to qisas, and the settlement is void. The author then engages in a debate (بحث) about whether the voidness of the settlement creates a doubt (shubhah) to avert qisas. The author argues that if the victim did not know the wound would spread, his ignorance creates a doubt, and thus qisas might be averted. However, the text ultimately upholds the qisas because the settlement is void and does not create a shubhah when the party knows it is void (the victim knew the amputation was intentional, so he should know the possibility of death). The passage then contrasts this with the case of 'afw (pardon) of the hand before death, where Abu Hanifa says the 'afw becomes void but no qisas is required due to shubhah. The answer given is that the settlement case is based on qiyas (analogy) while the 'afw case is based on istihsan (juristic preference), so they differ. Alternatively, some scholars distinguish between the two: sulh confirms the crime (it is a substitute for the crime), so the underlying crime subsists, whereas 'afw cancels the crime entirely. The author notes an objection to this distinction.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise between the victim and the offender, here involving the slave as compensation for the injury.
سراية (sarāyah): The spread of an injury (here the cut hand) leading to death, which changes the legal classification from injury to homicide.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt that can avert a hadd or qisas punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario of a slave cutting a free man's hand, if the victim takes the slave as a settlement and frees him, then dies from the wound, what is the ruling?
A) The settlement is void and qisas is required.
B) The settlement is valid and the slave is considered the compensation for the crime.
C) The slave is returned to his master and no compensation is due.
D) The victim's heirs must accept diyah from the master.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a settlement for a bodily injury depends on whether the injury proves fatal; if the settlement was on a slave and the victim freed him, it stands; otherwise it is void and retaliation applies.

Settlement for Hand Injury Leading to Death: Validity of Sulh and Qisas
Analysing a slave's settlement for cutting a free man's hand when the free man later dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a settlement for a limb injury that later causes death, explain why the settlement is void without emancipation, and evaluate the refinement regarding legal doubt (shubha).

Core Idea
A settlement for a limb injury is void if the victim dies without emancipation; emancipation creates a new valid settlement covering the death.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a nuanced case: a slave cuts a free man's hand, the free man accepts the slave as compensation and frees him, then dies from the wound. The ruling hinges on whether the slave was emancipated. The author presents the initial ruling, then subjects it to critical analysis, refining the application of the doctrine of legal doubt (shubha). We will work through the legal reasoning, focusing on the validity of the settlement and the effect of the spread of injury (saraya).

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario and initial ruling
Identify that a slave cuts a free man's hand, the free man accepts the slave as compensation (sulh) and frees him, then dies. The initial ruling: if freed, settlement valid; if not freed, settlement void and qisas.
Note the distinction between emancipation and non-emancipation as the decisive factor.
2
Identify the basis for voidness
The settlement was made for the diyat of the hand, but death transforms the obligation to qisas. Since the subject matter (diyat of hand) no longer exists, the settlement is void.
Sulh requires a valid subject matter (property) in exchange for the injury.
3
Analyze the critical refinement on shubha
The initial author analogized the void settlement to the case of intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife with knowledge, concluding no doubt (shubha). Ibn Nujaym refines: the master did not know the wound would spread, so his ignorance should create a doubt to avert qisas.
Knowledge of the invalidity is key; ignorance can produce shubha.
4
Understand the effect of emancipation
Emancipation implies a new settlement from the beginning. Since a rational person intends validity, the emancipation is interpreted as a settlement covering both the hand injury and any future consequences, including death.
The act of emancipation reestablishes the settlement with a wider scope.
5
Compare with forgiveness and intra-Hanafi dispute
Forgiveness of the hand leaves a lingering doubt (shubha) so no qisas, but settlement without emancipation does not because the master knew the settlement was only for the hand. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the analogy between settlement and forgiveness.
This dispute shows the nuance between 'ibra' (forgiveness) and mu'awada (transaction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد قطع يد حر ودفع إليه فحرره فمات من اليد فالعبد صلح بالجناية وإن لم يحرره رد على سيده ويقاد لأنه إذا لم يعتقه وسرى ظهر أن الصلح كان باطلاً لأن الصلح وقع على المال وهو العبد عن دية اليد لأن القصاص لا يجري بين الحر والعبد في الأطراف وبالسراية ظهر أن دية اليد غير واجبة وأن الواجب هو القود فصار الصلح باطلاً لأن الصلح لا بد له من مصالح عنه والمصالح عنه المال ولم يوجد فبطل الصلح والباطل لا يورث شبهة كما لو وطئ مطلقته ثلاثاً في عدتها مع العلم بحرمتها عليه فإنه لا يصير شبهة في درء الحد فكذا هذا فوجب القصاص. أقول : فيه بحث وهو أنه إذا أراد أن البطلان لا يورث الشبهة فيما إذا علم بطلانه كما هو الظاهر مما ذكره في نظيره حيث قال فيه مع العلم بحرمتها عليه فهو مسلم لكن لا يجدي نفعاً ههنا لأن الدافع لم يعلم أن القطع يسري فيكون موجبه القود بل ظن أن لا يسري وكان موجبه المال، وإن أراد أن الباطل لا يورث الشبهة وإن لم يعلم بطلانه فهو ممنوع ألا ترى أنه إذا وطئ المطلقة ثلاثاً في عدتها ولم يعلم بحرمتها عليه بل ظن أنها تحل له فإنه يورث الشبهة فيدرء الحد كما صرحوا به في كتاب الحدود، ويفهم أيضاً ههنا من قوله مع العلم بحرمتها عليه. وأما إذا أعتقة فقد قصد صحة الإعتاق ضرورة لأن العاقل يقصد تصحيح تصرفه ولا صحة له إلا بالصلح عن الجناية وما يحدث منها ابتداء ولهذا لو نص عليه ورضي به جاز فكان مصالحاً عن الجناية، وما يحدث منها على العبد مقتضى الاقدام على الإعتاق والمولى أيضاً مصالحاً معه على هذا الوجه راضياً به لأنه لما رضي بكون العبد عوضاً عن القليل كان راضياً بكونه عوضاً عن الكثير، فإذا أعتقه الصلح في ضمن الإعتاق ابتداء، وإذا لم يعتقه لم يوجد الصلح ابتداء والصلح الأول وقع باطلاً فيرد العبد إلى المولى والأولياء بالخيار إن شاؤوا عفوا عنه، وإن شاؤوا قتلوه. وذكر في بعض نسخ الجامع الصغير : رجل قطع يد رجل عمداً فصالح المقطوع يده على عبده ودفعه إليه فأعتقه المقطوع يده ثم مات من ذلك فالعبد صلح بالجناية وإن لم يعتقد رد على مولاه، وقيل للأولياء إما أن تقتلوه أو تعفوا عنه والوجه ما بيناه فاتحد الحكم والعلة واختلفا صورة: ثم هذه المسألة مسألة وهي الصلح ترد إشكالاً على قول أبي حنيفة فيما إذا عفا عن اليد ثم سرى إلى النفس ومات حيث يبطل العفو ولا يجب القصاص هناك، وفي هذه المسألة قال : يبطل الصلح ويجب القصاص فيما إذا لم يعتق العبد، وإن أعتقه فالصلح باق على حاله. فالجواب أما إذا لم يعتقه فقد قيل ما ذكر في مسألة الصلح جواب القياس، وما ذكر في مسألة الصلح جواب الاستحسان فيكونان على القياس والاستحسان وقيل بالفرق بينهما ووجه أن الصلح عن الجناية على مال يقرر الجناية ولا يبطلها لأن الصلح عن الجناية استيفاء للجناية معنى باستيفاء بدلها ولهذا تعينت الجناية وتوفر عليه عقوبتها وهو القصاص، أقول : يرد عليه أنه إن أريد بقولهم الصلح لا يبطل الجناية بل يقررها أن الصلح لا يسقط موجب الجناية بل يبقيه على حاله فهو ممنوع كيفما كان، وقد صرحوا في صدر كتاب الجنايات بأن موجب القتل العمد القود إلا أن يعفو الأولياء أو يصالحوا فقد جعلوا الصلح كالعفو وفي إسقاط موجب الجنايات ، وإن أريد بذلك أن الصلح لا ينافي ثبوت موجب الجناية في الأصل بل يقرر ذلك حيث وقع الصلح عنه على مال، وإن سقوطه بعد تحقق الصلح فهو مسلم لكن لا يتم حينئذ قولهم، فإذا لم تبطل الجناية لم يمنع العقوبة إذا لا يلزم من عدم بطلان الجناية بمعنى ثبوتها في الأصل عدم امتناع العقوبة بعد تحقق الصلح عنها كما هو الحال فيما نحن فيه بل لا يتم حينئذ الفرق رأساً بين صورتي الغدو والصلح، والعفو أيضاً لا ينافي ثبوت موجب الجناية في الأصل قبل العفو كما لا يخفى . وأما العفو فهو معدم للجناية والعفو عن القطع وإن بطل بالسراية إلى النفس لكن بقيت شبهته لوجود صورة العفو وهي كافية لدرء الحد، وأما إذا أعتقه فجوابه هو الفرق الذي ذكرناه أن العتق صلحاً ابتداء بخلاف العفو، وعلى قولهما أيضاً يرد في الصورتين لأنهما كانا يجعلان العفو عن القطع عفواً عما يحدث منه وفي الصلح لم يجعلا كذلك بل أوجبا القصاص عليه إذا لم يعتقه وجعلاه صلحاً مبتدأ إذا أعتقه . وقد قدمنا مسائل سراية الجرح فلا نعيدها والله أعلم .
A slave cuts the hand of a free man, and (the free man) is given (the slave) and frees him, then (the free man) dies from the hand (wound). Then the slave is a valid settlement for the injury. If he did not free him, (the slave) is returned to his master and qisas is imposed. Because if he did not emancipate him and the wound spread (caused death), it becomes clear that the settlement was void, because the settlement was on property—the slave—in exchange for the blood-money of the hand, for qisas does not run between a free man and a slave in limbs. And by the spread (of the wound to death) it becomes clear that the blood-money of the hand is not obligatory and that what is obligatory is retaliation, so the settlement becomes void because a settlement must have something settled upon, and what was settled upon was property, and it did not exist. So the settlement is void, and what is void does not create a legal doubt, just as if he had intercourse with his thrice-divorced wife during her waiting period with knowledge of its prohibition—it does not become a doubt to avert the hadd penalty. So likewise here, and thus retaliation is obligatory. I say: There is a discussion—if he means that voidness does not create a doubt when one knows it is void, as is apparent from what he mentioned in its analogy where he said 'with knowledge of its prohibition', then it is accepted but is of no benefit here because the payer did not know that the cut would spread (cause death), so its consequence would be retaliation; rather he thought it would not spread and its consequence would be property. If he means that the void does not create a doubt even if one does not know it is void, then it is rejected. Do you not see that if he had intercourse with a thrice-divorced woman in her waiting period and did not know of its prohibition but thought she was lawful for him, then it creates a doubt and the hadd is averted, as they explicitly stated in the Book of Hudud. And this is also understood here from his statement 'with knowledge of its prohibition'. As for when he emancipates him, he intended the validity of emancipation out of necessity, because a rational person intends the validity of his action, and it is not valid except as a settlement for the injury and what arises from it from the beginning. Hence if he specified it and was pleased with it, it is permissible, so he is settling for the injury and what arises from it. The act of emancipation implies that the master is also settling with him on this basis and is pleased with it, because when he was pleased with the slave as compensation for a small thing, he is pleased with him as compensation for a large thing. So when he emancipates him, the settlement occurs within the emancipation from the beginning. If he does not emancipate him, the settlement does not exist from the beginning, and the first settlement was void, so the slave is returned to the master and the guardians have the choice: if they wish, they may forgive him, and if they wish, they may kill him. It is mentioned in some copies of al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man cuts the hand of another man intentionally, and the victim settles on his slave and takes him, then the victim emancipates him, then dies from that—the slave is a valid settlement for the injury; if he did not emancipate, (the slave) is returned to his owner, and it is said to the guardians: either kill him or forgive him. The position is what we have explained, so the ruling and the cause (illa) coincide while they differ in form. Then this case (the settlement) poses a problem for Abu Hanifa's statement regarding when one forgives the hand and then it spreads to the soul and he dies—there the forgiveness becomes void and retaliation is not obligatory. In this case he said: the settlement becomes void and retaliation is obligatory if he did not emancipate the slave; if he emancipated him, the settlement remains valid. The answer: As for when he did not emancipate him, it is said that what was mentioned in the case of settlement is the answer by qiyas, and what was mentioned in the case of forgiveness is the answer by istihsan, so they are based on qiyas and istihsan. And it is said that there is a difference between them: the settlement for an injury in exchange for property confirms the injury and does not nullify it, because settlement for an injury is a satisfaction of the injury in meaning by taking its substitute, hence the injury is established and its penalty—retaliation—is incurred. I say: It is objected that if they mean by their statement 'the settlement does not nullify the injury but confirms it' that the settlement does not drop the consequence of the injury but keeps it as it is, then it is rejected completely. They have stated at the beginning of the Book of Injuries that the consequence of intentional killing is retaliation unless the guardians forgive or settle—thus they made settlement like forgiveness in dropping the consequences of injuries. If they mean that the settlement does not negate the existence of the consequence of the injury in origin but confirms it, in that the settlement happened on property, and its dropping after the settlement is actual—that is accepted, but then their statement does not hold. So if the injury is not nullified, it does not prevent punishment, because the non-nullification of the injury (meaning its existence in origin) does not entail the non-impediment of punishment after the settlement has taken place for it, as is the case in our subject. Rather the difference between the two forms (forgiveness and settlement) is not established. Forgiveness also does not negate the existence of the consequence of the injury in origin before forgiveness, as is well known. As for forgiveness, it is nullifies the injury, and forgiveness of the cut even if it becomes void by spreading to the soul, still its doubt remains due to the existence of the form of forgiveness, which is sufficient to avert the hadd. As for when he emancipates him, the answer is the difference we mentioned: that emancipation is a settlement anew, unlike forgiveness. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also, it is problematic in both forms because they consider forgiveness of the cut as forgiveness of what arises from it, but in the settlement they did not do so; rather they imposed retaliation when he did not emancipate and considered it a new settlement when he emancipated. And we have already presented cases of the spread of injury, so we will not repeat them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage deals with a complex case in Hanafi fiqh: a slave intentionally cuts the hand of a free man. The free man accepts the slave as compensation (sulh) and then frees him. Subsequently, the free man dies from the wound. The ruling is that if the slave was freed, the settlement is valid and no qisas is due; if he was not freed, the settlement is void and the victim's guardians may demand qisas. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) presents the original ruling, then critiques it, and provides a refined view. He first explains that if the slave was not freed, the settlement was for diyat of the hand, but since death occurred, the diyat is not due; instead qisas is due, so the settlement (which was for property) is void because the subject matter (the property) was not actually the correct compensation. He compares this to a case where a man knowingly has intercourse with his thrice-divorced wife—that does not create a doubt to avert the hadd. However, the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) points out that in our case, the master did not know the injury would spread, so he thought the compensation was for the diyat of the hand; thus his ignorance should create a doubt to avert qisas, unlike the divorce case where knowledge was present. If the slave was freed, the master intended a valid transaction: since rational people aim for validity, the emancipation implies that the master accepted the slave as compensation for the injury and any future consequences (including death). Hence the settlement is considered a new settlement from the beginning. The passage also discusses a discrepancy with Abu Hanifa's view on forgiveness: if someone forgives a hand injury and then the victim dies, the forgiveness is void and no qisas is due (because the forgiveness of the hand is not forgiveness of the soul). But here, if the slave is not freed, the settlement is void and qisas is due. The response distinguishes between forgiveness (which would be void but leaves a doubt) and settlement (which is either void or valid depending on whether the slave was freed). The commentator also notes a difference between the approaches of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the issue.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise in which the victim accepts compensation (mal) for the injury, thereby dropping the right of qisas.
سراية (saraya): The spread of an injury (e.g., from a limb to the whole body) leading to death; this transforms the legal obligation from diyat to qisas.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that can avert a fixed penalty (hadd or qisas); here, the question is whether the void settlement creates a shubha to prevent qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave cuts the hand of a free man intentionally. The free man accepts the slave as compensation (sulh) and frees him. Later, the free man dies from the wound. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the outcome?
A) The settlement is void and qisas is imposed on the slave.
B) The settlement is valid and the slave is freed; no qisas.
C) The settlement is void, but no qisas due to doubt.
D) The slave must be returned and the guardian chooses between forgiveness and qisas.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a settlement for a limb injury that later causes death hinges on whether the victim's emancipation indicates a new settlement covering both the injury and its future consequences.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Double Liability for a Slave with Debt and Injury When Freed Ignorantly
Explaining the double liability on the slave's value when he has debt and commits a non-lethal injury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's liability when he frees a slave who has debt and commits a non-lethal injury, and explain why two values are due when the master is ignorant.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave with debt and injury destroys two independent rights—master owes two full values.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced case where a slave, already encumbered by debt, accidentally injures someone. The master, unaware of the injury, frees the slave. The Hanafi school holds that the master must pay two full values: one to the creditor and one to the victim. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the legal concept that the slave's value is a security for both rights, and the master's act of emancipation forfeits both simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the first sentence: a slave with debt commits a non-lethal injury unintentionally, then the master frees him without knowledge. The ruling: two values upon the master.
The scenario has three elements: slave is madyūn (in debt), commits janiya (injury) below murder, and the master frees him ignorantly.
2
Understand the rationale: destruction of two rights
The text states 'because he destroyed two rights.' Each right (the creditor's claim on the slave's value and the victim's claim for compensation) individually guarantees the full value. The master's freeing eliminates both, so he must compensate both.
Even though the slave is one entity, the rights are independent and equal in strength.
3
Examine the practical combination of the two rights from one slave
The text explains that the two rights can be satisfied by first delivering the slave to the victim's guardian (which satisfies the injury claim), then selling the slave for the debt. The master covers both through 'tafwīt' (forfeiture).
This order—delivery prior to sale—is possible because the slave remains in the master's possession until delivery.
4
Distinguish the case where the master is ignorant versus knowing
The passage limits the double-value ruling to ignorance. If the master knew of the injury, he owes diyat (blood-money) for fatal injuries plus the value of the slave to the creditor, because emancipation after knowledge is treated as direct causation of loss.
Knowledge changes the legal character of emancipation, making it an intentional act that triggers additional liability.
5
Apply the general principle regarding a slave with debt and injury
The text concludes with the master's choice: either deliver the slave to the victim (then sold for debt) or ransom the slave. The benefit of delivering is that the victim obtains a right to take the slave for personal benefit.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that the victim's right is not merely financial but includes the possibility of taking the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنى ما دون مديون خطأ فحرره سيده بلا علم عليه قيمتان قيمة لرب الدين وقيمة لولي الجناية لأنه أتلف حقين كل واحد منهما مضمون بكل القيمة على الانفراد الدفع على الأولياء والبيع على الغرماء فكذا عند الاجتماع، ويمكن الجمع بين الحقين أيضاً من الرقبة الواحدة بأن يدفع إلى ولي الجناية أولاً ثم يباع للغرماء فيضمنهما بالتفويت بخلاف ما إذا أتلفه أجنبي والمسألة بحالها حيث يجب عليه قيمة واحدة للمولى بحكم الملك في رقبته فلا يظهر حق الفريقين بالنسبة إلى ملك المالك لأنه دون الملك فصار كأنه ليس فيه حق، ثم الغريم أحق بتمليك القيمة لأنها مالية العبد والغريم مقدم في المالية على ولي الجنابة لأن الواجب أن يدفع إليه ثم يباع للغريم فكان مقدماً معنی والقيمة هي فتسلم إليه، وفي الفصل الأول كان التعارض بين الحقين وهما متساويان فيضمنهما فيظهران وقيد بعدم العلم لأنه لو وهو عالم بالجناية كان عليه الدية إذا كانت الجناية في النفس لأوليائه وقيمة العبد لصاحب الدين لأن الإعتاق بعد العلم موجب الأرش، والأصل أن العبد إذا جنى وعليه دين خير المولى بين الدفع إلى ولي الجناية والفداء فإن اختار الدفع إلى ولي الجناية دفع ثم يباع في الدين، فإن فضل شيء فهو لولي الجناية لأنه بدل ملكه وإلا فلا شيء له، وإن بدأ بالدفع بين الحقين لأنه أمكن بيعه بعد الدفع، ولو بدأ ببيعه في الدين لا يمكن دفعه بالجناية لأنه لم يوجد في يد المشتري جناية، ولا يقال لا فائدة في الدفع إذا كان يباع عليه لأنا نقول : فائدته ثبوت استخلاص العبد لأن ولي الجناية ثبت له حق الاستخلاص وللإنسان أغراض في أعتقه جمعاً
He (a slave) commits an injury less than murder unintentionally while being in debt, then his master frees him without knowledge—there are two values upon him (the master): a value for the debt-holder and a value for the guardian of the injury. Because he has destroyed two rights, each of which is guaranteed by the full value individually—delivery to the guardians (of the injury) and sale for the creditors. So likewise when combined. It is possible to combine the two rights from one slave by delivering to the guardian of the injury first, then selling for the creditors, thus he (the master) encompasses both through forfeiture. Unlike if a stranger had destroyed him (the slave) and the case is the same—then only one value is due to the master due to his ownership of the slave's neck; the right of the two parties does not appear with respect to the owner's property because it is lesser than ownership, so it is as if there is no right. Then the creditor is more entitled to the value because it is the wealth of the slave, and the creditor takes precedence over the guardian of the injury in wealth matters because the obligation is to pay him first, then sell for the creditor, so he is prioritized in meaning, and the value is his. In the first case, there was conflict between the two rights, and they are equal, so he guarantees both, and they appear. It is conditioned upon (the master) not knowing, because if he knew of the injury, then upon him is the blood-money if the injury is to the soul for his guardians, and the value of the slave for the debt-holder, because emancipation after knowledge necessitates compensation (arsh). The principle: when a slave commits an injury and owes debt, the master is given a choice between delivering to the guardian of the injury or ransoming. If he chooses to deliver to the guardian of the injury, he delivers, then the slave is sold for the debt. If there is an excess, it is for the guardian of the injury because it is the substitute for his property; otherwise nothing. If he begins with delivery between the two rights, because it is possible to sell after delivery. If he began with sale for the debt, it is not possible to deliver for the injury because there is no injury in the hand of the buyer. It cannot be said there is no benefit in delivery if it will be sold—we say: its benefit is the establishment of the right of the guardian to take the slave, and a person has aims in emancipating him cumulatively.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a slave who owes a debt (dayn) commits a non-lethal injury (jani ma dūn madyūn khaṭa')—i.e., an accidental harm that is less than murder—and then his master frees him without knowing about the injury. The ruling is that the master owes two values: one value to the creditor (the slave's value as property to cover the debt) and another value to the victim (or his guardian) as compensation (diyah) for the injury. The reason is that the master, by freeing the slave, has effectively destroyed two rights: the right of the creditor to the slave as security and the right of the victim to claim the slave for the injury. Both rights are guaranteed by the full value of the slave when considered independently. The text explains how the two rights can be satisfied from one slave: first, the slave is handed over to the victim's guardian (taking his value), then the slave (or his value) is sold for the debt. The master is responsible for both values because he caused the loss of both rights by his act of emancipation. This is contrasted with a case where a stranger kills the slave: then only one value is due to the master because the rights of the creditor and victim are subordinate to the master's ownership. The creditor takes precedence over the injury claim in wealth matters because the debt is a financial right, while the injury claim is also financial but the creditor has priority in the property of the slave. The text also notes that this rule applies only if the master was ignorant of the injury. If he knew, then for a fatal injury he must pay full diyat to the victim's heirs and also the value of the slave to the creditor, because emancipation after knowledge is considered equivalent to causing the loss (it obligates arsh). The passage concludes with the general rule: when a slave both has debt and commits an injury, the master may choose to deliver the slave to the victim first (then it is sold for debt) or ransom the slave. If he delivers, the victim gets the slave, and then the creditor sells it. The benefit of delivering is that the victim's right to take the slave is established—the victim might have a personal interest in having the slave (e.g., for labor or ransom).

Key Terms
مديون (madyūn): A slave who owes debt, meaning the slave is encumbered by a debt (dayn) that his master owes to a creditor, or the slave himself has a debt.
ولي الجناية (walī al-jināya): The guardian or person who has the right to claim compensation for an injury (diyah) when the victim is not the offender.
تفويت (tafwīt): Causing the loss of a thing by an act that prevents its use or delivery; here, the master's emancipation causes the loss of both the creditor's and the victim's rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave who owes a debt of 500 dirhams accidentally injures a free man, causing a hand cut. The master, unaware of the injury, frees the slave. The slave's value is 1000 dirhams. According to the passage, what is the master liable for?
A) One value (1000 dirhams) divided between the creditor and the victim.
B) One value (1000 dirhams) only to the creditor because the injury claim is subordinate.
C) Two values (2000 dirhams): one to the creditor and one to the victim.
D) Nothing, because the master was ignorant of the injury.

Key Takeaway
When a master frees a slave who has both debt and an injury (and the master is ignorant of the injury), he must pay two full values—one to the creditor and one to the victim—because emancipation destroys two independent rights.

Two Full Values: Slave Freed with Pending Claims
Ruling and commentary on multiple claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master frees a slave who has committed an injury and owes debt, and explain why two full values are owed.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave with pending claims destroys two rights, each worth the slave's full value.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh where competing claims — a debt and a victim's right — both attach to a slave. The master's act of freeing without knowledge triggers a dual liability. Understanding the reasoning behind paying two full values is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurisprudence balances multiple rights against a single asset.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act in question: a master frees his slave without knowledge that the slave has committed a non-intentional injury and owes a debt. The ruling: the master must pay two full values—one to the creditor and one to the victim's guardian.
The ruling is conditional on the master being ignorant of the injury at the time of freeing.
2
Locate the Dalil (Legal Proof)
The passage does not quote a primary text but bases the ruling on qiyas. The master's act destroys two rights (creditor's and victim's), each of which attaches to the entire value of the slave. This is analogous to a case where an outsider destroys the slave, but distinguished because here the master himself commits the destruction.
The underlying principle: each right is a complete claim against the slave's value.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (Reasoning)
The reasoning is that the slave's value is a guarantee (damān) for both claims. Since the master eliminated that guarantee by freeing, he must compensate each claimant with the full value. The text emphasizes that the two claims are equal in strength, so the master guarantees both.
The slave is handed over to the victim first, then sold for the debt.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Scholarly Disagreement)
The passage references a difference between Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the effect of pardon and reconciliation on the master's liability. Abu Hanifa holds that pardon eliminates the claim, while reconciliation does not; the two equate reconciliation with pardon in dropping the claim.
This ikhtilaf is a separate issue from the core ruling on paying two values.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preponderant View)
The text does not explicitly state the mufta bihi view for the pardon/reconciliation dispute, but the main ruling (two values) is the relied-upon position from the zahir al-riwayah. For the dispute, the Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) appears to be the default, but the commentary leaves room for nuance.
Students should focus on the established ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه ١٥٧ على سيده ويقاد جنى ما دون مديون خطأ فحرره سيده بلا علم عليه قيمتان قيمة لرب الدين وقيمة لولي الجناية لأنه أتلف حقين كل واحد منهما مضمون بكل القيمة على الانفراد الدفع على الأولياء والبيع على الغرماء فكذا عند الاجتماع، ويمكن الجمع بين الحقين أيضاً من الرقبة الواحدة بأن يدفع إلى ولي الجناية أولاً ثم يباع للغرماء فيضمنهما بالتفويت بخلاف ما إذا أتلفه أجنبي والمسألة بحالها حيث يجب عليه قيمة واحدة للمولى بحكم الملك في رقبته فلا يظهر حق الفريقين بالنسبة إلى ملك المالك لأنه دون الملك فصار كأنه ليس فيه حق، ثم الغريم أحق بتمليك القيمة لأنها مالية العبد والغريم مقدم في المالية على ولي الجنابة لأن الواجب أن يدفع إليه ثم يباع للغريم فكان مقدماً معنی والقيمة هي فتسلم إليه، وفي الفصل الأول كان التعارض بين الحقين وهما متساويان فيضمنهما فيظهران وقيد بعدم العلم لأنه لو وهو عالم بالجناية كان عليه الدية إذا كانت الجناية في النفس لأوليائه وقيمة العبد لصاحب الدين لأن الإعتاق بعد العلم موجب الأرش، والأصل أن العبد إذا جنى وعليه دين خير المولى بين الدفع إلى ولي الجناية والفداء فإن اختار الدفع إلى ولي الجناية دفع ثم يباع في الدين، فإن فضل شيء فهو لولي الجناية لأنه بدل ملكه وإلا فلا شيء له، وإن بدأ بالدفع بين الحقين لأنه أمكن بيعه بعد الدفع، ولو بدأ ببيعه في الدين لا يمكن دفعه بالجناية لأنه لم يوجد في يد المشتري جناية، ولا يقال لا فائدة في الدفع إذا كان يباع عليه لأنا نقول : فائدته ثبوت استخلاص العبد لأن ولي الجناية ثبت له حق الاستخلاص وللإنسان أغراض في أعتقه جمعاً
Chapter of Injuries: Chapter on Injury by a Slave and Injury upon Him. Upon his master and he is led, if a slave who is indebted commits a less than intentional injury and his master frees him without knowledge, upon him (the master) are two values: a value for the creditor and a value for the guardian of the injury, because he has destroyed two rights, each of which is guaranteed by the full value individually, delivery to the guardians and sale for the creditors, so likewise at the time of combination. It is also possible to combine both rights from one slave by delivering first to the guardian of the injury then sold for the creditors, so he guarantees them by causing loss, unlike if a third party destroyed him and the case is the same where only one value is obligatory for the master by virtue of ownership in his neck, so the right of the two groups does not appear relative to the master's ownership because it is below ownership, so it becomes as if there is no right in it. Then the creditor is more entitled to the payment of the value because it is the monetary value of the slave and the creditor takes precedence in monetary matters over the guardian of the injury because the obligation is to deliver to him then sell for the creditor, so he is given precedence in meaning and the value is delivered to him. In the first case there was conflict between the two rights and they are equal, so he guarantees them and they appear. It was conditioned by lack of knowledge because if he was aware of the injury, then upon him is the blood money if the injury was to life for his guardians and the value of the slave for the creditor because freeing after knowledge necessitates compensation (arsh). The principle is that if a slave commits an injury and has a debt, the master chooses between handing over to the guardian of the injury or ransoming. If he chooses to hand over, he hands over and then he is sold for the debt. If anything remains, it is for the guardian of the injury because it is the substitute for his property; otherwise he gets nothing. If he begins by handing over between the two rights because it is possible to sell him after handing over. If he begins by selling him for the debt, it is not possible to hand him over for the injury because no injury occurred in the hand of the buyer. It is not said there is no benefit in handing over if he will be sold because we say: its benefit is the establishment of the slave's exemption because the guardian of the injury has the right of exemption and people have purposes in freeing him collectively.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq regarding a slave who commits a less-than-intentional injury (jina ma dun mudyun) and also owes debt. The master frees the slave without knowing about the injury. The ruling states that the master must pay two full values: one to the creditor (for the debt) and one to the guardian of the victim (for the injury). The commentary explains that the master has destroyed two rights, each of which is guaranteed by the slave's full value. The slave's value covers both, but since both claims are equal, they are satisfied by paying each claim from the same slave: first the slave is handed over to the victim's guardian (who has the right to take him) and then sold for the debt. The author distinguishes this from a case where an outsider destroys the slave, where only one value is paid to the master because the rights of the creditor and victim are subordinate to the master's ownership. The commentary also discusses the condition 'without knowledge': if the master knew about the injury, he would owe blood money (diya) for a life injury or the value for the creditor, because freeing after knowledge makes the master liable for the full compensation (arsh). The original principle: when a slave with debt commits a crime, the master may hand him over to the victim or pay compensation. If he hands over, the slave is sold for debt; any surplus goes to the victim. The commentary defends the benefit of handing over even if the slave is later sold, because the victim gains the right to take the slave. This lesson includes a detailed discussion on the priority of claims.

Key Terms
جناية ما دون مديون (jināyat mā dūna mudyun): An injury committed by a slave that is less than intentional killing, but the slave is also indebted.
قيمتان (qīmatān): Two full values of the slave that the master must pay: one to the creditor and one to the victim's guardian.
ولي الجناية (walī al-jināyah): The legal guardian or representative of the victim entitled to claim compensation for the injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
