A) One full value of the slave to the creditor only.
B) Two full values: one to the creditor and one to the victim's guardian.
C) Only the blood money (diya) to the victim's guardian.
D) The value of the slave to the master himself.

Key Takeaway
The master's liability for freeing a slave with pending injury and debt claims is to pay two full values—one to each claimant—because both rights are equal and complete against the slave's value.

Ruling on an Indebted Female Slave Who Gives Birth
Ruling on an indebted female slave's child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of sarāyah to determine when the child of an indebted female slave is sold for her debt and explain why criminal liability does not extend to the child.

Core Idea
Debt as a legal attribute extends from mother to child; criminal liability does not.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq: when an authorized female slave is in debt and gives birth, her child is sold alongside her to satisfy the debt. However, if she commits a crime and then gives birth, the child is not handed over to the victim. Why the difference? The key lies in the nature of debt as a legal attribute (waṣf ḥukmī) that extends to the child by analogy to ownership and slavery, whereas criminal liability is a tangible act that only affects the mother's person. We will also explore the condition that the debt must attach before birth and contrast this with the treatment of the slave’s earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct rulings: (a) an indebted female slave gives birth → she and her child are sold for the debt; (b) she commits a crime and gives birth → the child is not handed over for the crime, only the mother is. Identify the hukm (ruling) for each scenario.
2
Grasp the principle of sarāyah
Debt is a legal attribute (waṣf ḥukmī) attached to the slave’s neck. By analogy, such attributes extend from the origin (mother) to the branch (child), just as ownership, slavery, and freedom extend. This is the proof (dalīl) for including the child in the debt sale.
The child is only sold if born after debt attaches.
3
Contrast with criminal liability
Criminal liability (jīnāya) does not extend to the child because it is an obligation on the master’s dhimmah to hand over the slave, not an attribute attached to the slave’s neck. The handover is a physical act, not a legal attribute. Hence, the child remains free from that claim.
The text uses the terms 'فعلان محسوسان' (tangible acts) for qisās and ḥadd.
4
Analyze the condition of birth timing
The child is subject to the debt only if the debt attaches to the mother before birth. If the debt arises after birth, the creditors have no claim on the child. This is derived from the author’s statement: 'شرط السراية إلى الولد أن تكون الولادة بعد لحوق الدين'.
Compare with earnings: creditors claim earnings regardless of timing because of the slave's valid hand (yad muʿtabara).
5
Understand the objection and response
An objector asks: if the child is sold for the debt, why does the master become liable if he frees the indebted slave? The response: compensation is for destroying the right of collection (istīfāʾ), not for the debt itself. The master pays the value, not the full debt. This distinguishes from the criminal slave case where the master pays full value if he frees after knowledge.
The passage also discusses the substitute (badal) for a part of the slave in a crime scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( مأذونة مديونة ولدت بيعت مع ولدها في الدين وإن جنت فولدت لم يدفع الولد له والفرق أن الدين متعلق برقبتها لأن الدين عليها وهو وصف لها حكمي فسرى إلى الولد لأن الصفات الشرعية الثابتة في الأصل تسري إلى الفروع كالملك والرق والحرية وأما الدفع في الجناية فواجب في ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها وإنما يلاقيها أثر الفعل الحقيقي وهو الدفع وقبل الدفع كانت رقبتها خالية عن حق الجناية فكذلك لا يجري القصاص على الأولاد ولا الحد لأنهما فعلان محسوسان كالدفع ولا يبيعها فيه. فإن قيل : إذا كان الدين عليهما فلماذا يضمن المولى إذا أعتقها والإنسان إذا أتلف المديون لا يضمن شيئاً؟ قلنا : وجوب الضمان باعتبار تفويت ما تعلق به حقهم استيفاء لا باعتبار وجوب الدين على المولى ألا ترى أنه يضمن القيمة لا غير ولو كان باعتبار الوجوب عليه يضمن كل الدين كالعبد الجاني إذا أعتقه المولى بعد العلم بالجناية ولهذا يتبع الغريم بالفاضل العبد المديون بعد العتق ولو كان على المولى لما أتبعه كالعبد الجاني ولا يرد علينا وجوب دفع الأرش معها إذا جنى عليها قبل الدفع وأخذ المولى الأرش لأن الأرش بدل جزئها وهو ولي الجناية متعلق أجزائها، فإذا فات جزء منها وأخلف بدلاً تعلق به حقه كما إذا قتلت هي وأخلفت بدلاً اعتباراً للجزء بالكل بخلاف الولد. وقوله مأذونة ولدت» شرط السراية إلى الولد أن تكون الولادة بعد لحوق الدين لأنها إذا ولدت ثم لحقها الدين لا يتعلق حق الغرماء بالولد بخلاف الاكتساب حيث يتعلق حق الغرماء بما كسبت قبل الدين وبعده لأن لها يداً معتبرة في الكسب حتى لو نازعها فيه أحد كانت هي الخصم فيه، فباعتبار اليد كانت أحق به من سيدها لقضاء دينها بخلاف الولد فإنه إنما يستحق بالسراية وذلك قبل الانقضاء لا بعده كولد المكاتبة وولد أم الولد والمدبرة وكولد الأضحية لأنها حقوق مستقرة في الرقبة حتى صار صاحبها ممنوعاً عن التصرف. وإذا جنى العبد جناية ثم أذن له المولى في التجارة فلحقه دين دفع بجنايته فإن الدائن يتبعه، فإذا بيع لهم رجع أولياء الجناية على المولى بقيمة العبد، وكذلك لو أقر عليه بدين ثم دفعه بجنايته في دينه ورجع أولياء الجناية بقيمته على
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (An authorized female slave who is indebted gives birth, she is sold with her child for the debt, and if she commits a crime and gives birth, the child is not handed over for the crime. The difference is that debt is attached to her neck because debt is upon her and it is a legal attribute for her, so it extends to the child because legal attributes established in the origin extend to the branches like ownership, slavery, and freedom. As for handing over for the crime, it is an obligation upon the master's dhimmah, not upon her dhimmah, and the effect of the actual act, which is handing over, only encounters her. Before handing over, her neck was free from the right of the crime, so likewise qisas does not apply to the children nor the hadd penalty because they are tangible acts like handing over, and he does not sell her for it. If it is said: When the debt is upon both, why does the master guarantee if he frees her, while if a person destroys something that is in debt he does not guarantee anything? We say: The obligation of compensation is because of the loss of that to which their right of collection is attached, not because of the obligation of the debt upon the master. Do you not see that he only guarantees the value, not the entire debt? If it were based on obligation upon him, he would guarantee the entire debt like the slave who commits a crime when the master frees him after knowledge. For this reason, the creditor follows the surplus of the indebted slave after freeing, and if it were upon the master he would not follow him like the criminal slave. And it does not refute us the obligation to pay the compensation with it if she commits a crime before handing over and the master takes the compensation, because the compensation is a substitute for her part and the guardian of the crime is attached to her parts. So if a part of her is lost and a substitute is produced, his right attaches to it, as if she is killed and produces a substitute, considering the part like the whole, unlike the child. And his saying 'an authorized female slave gave birth' is a condition for the extension to the child that the birth be after the debt attaches, because if she gives birth and then debt attaches, the right of the creditors does not attach to the child, unlike earnings where the right of the creditors attaches to what she earns before and after the debt because she has a valid hand in earning such that if someone contests her, she is the litigant. By virtue of the hand, she is more entitled to it than her master to pay her debt, unlike the child, for it is only entitled by extension, and that is before extinction, not after, like the child of a mukatabah, the child of umm walad, the mudabbara, and the child of the udhiyah, because they are rights established in the neck such that their owner is prevented from disposing. And if a slave commits a crime, then the master gives him permission to trade, and he incurs debt, he is handed over for his crime, then the creditor follows him. If they are sold for them, the guardians of the crime have recourse against the master for the value of the slave. Similarly, if he confesses to a debt then hands him over for his crime to his debtor, the guardians of the crime have recourse against the master for his value.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a female slave (أمة) who is authorized to trade (مأذونة) and is indebted (مديونة). If she gives birth, the child is sold together with her to pay off her debt. However, if she commits a crime (جناية) and gives birth thereafter, the child is NOT handed over to the victim; only the mother is. The reason is that debt is a legal attribute (وصف حكمي) that attaches to the slave herself and therefore extends to her child, similar to ownership and slavery. In contrast, criminal liability (حق الجناية) does not attach to the slave's neck as an attribute; rather, it is an obligation on the master's dhimma to hand over the slave. Before the actual handing over, the slave's neck is free from any claim related to the crime. Therefore, the child is not affected. The commentary addresses an objection: if both debt and crime lead to claims on the slave's value, why does the master become liable if he frees the indebted slave? The answer distinguishes between the nature of the claims: debt gives a right to the slave's monetary value as a substitute, while the crime gives a right to the slave's person for retribution. When the master frees, he destroys the possibility of collection, so he compensates with a value. For debt, he compensates the value; for the crime, he compensates the full value as a substitute. The passage also clarifies that the extension of debt to the child only occurs if the child is born after the debt attaches. If the child is born before the debt, the creditors have no claim on the child. This is unlike earnings (كسب), which are always subject to creditors' claims because the slave has a legal capacity to earn (يد معتبرة). Finally, the passage gives examples of similar extensions in other legal contexts (mukatabah, umm walad, etc.) and a related case of a slave who is authorized to trade after committing a crime.

Key Terms
سراية (sarāyah): The extension of a legal attribute (like debt) from the original entity (mother) to the branch (child).
يد معتبرة (yad muʿtabara): A valid legal hand: the slave's capacity to earn and be subject to transactions such that she is considered the proprietor of her earnings for the purpose of paying her debts.
وصف حكمي (waṣf ḥukmī): A legal attribute that is not physical but has legal effects, such as debt being a quality attached to the slave's neck.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is sold for the debt along with the mother.
B) The child is handed over to the victim for the crime.
C) The child is not handed over for the crime and is not sold for the debt either.
D) The child is freed.

Key Takeaway
Debt as a legal attribute extends to the child, but criminal liability does not, because the former is an attribute of the slave’s neck and the latter is a tangible act.

Acknowledgment Bars Claim: Slave Claimed Free Kills Claimant's Relative
Explanation of a ruling with three-aspect analysis and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a guardian's acknowledgment (iqrar) of a slave's freedom removes the master's liability for the slave's crime.

Core Idea
A claimant's own acknowledgment that a slave is free bars him from holding the master liable.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq as explained in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The core scenario involves a slave who kills the relative of a man who had claimed the slave was freed by his master. The ruling turns on the principle of iqrar: one's own admission is binding against oneself. We will walk through the basic ruling, the three scenarios of acknowledgment, and a key analogy that illustrates the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basic Scenario
Identify the parties: a slave owned by a master, a third person (Zayd) who claims the slave was freed, and the slave kills Zayd's relative by mistake. Determine the initial ruling: Zayd has no claim against the master for daf' (surrender) or fida' (ransom).
The ruling hinges on Zayd's own claim – he has acknowledged the slave is free, so he cannot treat him as a slave.
2
Recognize the Effect of Acknowledgment
Zayd's claim that the master freed the slave is an iqrar that the slave is free. In Hanafi law, iqrar is binding against the one who makes it. Therefore, Zayd cannot demand the slave or compensation from the master, as those rights belong only to the victim's guardian when the slave is still a slave.
The master is not bound by Zayd's claim – the slave's status is still legally a slave unless proven otherwise, but Zayd has waived his own rights.
3
Distinguish Between Acknowledgment Before and After the Crime
The commentary differentiates acknowledgment before or after the injury (jinaaya) and before or after the master has surrendered the slave. The ruling changes based on timing and the type of acknowledgment (free by origin, absolutely free, or freed by master).
The three aspects (aujuh) from the original text: acknowledgment of freedom by origin, absolute freedom, or freeing by the master.
4
Analyze the Three Aspects (Aujuh)
1) If Zayd acknowledges the slave is free by origin (hurr al-asl): no liability on anyone. 2) If he acknowledges absolute freedom: same as above. 3) If he acknowledges the master freed him: if before the injury, same result; if after the injury, he may claim ransom or value from the master but the master can deny on oath.
The key difference: acknowledging the master's manumission after the injury may open a claim against the master if the master knew about the crime.
5
Apply the Analogy (Qiyas)
The text compares to a person who buys a slave and then the original master acknowledges freeing him before the sale. The buyer cannot claim from the master. Similarly, here the victim's guardian (who 'owns' the claim against the slave) acknowledges freedom, so the master is off the hook.
This analogy reinforces the principle: the guardian's iqrar destroys his right to daf' and fida'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه قال رحمه الله : (عبد زعم رجل أن سيده حرره وقتل وليه خطأ لا شيء له عليه) معناه إذا كان العبد لرجل فزعم رجل أن مولاه أعتقه فقتل العبد خطأ ولي ذلك الرجل الذي زعم أن مولاه أعتقه وليه فلا شيء له لأنه لما زعم أن مولاه أعتقه فقد أقر أنه لا يستحق على المولى دفع العبد ولا الفداء بالأرش وإنما يستحق الدية عليه وعلى العاقلة لأنه حر فيصدق في حق نفسه فيسقط الدفع والفداء عن المولى ولا يصدق في دعواه الدية عليهم إلا بحجة. وقال في النهاية : وضع المسألة فيما إذا جنى جناية ثم أقر المجني عليه أنه حرره قبل الدفع وجعل في الكتاب الإقرار بالحرية قبل الجناية وهما لا يتفاوتان، وكذا إذا أقر المجني عليه بعد الدفع إليه حر لأنه ملكه بالدفع وقد أقر له بحريته فيعتق عليه بإقراره، وصار نظير من اشترى عبداً ثم أقر بتحريره مولاه قبل الدفع وفي الأصل جعل المسألة على ثلاث أوجه : أما إن أقر ولي الجناية أن العبد حر الأصل أو أقر أنه حر أو أقر أن مولاه أعتقه، فإن أقر أنه حر الأصل فلا ضمان لولي الجناية لا على العبد ولا على المولى، وكذلك الجواب إذا أقر أنه أو أقر أن حر مولاه أعتقه، فأما إذا أقر أنه أعتقه فإن أقر به قبل الجناية فالجواب كالجواب فيما إذا أقر أنه الأصل، وإن أقر أنه أعتقه بعد الجناية فقد أقر ببراءة العبد وادعى على المولى الفداء إن أنه أعتقه وهو عالم بالجناية، إن ادعى أنه لم يكن عالماً ادعى على المولى ضمان القيمة وأنكر المولى ما ادعى عليه من ضمان الفداء أو القيمة فيكون القول قول المولى مع يمينه وعلى ولي الجناية إقامة البيئة، وفي المسألتين الأوليين لا يدع على المولى ضماناً فلا يكون بين ولي الجناية وبين المولى خصومة ويكون العبد على حاله هذا إذا كان الإقرار من ولي الجناية قبل الدفع، فأما إذا كان الإقرار من ولي الجناية وبين المولى خصومة ويكون المولى بعد الدفع إليه أقر أنه الأصل أو أقر أنه حر لم يكن له على المولى سبيل ولا على العبد إلا أن العبد يعتق ولا يكون لأحد على العبد ولاء، وإن أقر أنه كان أعتقه قبل الجناية فإنه يحكم بحرية العبد لأنه أقر بحريته والعبد في ملكه ويكون ولاؤه موقوفاً لأنه لمولى العبد ومولى العبد يبرء من ذلك وأقر بأنه لولي الجناية، فإن زعم أنه أعتق من جهته فيكون ولاؤه موقوفاً.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (A slave: a man claimed that his master freed him, and he killed the claimant's relative by mistake. He has nothing against him.) Its meaning: If the slave belonged to a man, and a man claimed that his master freed him, then the slave killed by mistake the relative of that man who claimed that his master freed him, then there is nothing for him, because when he claimed that his master freed him, he has acknowledged that he does not deserve from the master the surrender of the slave nor the ransom for the blood money; rather, the blood money is due from him and from the 'aqila because he is free; so his statement is believed regarding his own right, so the surrender and ransom become void from the master, and his claim of blood money against them is not believed except with proof. And it is said in al-Nihaya: The scenario is set when the injury occurred and then the victim acknowledged that he freed him before surrender; and in the book he placed the acknowledgment of freedom before the injury, and they are not different. Likewise, if the victim acknowledged after the surrender to him as a free person, because he owns him by surrender and has acknowledged his freedom, so the slave becomes free by his acknowledgment, and it becomes similar to one who bought a slave and then his master acknowledged freeing him before surrender. In the original, he made the scenario on three aspects: either the guardian of the injury acknowledged that the slave is free by origin, or he acknowledged that he is free, or he acknowledged that his master freed him. If he acknowledged that he is free by origin, then there is no liability on the guardian of the injury, neither on the slave nor on the master. Similarly, the answer if he acknowledged that he is free or that his master freed him. As for if he acknowledged that he freed him: if he acknowledged it before the injury, the answer is like the answer when he acknowledged that he is the origin. If he acknowledged that he freed him after the injury, then he has acknowledged the slave's innocence and claimed against the master the ransom if he freed him knowing of the injury. If he claimed that he was not aware, he claimed against the master the guarantee of value, and the master denied what he claimed of guarantee of ransom or value. Then the statement is the statement of the master with his oath, and the guardian of the injury must provide evidence. In the first two scenarios, he does not claim any guarantee against the master, so there is no litigation between the guardian and the master, and the slave remains as he was. This is if the acknowledgment was from the guardian of the injury before surrender. As for if the acknowledgment was from the guardian of the injury and there is litigation with the master, and the master after the surrender acknowledged that he is the origin or acknowledged that he is free, the guardian has no way against the master nor against the slave, except that the slave becomes free, and no one has patronage over the slave. If he acknowledged that he had freed him before the injury, then the freedom of the slave is decreed because he acknowledged his freedom while the slave is in his possession, and his patronage is suspended because it belongs to the master of the slave, and the master of the slave is free from that and acknowledged that he belongs to the guardian of the injury. If he claims that he freed from his side, then his patronage is suspended.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The scenario: A slave belongs to a master. A third person (Zayd) claims that the master freed the slave. Then this slave kills the brother of Zayd by mistake. The ruling: Zayd has no claim against the master for surrendering the slave or paying compensation (fida') because Zayd's own claim that the slave is free means he has acknowledged that the master is not liable. Instead, the blood money (diyah) is due from the slave and his 'aqila (tribal group) like a free person. However, Zayd's claim for blood money against the slave and 'aqila is not accepted without proof. The commentary then discusses three scenarios based on when the victim's guardian (Zayd) acknowledges the slave's freedom: before or after the injury, and before or after the master has already surrendered the slave. The ruling varies depending on the timing and whether Zayd is trying to claim compensation from the master. The author also addresses the situation where the master himself acknowledges the freedom after the surrender. The key principle: acknowledgment (iqrar) is binding against the one who makes it, but not against others. So if Zayd acknowledges the slave is free, he cannot later hold the master liable for the slave's crime. The commentary also references al-Nihaya and discusses the 'three aspects' (aujuh) of acknowledgment. The lesson emphasizes that the guardian's own statement determines his right to seek compensation.

Key Terms
دفع (daf'): Surrender of the slave to the victim or his guardian as compensation for the crime.
فداء (fida'): Payment of ransom (blood money) by the master in lieu of surrendering the slave.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission of a fact, which is legally binding against the one who makes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the victim's guardian (wali al-jinaya) acknowledges that the slave is free by origin, what is the ruling on the master's liability?
A) The master must pay full diyah.
B) The master must surrender the slave or pay fida'.
C) The master has no liability because the guardian's claim implies the slave is free.
D) The master is liable only if the slave was manservant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a claimant's own acknowledgment that a slave is free removes the master's liability for the slave's crime, because iqrar is binding against the one who makes it.

Dispute on Liability for Injuring an Umm Walad: The Two vs. Muhammad
Ruling on a dispute about whether an injury occurred while the victim was a slave or free

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on liability for injuring an umm walad, and apply the qiyas reasoning from the eye case to similar disputes.

Core Idea
When an act is attributed to a state that negates liability, the doer's claim is accepted; otherwise, admission of the act creates liability.

This lesson examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school regarding claims about injuries to a slave. The scenario involves a freed umm walad (a slave who bore her master's child) and her former master's conflicting statements about when the injury occurred. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to liability when the timing of events affects the legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two) and the ruling of Muhammad al-Shaybani on the same case.
The two accept the woman's statement; Muhammad does not.
2
Identify the opposing proof
Analyse Muhammad's reasoning: he attributes the act to a customary state (slavery) that negates liability.
He compares it to intercourse and produce, which also do not incur liability.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the two's rebuttal: acknowledging a clear cause (the act) and then claiming an exonerating circumstance is not accepted.
They use the eye case as an analogy (qiyas).
4
Follow the qiyas reasoning
Work through the asl (eye case), far (hand case), and illa (acknowledgment of act + claim of exonerating state).
Note Muhammad's objection: the state of slavery is a customary negation, unlike eye health.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under the two, the master pays diyah for the hand; under Muhammad, he does not.
Both agree on returning existing items, but differ on the injury itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجنابة عليه قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي وقالت بعد العتق فالقول لها وكذا كل ما أخذ منها إلا الجماع والغلة وهذا عندهما. وقال محمد : لا يضمن إلا شيئاً قائماً بعينه يؤمر برده عليها لأنه منكر وجوب الضمان لإسناد الفعل إلى حالة معهودة منافيه له كما في المسألة الأولى، وكما في الوطء والغلة وفي القائم أقر للضمان حيث اعترف بالأخذ منها ثم ادعى التملك عليها وهي تنكر والقول قول المنكر ولهذا يؤمر بالرد عليهما. ولهما أنه أقر بسبب ظاهر ثم ادعى ما يبرئه فلا يكون القول قوله كما إذا قال لغيره أذهبت عينك اليمنى وعيني اليمنى صحيحة ثم فقئت فقال المقر لا بل أذهبتها وعينك اليمنى مفقوءة كان القول قول المقر له. وهذا إذا لم يسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان لأنه لا يضمن يدها إذا قطعها وهي مديونة بخلاف الوطء والغلة لأن وطء المولى أمته المديونة لا يوجب العقر، وإذا أخذه من غلتها أو إن كانت مديونة لا يوجب الضمان عليه فحصل الإسناد إلى حالة معهودة منافية للضمان في حقها أي في حق الغلة والوطء، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو قال رجل لرجل حربي أسلم أخذت مالك وأنت حربي فقال بل أخذته ما أسلمت . وفي العناية : ومثلها مسألة الحربي وصورتها : مسلم دخل دار الحرب بأمان فأخذ مال حربي ثم أسلم الحربي ثم خرجا إلينا فقال المسلم أخذت منك وأنت حربي وقال الحربي الذي أسلم أخذت مني وأنا مسلم فالقول للحربي على الخلاف المتقدم اهـ. وعلى هذا الاختلاف إذا قال أخذت منك ألف من كسبك وأنت عبدي وقال العبد لا بل أخذته بعد العتق وعلى هذا الخلاف ما إذا أسلم الحربي أو صار ذمياً فقال له رجل مسلم قطعت يدك وأنت حربي وأخذت كذا وكذا وأنت حربي في دار الحرب وقال الحربي لا بل فعلت بعد ما أسلمت أو قال بعد ما صرت إلى دار درهم
The Book of Blood Money, Chapter on the Injury of a Slave and Injury upon Him. (A person said to a woman) 'I cut your hand while you were my umm walad,' and she said after freedom ('I was free at the time'), then the statement is hers. And likewise everything taken from her except intercourse and the produce. This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: He is not liable except for a specific existing thing which he is ordered to return to her. Because he denies the obligation of liability due to attributing the act to a customary state contradictory to liability, as in the first case, and as in intercourse and the produce. And regarding the existing thing, he has acknowledged liability since he admitted taking from her, then claimed ownership over her while she denies, and the statement is that of the denier, so he is ordered to return to her. According to them (the two), he has acknowledged a clear cause then claimed what would exonerate him, so his statement is not accepted, like when someone says to another, 'I removed your right eye while my right eye was healthy,' then the eye became blind; the victim says, 'No, you removed it while my right eye was already blind,' then the statement is the victim's. This is when he did not attribute it to a state contradictory to liability, because he is not liable for her hand if he cut it while she was in debt, unlike intercourse and produce because the master's intercourse with his umm walad who is in debt does not require compensation, and if he takes from her produce while she is in debt, it does not make him liable. So the attribution to a customary state contradictory to liability holds regarding them, i.e., regarding produce and intercourse. And on this difference, if a man said to a harbi who embraced Islam, 'I took your property while you were a harbi,' and he said, 'Rather you took it after I embraced Islam.' In al-'Inaya: Its example is the case of the harbi: a Muslim entered dar al-harb with aman, took the property of a harbi, then the harbi embraced Islam, then both came to us; the Muslim said, 'I took it from you while you were a harbi,' and the harbi who embraced Islam said, 'You took it from me while I was a Muslim.' Then the statement is the harbi's, on the previously mentioned difference. And on this difference, if he said, 'I took a thousand dirhams from your earnings while you were my slave,' and the slave said, 'No, you took it after freedom.' And on this difference, if a harbi embraced Islam or became a dhimmi and a Muslim man said to him, 'I cut your hand while you were a harbi and took such-and-such while you were a harbi in dar al-harb,' and the harbi said, 'No, you did it after I embraced Islam' or 'after I came to dar al-Islam dirham'.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding a claim about an injury to a slave woman. The scenario: A man says to a freedwoman, 'I cut your hand while you were my umm walad' (a concubine who bore his child). She denies this and says it happened after she was freed. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two, 'indahuma), her statement is accepted, and he is not liable for the hand, except for articles still in his possession (qaim 'ala ra'sihi). Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he says the man is only liable for any specific existing item taken from her that she still claims, and he must return it. The reasoning: In the first case (hand cut), the man attributes the act to a state (slavery) that negates liability because a master is not liable for injuries to his own slave; therefore, his statement is not taken as an admission of liability. However, for an existing article (like a dirham), he has admitted taking it, and then claims ownership. Since she denies his ownership, his claim is not accepted, and he must return the article. The two scholars (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that the man has acknowledged a clear cause (the taking) and then claims an exonerating circumstance, so his statement is not accepted; they draw an analogy with a case where a man says, 'I knocked out your eye while my eye was healthy,' and the victim says it was already blind. The passage then extends the same principle to similar cases: a Muslim who took property from a harbi (enemy) while he was still a harbi, but the harbi later converts; the slave's earnings taken by the master; and a Muslim who cuts a harbi's hand in dar al-harb. The common thread: if the act is attributed to a state that would normally negate liability, the doer's statement is accepted to deny liability; otherwise, if the cause is clear, the denial is not accepted. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to later scholars is often the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) in matters of liability, as it protects the rights of the victim.

Key Terms
أمتي (umm walad): A slave concubine who has borne a child for her master, hence has a special status: she becomes free upon her master's death.
غلة (ghalla): The produce or earnings of a slave; the master is entitled to them.
عقر ('aqr): Compensation for vaginal injury or dower in case of intercourse with a female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Her statement is not accepted; the man's statement is taken.
B) Her statement is accepted, and the man is liable for the hand.
C) Her statement is accepted only if she has witnesses.
D) The case is referred to a judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) accepts the freed woman's statement regarding the timing of the injury, making the master liable for the hand and taken items except intercourse and produce.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability for Acts During Slavery
Analyzes claims of when an act occurred and the resulting liability after freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the liability of a former master for acts taken during slavery, and apply the underlying principles to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Attribution to a customary state negating liability voids confession, but once a cause is confessed, later exemption claims need proof.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about liability when an act is attributed to the period of slavery. The core question: when a former master or slave makes a claim about what happened during slavery, whose word is taken and what is the legal consequence? The passage presents two fundamental principles and applies them to various scenarios, revealing a disagreement between the two leading imams. We will dissect this dispute to understand the legal reasoning behind each position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two agreed principles
The passage states two principles that all Hanafi imams agree upon: (1) attributing an act to a customary state that negates liability (like slavery) voids the confession, and (2) one who confesses to a cause of liability then claims an exemption is not heard without proof. Understand these first.
These are the foundations of the dispute.
2
Analyze the first scenario: former slave confesses to killing during slavery
A freed slave says he killed someone while a slave; the victim's heir says it happened after freedom. According to the principles, the former slave's statement is accepted because he attributes the act to slavery, which negates liability. The passage explains why the heir's objection (that the slave is claiming a date to avoid liability) is overruled.
See the analogy with a minor or insane person.
3
Examine the disputed scenario: former master claims he took things during slavery
When a freed slave claims the master took items or cut her hand during slavery, and the master admits the taking but says it was during slavery (thus no liability), the dispute arises. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the master liable for everything except intercourse and produce. Muhammad holds him liable only for things still in existence.
Pay attention to the exceptions and the reasoning for each.
4
Trace the analogical extensions to other cases
The passage extends the dispute to similar cases involving warring infidels (harbi) converting, and earnings from a slave. Identify the common element: attribution to a state that negates liability (slavery, harbi status, indebtedness).
These extensions show the generality of the principles.
5
Note the consensus points
The passage ends with two agreed cases: (1) master says he had intercourse with slave-girl before freeing, she denies; master's word accepted. (2) former slave confesses to cutting a man's hand while a slave; his word accepted. These clarify that the dispute is limited to cases where the confessor is the one who would benefit from the claim of exemption (like avoiding payment of an existing thing).
These consensus cases align with the first agreed principle.

وهنا هو منكر لأصله فصار كمن يقول لعبده أعتقتك قبل أن تخلق أو قبل أن أخلق . قال رحمه الله : ( وإن قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي وقالت بعد العتق فالقول لها وكذا كل ما أخذ منها إلا الجماع والغلة وهذا أمته المديونة لا يوجب العقر، وإذا أخذه من غلتها أو إن كانت مديونة لا يوجب الضمان عليه فحصل الإسناد إلى حالة معهودة منافية للضمان في حقها أي في حق الغلة والوطء، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو قال رجل لرجل حربي أسلم أخذت مالك وأنت حربي فقال بل أخذته ما أسلمت . وفي العناية : ومثلها مسألة الحربي وصورتها : مسلم دخل دار الحرب بأمان فأخذ مال حربي ثم أسلم الحربي ثم خرجا إلينا فقال المسلم أخذت منك وأنت حربي وقال الحربي الذي أسلم أخذت مني وأنا مسلم فالقول للحربي على الخلاف المتقدم اهـ. وعلى هذا الاختلاف إذا قال أخذت منك ألف من كسبك وأنت عبدي وقال العبد لا بل أخذته بعد العتق وعلى هذا الخلاف ما إذا أسلم الحربي أو صار ذمياً فقال له رجل مسلم قطعت يدك وأنت حربي وأخذت كذا وكذا وأنت حربي في دار الحرب وقال الحربي لا بل فعلت بعد ما أسلمت أو قال بعد ما صرت إلى دار الإسلام فعلى قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف القول قول الحربي والمسلم ضامن، وعلى قول محمد وزفر القول قول المسلم ولا ضمان عليه. وإذا أسلم الحربي فقال لرجل مسلم قطعت يدك وأنا حربي في دار الحرب وقال المسلم فعلت ما فعلت وأنت في دار الإسلام، وذكر في كتاب الإقرار من الأصل أنه على هذا الخلاف وأجمعوا على أنه إذا قال الجاريته بعد ما عتقها وطئتك قبل العتق وقالت الجارية لا بعد العتق أن القول قول المولى ولا ضمان عليه وأجمعوا على أن أعتق عبداً له فقال العبد لرجل آخر قطعت يدك وأنا عبد وقال ذلك الرجل لا بل بعد ما أعتقت أن القول قول المقر ولا ضمان عليه
And he said: 'A man who is freed says to a man, "I killed your brother by mistake while I was a slave," and says after freedom, then the statement is the slave's (the freed slave is believed). Its meaning: when the slave is freed, then a man says after freedom, "I killed your brother by mistake while I was a slave," and the man says, "You killed him while you were free," then the statement is the slave's statement because he is denying liability, since he attributed it to a state (slavery) that is customary and negates liability, for the discussion is when his slavery is known. And the obligation for the slave's injury is upon the master by payment or ransom, so it becomes as if a sane adult said, "I divorced my wife while I was a minor" or said, "I divorced my wife while I was insane," and his insanity was known, then his statement is accepted for what we mentioned. And they agreed upon two principles: one is that attribution to a customary state negating liability causes the dropping of what is confessed; the other is that whoever confesses to a cause of liability then claims what absolves him is not heard except with proof. For if it is said: The slave has claimed an earlier date in his confession, and the confessee denies, so the statement should be his (the confessee's), it is answered that consideration of date is for weighting after obligation, as if he said to her, "I cut off your hand due to ownership," and here he denies the very basis, so it becomes like one who says to his slave, "I freed you before you were created" or "before I was created." He said, may Allah have mercy on him: "And if he said to her, 'I cut off your hand while you were my slave-girl,' and she said after freedom, then the statement is hers, and likewise everything taken from her except intercourse and produce, and this is according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). And Muhammad said: He is not liable except for something still in existence that he is ordered to return to her, because he denies the obligation of liability by attributing the act to a customary state negating it, as in the first case, and as in intercourse and produce; but in the case of an existing thing, he confessed to liability when he acknowledged taking from her, then claimed ownership over her while she denies, and the statement of the denier is accepted, so he is ordered to return it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), he confessed to an apparent cause then claimed what absolves him, so his statement is not accepted, as if he said to another, 'I took out your right eye while your right eye was sound, then it was gouged out,' and the confessor says, 'No, I took it out while your right eye was already missing,' then the statement is the confessee's. This is when he does not attribute it to a state negating liability, because he is not liable for her hand if he cuts it while she is indebted, unlike intercourse and produce, because the master's intercourse with his indebted slave-girl does not require compensation, and if he takes from her produce while she is indebted, it does not require liability on him, so the attribution to a customary state negating liability occurs in her case, i.e., in the case of produce and intercourse. And on this difference, if a man says to a warring infidel who embraces Islam, 'I took your property while you were a warring infidel,' and he says, 'No, you took it while I was not a Muslim.' In al-Inayah: And like it is the case of the warring infidel: a Muslim entered the war territory under safe conduct and took the property of a warring infidel, then the warring infidel embraced Islam, then they both came out to us, and the Muslim says, 'I took from you while you were a warring infidel,' and the warring infidel who embraced Islam says, 'You took from me while I was a Muslim,' then the statement is the warring infidel's according to the preceding difference. End. And on this difference, if he says, 'I took a thousand from your earnings while you were my slave,' and the slave says, 'No, you took it after emancipation,' and on this difference is the case when the warring infidel embraces Islam or becomes a dhimmi, and a Muslim man says to him, 'I cut off your hand while you were a warring infidel and took such-and-such while you were a warring infidel in the war territory,' and the warring infidel says, 'No, you did it after I embraced Islam' or 'after I came to the land of Islam,' then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the statement is the warring infidel's and the Muslim is liable; according to Muhammad and Zufar, the statement is the Muslim's and no liability on him. And if the warring infidel embraces Islam and says to a Muslim man, 'I cut off your hand while I was a warring infidel in the war territory,' and the Muslim says, 'You did what you did while you were in the land of Islam,' and it is mentioned in Kitab al-Iqrar from al-Asl that it is on this difference. And they have agreed that if he says to his slave-girl after freeing her, 'I had intercourse with you before freedom,' and the slave-girl says, 'No, after freedom,' then the statement is the master's and no liability on him. And they have agreed that if a man frees his slave, and the slave says to another man, 'I cut off your hand while I was a slave,' and that man says, 'No, after you were freed,' then the statement is the confessor (the slave) and no liability on him.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding claims made by or against a slave after emancipation about when an act occurred. The core issue is whether the former slave's attribution of the act to the period of slavery (which negates liability because the slave's actions are the master's responsibility) absolves him of liability. The passage first presents the ruling: if a former slave confesses to killing or injuring someone and claims it happened during slavery, he is believed because he is denying liability by attributing the act to a state (slavery) that is incompatible with liability. Two principles support this: (1) attributing an act to a customary state that negates liability voids the confession; (2) one who confesses to a cause of liability then claims an exemption is not heard without proof. The text then addresses an objection: why not consider the date claim as merely a defense after the fact? The answer distinguishes between weight after obligation and denial of the basis. The dispute surfaces with the phrase 'وهذا عندهما' (this is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and 'وقال محمد' (Muhammad said). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the former master is liable for everything taken from the slave except intercourse and produce (ghalla), because those are inherently non-liable due to the master's ownership and the slave's indebtedness. Muhammad holds that the former master is liable only for things still in existence (qaim bi-aynihi) because in that case the confession to taking is a confession to a cause of liability, and the subsequent claim of ownership is a claim of exemption that is not accepted without proof. The disagreement extends to analogous cases: a Muslim who took property from a warring infidel (harbi) before his conversion, or a former master who took from his slave's earnings. In all these, the principle is whether the act is attributed to a state that negates liability. The passage ends with two consensus points: (1) if a master says he had intercourse with his slave-girl before freeing her and she denies, the master's statement is accepted; (2) if a former slave confesses to cutting a man's hand and says it happened during slavery, the confessor's statement is accepted. These consensuses show that the disagreement is limited to cases where the confessor is benefiting from the claim (like avoiding payment of an existing thing, or compensation for intercourse/produce).

Key Terms
مقر (muqirr): The person who makes a confession or claim (here, the former slave or the master).
منكر (munkir): The person who denies the claim (here, the opposite party).
عادات معهودة (aadatun ma’hudatun): A customary state that is well-known and legally recognized, such as slavery or minority, which negates liability.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for harm or loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, what is the liability of a former master who admits to taking a specific item from his former slave during slavery, when the item is still in existence?
A) The master is not liable because attribution to slavery negates liability.
B) The master must return that item to the freed slave.
C) The master is liable only if the item is consumed.
D) The master's statement is accepted, so no liability.

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi disputes on liability for slavery acts, the key distinction is whether the confessor is denying the basis of liability or claiming an exemption after admitting a cause.

Liability when a Mahjur Slave Commands a Minor to Kill
Ruling on diyah when a mahjur slave commands a free minor to kill, with analogies and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on diyah when a mahjur slave commands a free minor to kill, distinguish direct perpetration (mubashir) from command (amr), and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the aqila's recourse against the slave after emancipation.

Core Idea
Diyah is on the direct perpetrator's aqila; the commanding slave bears no direct liability even after emancipation.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario from the chapter of diyat: a mahjur slave orders a free minor to kill, and the minor complies. The passage establishes that the minor's aqila pays the diyah, and explores whether the slave commander can be held accountable later. The ruling builds on the principle that direct perpetrators (mubashir) bear primary liability, but command can shift liability under certain conditions. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the aqila can recover from the slave after his emancipation, with the preferred view denying such recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is a mahjur slave commanding a free minor to kill a man, and the minor kills him. The ruling: diyah is on the minor's aqila; the slave commander owes nothing.
Focus on the primary liability: the minor is the mubashir.
2
Locate the Dalil
The passage does not quote a specific Quran or Hadith but relies on the principle that a minor's intentional and accidental acts are both liable on his aqila (based on Hadith that aqila pays for unintentional killing, extended to minors). Also analogies to command cases (e.g., sheep slaughter) are used.
The dalil is primarily from the Sunnah and general principles of liability.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The minor is the mubashir, so his act is attributed to him. The principle 'عمده وخطؤه سواء تجب على عاقلته' means his intentional act is treated like an accident for diyah purposes, so his aqila pays. The commander is not an immediate cause.
The command does not make the commander directly liable; only if the commanded is unaware of the wrongfulness can the commander be indirectly liable (as in the sheep example, but that is about financial loss, not killing).
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the aqila can recover the diyah from the slave commander after his emancipation. Al-Muhit says yes; Sharh al-Ziyadat says no, and the text favors the latter as 'more in line with the principles'.
The ikhtilaf is about recourse, not the initial liability.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Sharh al-Ziyadat's view is preferred because liability for injury (ḍamān jīnāya) is on the master, not the slave; the slave's interdiction (hajr) makes it impossible to obligate the master, but the slave never becomes personally liable. Analogous to the well-digging case: if a slave digs a well and is freed, then someone falls in, the master pays, not the slave.
The tarjih authority is the text itself, and the reasoning is consistent with the slave's lack of full capacity during slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد محجور أمر صبياً حراً بقتل رجل فقتله فديته على عاقلة الصبي لأن الصبي هو المباشر للقتل وعمده وخطؤه سواء تجب على عاقلته ولا شيء على العبد الآمر وكذا الحكم إذا أمره بذلك والأصل صبي، أن الأمر بما لا يملكه الأمر إذا لم يعلم المأمور بفساد الأمر صحيح في حق الآمر والمأمور حتى يثبت للمأمور والرجوع عن الأمر إذا لحقه غرم في ذلك بيان ذلك : أمر رجلاً بأن يذبح هذه الشاة وهي لجاره ولم يعلم المأمور بذلك فإنه يصح الأمر في حقهما حتى إذا ضمن الذابح للجار قيمة الشاة . يرجع بها على الآمر، فإن علم أن الشاة لغيره وهو حر بالغ لا يصح الأمر حتى لا يرجع بما لحقه من مغرم لأنه لم يصر عاملاً للآمر ، وإن كان المأمور صبياً يصح الأمر سواء كان عالماً بفساد الأمر حتى لا يرجع بما لحقه من مغرم أو لا لنقصان عقل ويلحق به المجنون، وأما مسألتنا فالأصل أن الصبي مؤاخذ بضمان الأفعال دون الأقوال فيما يتنوع و فاسد ؛ أما صحة فعله فلصدوره من أهله في محله النوادر : أمر صبياً بقتل دابة صحيح أو بمزق ثوب أو بأكل طعام لغيره فالضمان على الصبي في ماله بذلك على الأمر، ويرجع ولو أمر الصبي بالغاً ففعل لم يضمن الصبي، ولو أمر الحر البالغ بذلك فالضمان على الفاعل. وفي المحيط : لو قال اقتل ابني أو اقطع يده أو اقتل أخي فقتله اقتص من القاتل قياساً وتجب الدية استحساناً ولا رجوع لعاقلة الصبي على الصبي الأمر أبداً ويرجعون على العبد الآمر بعد العتق لأن عدم الاعتبار كان لحق المولى لا بنقصان أهلية العبد وقد زال حق المولى بالإعتاق بخلاف الصبي لأنه قاصر الأهلية. وفي شرح الزيادات : لا ترجع العاقلة على العبد أيضاً أبداً لأن هذا ضمان جناية وهو على المولى لا على العبد وقد تعذر إيجابه على المولى لما كان على العبد الحجر، وهذا أوفق للقواعد ألا ترى أن العبد إذا أقر بعد العتق بالقتل قبله لا يجب عليه شيء لكونه أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان على ما بينا قبل هذا ولهذا لو حفر العبد بئراً فأعتقه مولاه ثم وقع فيها إنسان فهلك لا يجب على العبد شيء وإنما يوجب على المولى فيجب عليه قيمة واحدة، ولو مات فيها ألف نفس فيقسموها بالحصص.
A mahjur (interdicted) slave ordered a free minor to kill a man, and he killed him: his diyah is on the aqila (inheritance group) of the minor because the minor is the direct perpetrator, and his intentional and accidental (acts) are both obligatory on his aqila, and nothing is on the commanding slave. And likewise the ruling if he ordered him to do that, and the origin is the minor: that an order for something the commander does not own, if the commanded does not know its wrongfulness, is valid regarding the commander and the commanded until it is established for the commanded, and the recourse from the order if he incurs loss in that. The explanation of that: He ordered a man to slaughter this sheep, and it belonged to his neighbor, and the commanded did not know that; then the order is valid regarding both, so that if the slaughterer pays the neighbor the value of the sheep, he recovers it from the commander. If he knew that the sheep belonged to another and he is a free adult, the order is not valid, so he does not recover what he incurred because he did not become an agent for the commander. And if the commanded is a minor, the order is valid, whether he knew the order's wrongfulness or not, because of deficiency of intellect, and the insane is attached to him. As for our issue, the origin is that the minor is held liable for the guarantee of actions, not statements, in what is varied and corrupt; as for the validity of his action, because it proceeds from one who is capable of it in its place. In al-Nawadir: He ordered a minor to kill a sound animal, or to tear a garment, or to eat another's food, then the liability is on the minor from his wealth for that, and he recovers from the commander. If the minor ordered an adult and he did it, the minor is not liable. And if a free adult ordered that, the liability is on the doer. In al-Muhit: If he said, 'Kill my son' or 'cut off his hand' or 'kill my brother,' and he killed him, the killer is subject to retribution by analogy, and diyah is obligatory by istihsan, and the aqila of the minor never have recourse against the minor commander; they have recourse against the commanding slave after his emancipation, because the disregard (of his action) was due to the master's right, not due to deficiency of the slave's capacity, and the master's right ceases with emancipation, unlike the minor because he is deficient in capacity. In Sharh al-Ziyadat: The aqila do not have recourse against the slave either ever, because this is a guarantee of injury, which is on the master, not on the slave, and it has become impossible to obligate it on the master because the slave was interdicted, and this is more in line with the principles. Do you not see that if the slave confesses after emancipation to killing before it, nothing is obligatory on him because he attributed it to a state negating liability, as we explained before this. And for this, if a slave digs a well, then his master frees him, then a person falls into it and dies, nothing is obligatory on the slave; rather it is obligatory on the master, who must pay one blood money; but if a thousand souls die in it, they divide it proportionally.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the liability when a mahjur slave (one under interdiction due to being a slave) commands a free minor to kill someone. The ruling is that the diyah (blood money) is on the aqila (the minor's paternal male relatives who pay blood money) because the minor is the direct perpetrator (mubashir). The slave commander is not directly liable. The passage then explains the principle: if a person commands someone to do an act that the commander does not own (e.g., slaughtering another's sheep) and the commanded person is unaware of the wrongfulness, the order is valid in the sense that the commander is liable for any loss incurred by the commanded. However, if the commanded person knows the wrongfulness and is a free adult, the order is invalid. For a minor, the order is always valid regardless of knowledge, due to the minor's mental deficiency. The passage then extends this to the case of a slave commanding a minor: the minor's aqila pays the diyah, and they can recover from the slave after emancipation (according to some views). The passage also references a more authoritative view from Sharh al-Ziyadat that the aqila cannot recover from the slave even after emancipation because the liability is on the master, not the slave, and the master cannot be held due to the slave's interdiction. This aligns with the principle that a slave's actions during slavery, when he is not a free agent, do not create personal liability after emancipation. The analogy is drawn with a slave digging a well before freedom: if someone dies after emancipation, the master pays, not the slave. The passage thus clarifies the nuanced interplay between direct perpetration, command, capacity, and the effect of slavery.

Key Terms
محجور (mahjur): A slave who is under legal interdiction (hajr) because he is owned, so his actions are restricted.
مباشر (mubashir): The direct perpetrator of the killing (the minor who actually kills).
عاقلة (aqila): The group of paternal male relatives (or tribe) who pay diyah for unintentional killings/injuries.
آمر (amir): The person who gives the order (here, the mahjur slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on diyah when a mahjur slave orders a free minor to kill a man?
A) The diyah is on the commanding slave's master
B) The diyah is on the commanding slave (after freedom)
C) The diyah is on the minor's aqila
D) The diyah is on the minor himself from his wealth

Key Takeaway
In a killing by a free minor ordered by a mahjur slave, the minor's aqila pays the diyah, and the commanding slave bears no direct liability, even after emancipation, according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Claim of a Former Harbi After Conversion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whose claim is accepted when a former harbi claims injury before Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar on whose claim is accepted when a former harbi and a Muslim dispute injury timing, and apply the analogous consensus rulings.

Core Idea
When a former harbi and a Muslim dispute injury timing, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf believe the harbi's claim; Muhammad and Zufar believe the Muslim's.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the acceptance of claims when a former enemy combatant (harbi) and a Muslim disagree about whether an injury occurred before or after the harbi's conversion. The Hanafi school splits: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf side with the harbi, while Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar side with the Muslim. Two consensus rulings are cited to support the principle. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply legal maxims like al-qawl qawl in complex factual contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Recognize the factual disagreement: the former harbi claims injury occurred in dar al-harb before his Islam; the Muslim claims it was in dar al-Islam after conversion.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the harbi's word is accepted, making the Muslim liable for diyah. Muhammad and Zufar: the Muslim's word is accepted, and the harbi is not liable.
3
Analyze the basis for each position
Consider the underlying principle: believing the party who denies liability (harbi denies liability because injury before conversion) vs. believing the victim. The consensus cases support the former.
4
Examine the consensus analogies
Two consensus cases: (1) master claims intercourse before freeing slave – master's word accepted; (2) freed slave claims injury while still a slave – freed slave's word accepted. In both, the one who would be free of liability is believed.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The text does not give tarjih, but later Hanafi works (e.g., al-Hidayah, al-Bahr al-Raiq) follow Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Note this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with valid differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد محجور أمر صبياً حراً بقتل رجل فقتله فديته على عاقلة الصبي وكذا إن أمر [؟] الحربي فقال لرجل مسلم قطعت يدك وأنا حربي في دار الحرب وقال المسلم فعلت ما فعلت وأنت في دار الإسلام فعلى قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف القول قول الحربي والمسلم ضامن، وعلى قول محمد وزفر القول قول المسلم ولا ضمان عليه. وأجمعوا على أنه إذا قال الجاريته بعد ما عتقها وطئتك قبل العتق وقالت الجارية لا بعد العتق أن القول قول المولى ولا ضمان عليه وأجمعوا على أن أعتق عبداً له فقال العبد لرجل آخر قطعت يدك وأنا عبد وقال ذلك الرجل لا بل بعد ما أعتقت أن القول قول المقر ولا ضمان عليه
A slave under interdiction ordered a free minor to kill a man, and he killed him, so his blood money is on the agnatic relatives of the minor. Similarly, if [the] harbi [said?], 'I cut off your hand while I was a harbi in dar al-harb,' and the Muslim said, 'You did that while you were in dar al-Islam,' then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the word is the word of the harbi, and the Muslim is liable; according to Muhammad and Zufar, the word is the word of the Muslim, and there is no liability on him. And they have consensus that if a man says to his female slave after freeing her, 'I had intercourse with you before freeing,' and the female slave says, 'No, after freeing,' then the word is the word of the master, and there is no liability on him. And they have consensus that if a man freed his slave, and the slave said to another man, 'I cut off your hand while I was a slave,' and that man said, 'No, after you were freed,' then the word is the word of the one making the claim (the freed slave), and there is no liability on him.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute and two consensus rulings. The issue is a former harbi (enemy combatant) who converts to Islam and claims that he injured a Muslim while he was still a harbi in enemy territory, whereas the injured Muslim claims the injury occurred after the harbi's conversion. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the claim of the harbi is accepted (his word is taken) and the Muslim is liable for blood money because the injury would have occurred when the harbi had no liability. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar hold that the Muslim's claim is accepted, and the former harbi has no liability. The text then cites two consensus rulings: (1) If a master claims intercourse with his female slave before freeing her, and she denies it, the master's word is accepted; (2) If a freed slave claims he injured someone while still a slave, and the victim says it was after freedom, the freed slave's claim is accepted. These consensus cases illustrate the principle that the person who would be free from liability (the former slave/owner) is believed unless the other party has proof.

Key Terms
الحربي (al-harbi): An enemy combatant who is at war with the Islamic state and not under Muslim protection.
القول قول (al-qawl qawl): A legal principle that the claim of a particular party is accepted over the other in the absence of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a former harbi claims he injured a Muslim before his conversion, and the Muslim claims it was after, whose claim is accepted and who is liable?
A) The Muslim's claim is accepted; the former harbi is liable.
B) The former harbi's claim is accepted; the Muslim is liable.
C) Both claims are rejected; no liability.
D) The matter is decided by Qiyas without a settled view.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the former harbi's claim about the timing of an injury is accepted over the Muslim's, consistent with the principle that the one denying liability is believed.

Liability for Murder by a Minor Ordered by a Slave
Hanafi ruling and legal analysis of a mahjoor slave commanding a free minor to commit murder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood money when a slave under interdiction orders a free minor to commit murder, and explain why the minor's aqila bears the diyah.

Core Idea
The direct perpetrator bears liability — the minor's aqila pays, not the commanding slave.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific scenario: a mahjoor slave ordering a free minor to kill. The ruling clarifies that the diyah falls on the minor's aqila, not the slave. The reasoning hinges on the principle of direct causation (mubasharah) and the legal capacity of the minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
Recognize the three parties: the mahjoor slave (commander), the free minor (executor), and the victim. Note the legal status of each.
The slave is under interdiction (mahjoor), meaning he cannot manage his property; the minor is free and has limited capacity.
2
Determine the Ruling
State the explicit ruling: the blood money (diyah) is on the aqila of the minor, and the slave bears no liability.
This is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq.
3
Understand the Rationale
The minor is the direct perpetrator (mubashir). In Hanafi law, the direct actor is primarily liable for harm, whether intentional or mistaken.
The principle 'al-janaya 'ala al-jani' supports this.
4
Apply the General Principle of Commands
The commentary explains that an order to do something unlawful, if the commanded person does not know it is unlawful, is valid for recourse. But the minor's lack of knowledge or capacity does not shift liability from his aqila.
A sane adult who knows the act is unlawful cannot seek recourse, but a minor always can due to deficiency of intellect.
5
Note Exceptions and Comparisons
If the minor ordered an adult, the minor is not liable. Also, in al-Nawadir, if a minor is ordered to kill an animal or destroy property, the minor's guarantee is on his own property, and he can recoup from the commander.
These confirm the principle of direct liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (عبد محجور أمر صبياً حراً بقتل رجل فقتله فديته على عاقلة الصبي) لأن الصبي هو المباشر للقتل وعمده وخطؤه سواء تجب على عاقلته ولا شيء على العبد الآمر وكذا الحكم إذا أمره بذلك والأصل صبي، أن الأمر بما لا يملكه الأمر إذا لم يعلم المأمور بفساد الأمر صحيح في حق الآمر والمأمور حتى يثبت للمأمور والرجوع عن الأمر إذا لحقه غرم في ذلك بيان ذلك : أمر رجلاً بأن يذبح هذه الشاة وهي لجاره ولم يعلم المأمور بذلك فإنه يصح الأمر في حقهما حتى إذا ضمن الذابح للجار قيمة الشاة . يرجع بها على الآمر، فإن علم أن الشاة لغيره وهو حر بالغ لا يصح الأمر حتى لا يرجع بما لحقه من مغرم لأنه لم يصر عاملاً للآمر ، وإن كان المأمور صبياً يصح الأمر سواء كان عالماً بفساد الأمر حتى لا يرجع بما لحقه من مغرم أو لا لنقصان عقل ويلحق به المجنون، وأما مسألتنا فالأصل أن الصبي مؤاخذ بضمان الأفعال دون الأقوال فيما يتنوع و فاسد ؛ أما صحة فعله فلصدوره من أهله في محله النوادر : أمر صبياً بقتل دابة صحيح أو بمزق ثوب أو بأكل طعام لغيره فالضمان على الصبي في ماله بذلك على الأمر، ويرجع ولو أمر الصبي بالغاً ففعل لم يضمن الصبي
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (A slave under interdiction ordered a free minor to kill a man, and he killed him, so his blood money is on the agnatic relatives of the minor) because the minor is the direct perpetrator of the killing, and his intentional and mistaken acts are alike in that the blood money is required from his agnatic relatives, and there is nothing on the commanding slave. The same ruling applies if he ordered him to do that. The principle is that an order to do something that the commander does not own, if the person ordered does not know the wrongfulness of the order, is valid in terms of the rights of the commander and the one ordered, so that the one ordered has recourse against the commander for any liability he incurs. Clarification of that: a man ordered someone to slaughter this sheep, and it belonged to his neighbor, and the one ordered did not know that; the order is valid with respect to both, so if the slaughterer compensates the neighbor for the value of the sheep, he may recoup it from the commander. If he knew that the sheep belonged to someone else and he is a free adult, the order is not valid, so he does not recoup what he incurred, because he did not act as the commander's agent. If the one ordered is a minor, the order is valid whether he knew the wrongfulness or not, due to deficiency of intellect, and the same applies to the insane. As for our issue, the principle is that the minor is held liable for the guarantees of actions, not words, in what is variant and corrupt. As for the validity of his action, it is because it issued from someone who is competent. In al-Nawadir: if one orders a minor to kill an animal, it is valid, or to tear a garment, or to eat another's food, the guarantee is on the minor from his property for that, and he can recoup from the commander. And if the minor ordered an adult and he did it, the minor is not liable.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the text of Kanz al-Daqaiq (the matn) and its commentary in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The ruling is clear: when a slave under interdiction (mahjoor) orders a free minor to kill someone, the blood money (diyah) falls on the aqila (agnatic relatives) of the minor, and the slave bears no liability. The reason given is that the minor is the direct perpetrator (mubashir), and in Hanafi law, both intentional and mistaken killing by a minor are treated the same regarding diyah on the aqila. The commentary then elaborates on the general principle of commands: an order to do something unlawful, if the commanded person does not know the unlawfulness, the order is effective in terms of liability — the commanded person can seek recourse from the commander if he suffers loss. However, if the commanded person is a free, sane adult who knows the act is unlawful, the order is ineffective and no recourse is possible. For a minor (or insane person), the order is always effective because they lack full legal capacity to distinguish right from wrong. Thus, in our case, the minor is liable first, but he can seek recourse from the slave commander. The commentary also cites from al-Nawadir (rare narrations) that if a minor is ordered to kill an animal or destroy property, the minor's guarantee is on his own property, and he can recoup from the commander. Finally, if a minor orders an adult to act, the minor is not liable, because the order of a minor is not legally binding.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubashir): The person who directly performs the act causing harm; the immediate perpetrator.
عاقلة (al-aqila): The agnatic relatives of a person who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) in cases of homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a mahjoor slave orders a free minor to kill a man, who bears the blood money?
A) The slave commander pays immediately.
B) The minor's aqila pay, and the slave is not liable.
C) The minor pays from his own property.
D) The slave's master pays.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: the direct perpetrator (mubashir) bears liability; the commander is not liable if the executor is a minor or unaware.

Istihsan Overrides Qiyas in Ordering to Kill a Close Relative
Hanafi ruling with istihsan overriding qiyas in case of ordering to kill a close relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi doctrine of istihsan to override qiyas in cases where a commander orders the killing of his own son or brother, and identify the consequences for the commander's liability.

Core Idea
When a father orders the killing of his son, qiyas demands qisas, but istihsan imposes diyah instead.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a scenario that illustrates the Hanafi method of istihsan. By qiyas, a killer who acts on the command of the victim's father would be subject to retaliation. However, Hanafi jurists apply istihsan to replace qisas with blood money (diyah), respecting the special relationship between commander and victim. The passage also explores the liability of the commander for the diyah, with two views on whether his aqila can later claim reimbursement after his freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the apparent hukm by qiyas
The act is commanding someone to kill your own son or brother, and the command is carried out. By analogy (qiyas) to direct murder, the killer would be subject to qisas (retaliation).
2
Recognize the istihsan override
Istihsan overrides qiyas: diyah (blood money) is wajib instead of qisas. The close relationship between the commander and the victim mitigates the killer's punishment.
The commander's relationship to the victim is the key consideration for istihsan.
3
Analyze the recourse issue for the aqila
When the commander is a minor, the aqila never have recourse against him. When the commander is a slave, there are two opinions: Al-Muhit allows recourse after emancipation, while Sharh al-Ziyadat denies it ever.
Minor's deficiency is inherent; slave's deficiency is due to master's rights.
4
Evaluate the reasoning for the slave commander
Al-Muhit: The slave's lack of capacity was due to the master's right, which ends with freedom, so recourse is possible. Sharh al-Ziyadat: The liability for the crime is on the master, not the slave; since the master cannot be held due to the slave's interdiction, liability drops entirely.
Sharh al-Ziyadat's view is considered more consistent with principles.
5
Connect to broader principles via analogies
The consistency of Sharh al-Ziyadat is illustrated by examples: a slave's confession after freedom is not binding, and liability for a well dug by a slave falls on the master, not the freed slave.
These analogies reinforce that liability does not transfer to the slave after freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال اقتل ابني أو اقطع يده أو اقتل أخي فقتله اقتص من القاتل قياساً وتجب الدية استحساناً ولا رجوع لعاقلة الصبي على الصبي الأمر أبداً ويرجعون على العبد الآمر بعد العتق لأن عدم الاعتبار كان لحق المولى لا بنقصان أهلية العبد وقد زال حق المولى بالإعتاق بخلاف الصبي لأنه قاصر الأهلية. وفي شرح الزيادات : لا ترجع العاقلة على العبد أيضاً أبداً لأن هذا ضمان جناية وهو على المولى لا على العبد وقد تعذر إيجابه على المولى لما كان على العبد الحجر، وهذا أوفق للقواعد ألا ترى أن العبد إذا أقر بعد العتق بالقتل قبله لا يجب عليه شيء لكونه أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان على ما بينا قبل هذا ولهذا لو حفر العبد بئراً فأعتقه مولاه ثم وقع فيها إنسان فهلك لا يجب على العبد شيء وإنما يوجب على المولى فيجب عليه قيمة واحدة، ولو مات فيها ألف نفس فيقسموها بالحصص.
And in al-Muhit: If he said, 'Kill my son' or 'cut his hand' or 'kill my brother,' and he killed him, qisas is applied in qiyas, but diyah is wajib by istihsan. And the aqila of the minor never have recourse against the minor commander, but they have recourse against the slave commander after his freedom, because the lack of consideration was due to the right of the master, not a deficiency in the slave's capacity, and the master's right is removed by emancipation, unlike the minor because he is deficient in capacity. And in Sharh al-Ziyadat: The aqila do not have recourse against the slave ever, because this is a guarantee of a crime and it is on the master, not the slave, and it is not possible to impose it on the master because the slave is under interdiction. And this is more consistent with the principles. Do you not see that if the slave confesses after freedom to a killing before freedom, nothing is required of him because he attributed it to a state that precludes guarantee, as we explained before. And for this reason, if a slave dug a well, then the master freed him, then a person fell into it and died, nothing is required of the slave, but it is imposed on the master, and he must pay one value; and if one thousand souls died in it, they divide (the value) proportionally.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage from Al-Muhit presents a case where a person orders someone to kill the commander's own son or brother. By qiyas (analogy), the killer is subject to qisas (retaliation) because he directly caused the death. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), diyah (blood money) is wajib instead. The reasoning is that the commander, being the father or brother, has an inherent right over the victim's life in some contexts, so the killer should not be executed. The commentary then discusses recourse: the aqila of the minor commander never have recourse against the minor commander. They can, however, have recourse against a slave commander after his emancipation, because the slave's incapacity was due to the master's rights, not a personal deficiency. Once freed, those rights are gone, and the slave can be charged. But Sharh al-Ziyadat disagrees, stating that the aqila never have recourse against the slave, even after freedom, because the liability for a crime (jinayat) is on the master, not the slave, and since the master cannot be held due to the slave's interdiction, it drops entirely. This view is said to be more consistent with principles, as illustrated by the example of a slave digging a well and then being freed; if someone dies, the slave is not liable; the master is liable for one value even if many die (proportionally).

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of legal reasoning that prefers a less obvious ruling to the one derived from explicit analogy (qiyas) based on considerations of equity, necessity, or custom.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to pay compensation for harm caused; guarantee.
العاقلة (al-aqila): The clan or group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the wrongdoer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a man orders someone to kill his own son, what is the ruling by istihsan?
A) Qisas is applied to the killer.
B) Diyah is wajib, not qisas.
C) The commander is liable for qisas.
D) No liability because the commander is the father.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan can override qiyas to replace qisas with diyah when the victim is a close relative of the commander.
